Negative Propositions Do Not Require Subject Existence
Author argues that negatives don't require subject existence, rebutting a counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the claim that negative propositions can be true even when the subject does not exist, using the dialectical reasoning of Islamic logicians.

Core Idea
Negative propositions are more general than affirmatives: they do not need their subject to exist.

In Islamic logic, a key debate is whether a negative proposition requires its subject to actually exist. The author takes the position that it does not, and replies to a strong objection from al-Shaykh al-Maqtul. This lesson walks through the exchange, clarifying the concepts of 'contract of positing' and 'attributive relation'.

Guided Path
1
State the common view
The author begins: negative propositions do not require subject existence and can be true while the subject is nonexistent. This makes the subject of a negative more general than that of an affirmative.
For example, 'Some jinn are not believers' can be true even if no jinn exist in the external world (though they do in Islamic theology, but the logical point stands).
2
Identify al-Shaykh al-Maqtul's objection
Al-Shaykh al-Maqtul argues that for quantified propositions (like 'Every human is not a bird'), the 'contract of positing' (the specification of the quantifier) requires the existence of individuals. Thus negatives also require existence, though with a weaker requirement than affirmatives.
The contract of positing refers to the mental operation of restricting the subject to a certain quantity (e.g., 'every' or 'some').
3
Analyse the response to the objection
The response is that the contract of positing is a restrictive composition (tarkib taqyidi) that does not imply actual existence—it only specifies the logical form. The quantifier does not by itself require external individuals.
This is a key distinction: logical form does not entail ontological commitment.
4
Examine the further objection and conclusion
A further objection claims that the attributive relation (nisba tawsifiyya) indicates completeness and thus requires existence, making both sides equal. The author leaves the issue open ('there is something in it'), showing the debate is unresolved on this point.
This humility is a feature of rigorous dialectic: not forcing a conclusion when the arguments are finely balanced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا السَّالِبَةُ فَلَا تَسْتَدْعِي وُجُودَ الْمَوْضُوعِ، بَلْ قَدْ يَصْدُقُ بِانْتِفَائِهِ)، هذا معنى ما تسمعهم يقولون: موضوع السالبة أعم من موضوع الموجبة. قال الشيخ المقتول: هذا الحكم صحيح في غير المحصورة، وأما فيها فلأن عَقْدَ الْوَضْعِ يستدعي وجود الأفراد، فيستدعي السالبة وجود المسلوب عنه، إلا أن في الموجبة اقتضاء مكررا ، بخلاف السالبة. أجابوا عنه بأن عقد الوضع تركيب تقييدي لا يقتضي الوجود أصلا، ويرد عليه أن النسبة التوصيفية تنادي على التامة ، وإنكار ذلك مكابرة ، على أن التوصيفية في معنى الإخبارية في استدعاء قيام الصفة بالموصوف ، والفرق بكون النسبة التامة حكاية دون الأولى، وقيام الصفة إن استدعى وجود الموصوف فكلاهما سِيَّانِ ، وفيه ما فيه. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب. (نَعَمْ، تَحَقُّقُ مَفْهُوم السَّالِبَةِ في الذِّهْنِ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بِوُجُودِهِ فِيهِ حَالَ الْحُكْمِ فَقَطْ)؛ ضرورة أن ما لا يتصور بالذات لا يحكم عليه، هذا على رأيه ، وأما على رأي المتأخرين فلا بد من تصوره، ولو بالوجه.
As for the negative, it does not require the existence of the subject; rather, it may be true with its non-existence. This is the meaning of what you hear them say: the subject of the negative is more general than the subject of the affirmative. Al-Shaykh al-Maqtul said: This ruling is correct for non-quantified propositions, but for quantified ones, because the 'contract of positing' requires the existence of individuals, so the negative requires the existence of what is negated from, except that in the affirmative there is a repeated requirement, unlike the negative. They answered him that the contract of positing is a restrictive composition that does not require existence at all. It is objected that the attributive relation indicates completeness, and denying that is obstinacy, and that the attributive is in the sense of the declarative in requiring the ascription of the attribute to the described, and the difference is that the complete relation is a report without the first, and if the ascription of the attribute requires the existence of the described, then both are equal. There is something in it. This, and God knows best. (Yes, the realization of the concept of the negative in the mind does not occur except with its existence in it at the time of judgment only); necessarily because what is not conceived in essence cannot be judged. This is according to his view, and according to the later scholars, it must be conceived, even if by aspect.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange about whether negative propositions require existence of their subjects. The author first states the common view: negative propositions do not require subject existence, and can be true even if the subject does not exist, making the subject of a negative more general than that of an affirmative. Al-Shaykh al-Maqtul (likely Suhrawardi) objects that for quantified (mahsura) propositions, the 'contract of positing' (aqd al-wad')—the specification of the subject as 'every' or 'some'—requires the existence of individuals, so negatives too require existence of the subject, though with a weaker requirement than affirmatives. The response is that the contract of positing is a restrictive composition (tarkib taqyidi) that does not imply existence. The author then notes an objection: the attributive relation (al-nisba al-tawsifiyya) indicates completeness, and thus requires existence, so both sides are similar. The author leaves the issue open ('there is something in it'). Finally, a concession: the concept of the negative exists in the mind only when the concept is present at the time of judgment, but that does not require external existence. Later scholars require conception by any means.

Key Terms
عقد الوضع
النسبة التوصيفية
السالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response to al-Shaykh al-Maqtul, why does the contract of positing not require existence?
A) Because it is a restrictive composition
B) Because it is a declarative relation
C) Because quantified propositions are hypothetical
D) Because the subject is universal

Key Takeaway
In Islamic logic, negative propositions do not require the external existence of their subject; the concept need only be present in the mind at the time of judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the negative proposition: 'Every intoxicant is not permissible' (kuḥl). Does its truth require intoxicants to exist? Using the reasoning from this lesson, explain why or why not.
?
In a debate, your opponent claims that 'Some jinn are not believers' is false because jinn do not exist. Craft a response based on the author's position.

Conception and Negative Judgments
Author concedes that the concept must exist in the mind at time of judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a negative judgment requires only the mental conception of the subject, not its external existence, and distinguish between the views of earlier and later scholars.

Core Idea
To judge a subject, you must first conceive it—even if it does not exist externally.

In the final part of the passage, the author adds a crucial nuance: while negative propositions do not require external subject existence, they do require the concept to exist in the mind at the moment of judgment. This lesson unpacks that idea and contrasts two scholarly approaches.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concession
The author states: 'Yes, the realization of the concept of the negative in the mind does not occur except with its existence in it at the time of judgment only.' This means that to form a negative judgment, you must have the subject's concept present in your mind.
This does not mean the subject must exist outside; only that the idea is mentally present.
2
Identify the necessity of conception
The reason given is that 'what is not conceived in essence cannot be judged'. You cannot affirm or deny something of a subject you have no idea about.
This is a basic principle: judgment presupposes conception.
3
Contrast the views
The author notes that this is according to 'his view' (likely Suhrawardi's or his own earlier stance), and that 'later scholars' require conception even if by a mere aspect (wajh). The difference is that earlier scholars might allow judgment without full conception, while later ones insist on at least a minimal conceptual grasp.
'Aspect' here means the subject is conceived through its relation or attribute, not necessarily its quiddity.

Valid Judgments on Impossible Subjects
Author explains how judgments on impossible subjects are valid through the nature and its accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how propositions with impossible subjects (e.g., 'The partner of God is impossible') are logically valid using the distinction between essential and accidental predication.

Core Idea
Impossible subjects are judged via their universal nature; impossibility is accidental to the nature but essential to its instances.

How can we logically assert a proposition like 'The partner of God is impossible' when the subject 'partner of God' has no existence? This puzzle puzzled logicians, and the solution lies in a subtle distinction between essential and accidental predication. In this lesson, we will walk through the author's resolution, which clarifies that the judgment applies to the universal nature considered with respect to its instances, not to the impossible thing itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Propositions like 'The partner of God is impossible' have a subject that is impossible (non-existent and inconceivable). The question is: how can such a proposition be meaningful or true?
This is a classic issue in Islamic logic (mantiq).
2
Distinguish essential vs. accidental predication
A property can belong to something essentially (bi al-dhat) or accidentally (bi al-'arad). Impossibility belongs essentially to the instances (e.g., the concept of a partner of God in reality), but accidentally to the universal nature (the general idea of 'partner of God').
Essential: directly by virtue of itself. Accidental: through something else.
3
Understand the role of the conceived nature
The subject of the judgment is not the impossible external reality, but the conceived nature (al-tabi'ah al-mutasawwarah) which exists in the mind. This nature is fixed and can be considered with respect to its instances.
Even though the instances do not exist, the nature is conceivable.
4
Apply the distinction to the example
Consider 'The partner of God is impossible'. The universal nature 'partner of God' is considered with respect to its (non-existent) instances. Impossibility is essential to those instances (they cannot exist), so it is accidental to the nature. Thus the judgment is valid.
The author says: 'impossibility is fixed for the nature accidentally, and that is true by the non-existence of the instances.'
5
Recognize the three objections
The passage lists three objections: (1) judging what is not conceived, (2) no actual/possible referent, (3) truth without subject existence. The author begins to address the first by showing that the universal nature is conceived.
The rest of the resolution builds on this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة في تحقيق المحصورات
(فَلَا يُحْكَمُ عَلَيْهِ) ، أي على المحال (إيجابًا بِالِامْتِنَاعِ، أَوْ سَلْبًا بِالْوُجُودِ مَثَلًا ، إِلَّا ) ، أي لكن يحكم عَلَى أَمْرِ كُلِّيَّ ، إِذَا كَانَ مِنَ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ تَصَوُّرُهُ.
وَكُلُّ مَحْكُومٍ عَلَيْهِ بِالتَّحْقِيقِ (1) هِيَ الطَّبِيعَةُ المُتَصَوَّرَةُ، وَكُلُّ مُنصور مُتَصَوَّرٍ ثابت)، فذلك الأمر الكلي ثابت، فَلَا يَصِحُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ هُوَ بِالِامْتِنَاعِ وَمَا يَحْذُو حَذْوَهُ)، من سلب الوجود أو الإمكان. (نَعَمْ، إِذَا لُوحِظُ) الأمر الكلي (بِاعْتِبَارِ جَمِيعِ مَوَارِدِ تَحَقُّقِهِ أَوْ بَعْضِهَا يَصِحُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ بِالِامْتِنَاعِ مَثَلًا) ؛ لكونه ثابتا لموارد تحققه بالذات، فله بالعرض، وذلك كاف في الحكم ، على ما تقدم. (فَالِامْتِنَاعُ ثَابِتُ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ) بالعرض، (وَذَلِكَ صَادِقٌ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوَارِدِ) : لكونه ثابتا لها بالذات، وثبوته لشيء مستلزم لانتفائه عن نفس الأمر، (وَحِينَئِذٍ)، أي حين تقرر ما ذكر (لَا إِشْكَالَ بِالْقَضَايَا الَّتِي مَحْمُولَاتُهَا مُنَافِيَةٌ لِلْوُجُودِ، نَحْو شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُمْتَنِعٌ ، وَاجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْن مُحَالٌ ، وَالْمَجْهُولُ الْمُطْلَقُ يَمْتَنِعُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ ، وَالْمَعْدُومُ الْمُطْلَقُ يُقَابِلُ الْمَوْجُودَ الْمُطلَقَ ) . مع عدم صدق هذا النظير خارج عن المبحث على ما لا يخفى، وتحقيق المقام: أن ههنا ثلاث إشكالات الأول: أن هذه القضايا منعقدة مع أنه حكم فيها على ما ليس بمتصور ؛ لكون الموضوع محالا، والثاني: أنها منعقدة العنوان، لا بالفعل ولا بالإمكان؛ إذ ليس شيء في نفس الأمر متصفا بكونه شريك الباري واجتماع النقيضين الثالث: أن هذه الموجبات صادقة من غير وجود الموضوع . فإن أراد المصنف الاعتذار عن الأول، كما يقتضيه ظاهر سوق عبارته
Admonition on the verification of quantified propositions. So you cannot judge upon it, i.e., the impossible, affirmatively by impossibility, or negatively by existence, for example, unless, i.e., but one judges upon a universal matter, when its conceptualization is among possible things. And every thing judged upon in reality is the conceived nature, and every conceived [nature] is fixed, so that universal matter is fixed; thus judgment upon it in itself by impossibility and what follows it, i.e., negation of existence or possibility, is not valid. Yes, when the universal matter is considered with respect to all its actual instances or some of them, judgment upon it by impossibility, for example, becomes valid; because it is fixed for its instances essentially, so it is fixed for it accidentally, and that suffices for judgment, as previously stated. So impossibility is fixed for the nature accidentally, and that is true by the non-existence of the instances: because it is fixed for them essentially, and its being fixed for something necessitates its negation from the reality of the matter. And then, i.e., when what was mentioned is established, there is no problem with propositions whose predicates are incompatible with existence, such as 'The partner of al-Bari is impossible', 'The conjunction of contradictories is impossible', 'The absolutely unknown cannot be judged upon', and 'The absolute non-existent is the opposite of the absolute existent'. With the lack of truth of this counterpart, it is outside the discussion, as is not hidden. And the verification of the subject: that there are three objections: First, that these propositions are formed even though they judge upon what is not conceptualized, because the subject is impossible; Second, they are formed in terms of content, neither actually nor possibly, because there is nothing in reality described as being the partner of al-Bari and the conjunction of contradictories; Third, these affirmative propositions are true without the existence of the subject. If the author intended to apologize for the first, as the apparent flow of his expression indicates.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and logical analysis of judgments (propositions) about impossible subjects. The author begins by stating the rule: one cannot directly judge an impossible subject—either affirmatively with impossibility or negatively with existence—because the subject itself has no mental concept. However, judgment is possible when the subject is considered as a universal concept that can be conceived (i.e., is possible in conception). Then the passage explains that every judged thing is the conceived nature, and that nature is fixed in the mind. But judgment of impossibility directly on that nature in itself is not valid. However, if the universal is considered with respect to its actual or hypothetical instances (موارد تحققه), then judgment of impossibility becomes valid accidentally. For example, 'The partner of al-Bari is impossible' is a judgment about the universal 'partner of al-Bari' considered with respect to its instances—which are none—so impossibility is attributed to the nature accidentally. This resolves the apparent problem of propositions with impossible subjects. The author then lists three objections to such propositions: (1) They judge what is not conceived, (2) They have no actual or possible referent, (3) They are true without subject existence. The passage begins to address the first objection. The commentary notes that the author's resolution relies on the distinction between essential and accidental predication, and the consideration of instances. This is a classic issue in Islamic logic (mantiq) about the validity of negative propositions with impossible subjects, often illustrated with examples from kalam (theology) like 'The partner of God is impossible'.

المحال
بالذات
بالعرض
نفس الأمر
الطبيعة المتصورة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how can a judgment like 'The partner of God is impossible' be valid despite the subject being impossible?
A) The subject 'partner of God' has actual existence in the mind.
B) The judgment is about the universal nature 'partner of God' considered with respect to its instances, and impossibility is attributed accidentally.
C) The subject is not necessary for the judgment to be true.
D) The judgment is actually a negation of existence.

Key Takeaway
Judgments on impossible subjects are logically valid by attributing impossibility accidentally to the universal nature, while it is essential to the non-existent instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar proposition about an impossible subject from Islamic theology (e.g., 'The son of God is impossible') and explain how the judgment is valid using the distinction between essential and accidental predication.
?
Identify which of the three objections (from the passage) might be raised against the proposition 'The conjunction of contradictories is impossible' and explain how the author's resolution answers it.

Judgment on the Universal Nature
Author states the principle of judging universal natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the object of judgment in propositions with impossible subjects and explain how impossibility is attributed accidentally.

Core Idea
Propositions about impossibles judge the conceived nature, not real things.

When logicians make statements like 'The partner of God is impossible', a problem arises: the subject doesn't exist. How can we judge something non-existent? The author establishes a key principle: judgment is on the universal nature, not on external individuals. This lesson unpacks this principle and shows how impossibility can be truly attributed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize that propositions like 'Sharik al-Bari is impossible' have a subject that cannot exist. The puzzle: how can a true proposition have a non-existent subject?
2
Locate the object of judgment
The author states that judgment is on the universal nature (al-tabīʿa) that is conceptualized in the mind, not on external individuals. Find where he says 'the judged thing is the conceptualized nature'.
Look for the phrase: 'المحكوم عليه بالتحقيق هي الطبيعة المتصورة'.
3
Distinguish essential from accidental attribution
Impossibility is essentially true of the instances (mawārid) but only accidentally true of the nature. The nature is judged impossible because it covers instances that are impossible.
The author says: 'الامتناع ثابت للطبيعة بالعرض'.
4
Apply the principle to a sample proposition
Take 'Sharik al-Bari is impossible'. The nature 'partner of God' is conceived; its instances are impossible. So the proposition is true by accidental attribution to the nature.

تبصرة في تحقيق المحصورات
(فَلَا يُحْكَمُ عَلَيْهِ) ، أي على المحال (إيجابًا بِالِامْتِنَاعِ، أَوْ سَلْبًا بِالْوُجُودِ مَثَلًا ، إِلَّا ) ، أي لكن يحكم عَلَى أَمْرِ كُلِّيَّ ، إِذَا كَانَ مِنَ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ تَصَوُّرُهُ.
وَكُلُّ مَحْكُومٍ عَلَيْهِ بِالتَّحْقِيقِ هِيَ الطَّبِيعَةُ المُتَصَوَّرَةُ، وَكُلُّ مَنْصُورٍ مُتَصَوَّرٍ ثابت)، فذلك الأمر الكلي ثابت، فَلَا يَصِحُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ هُوَ بِالِامْتِنَاعِ وَمَا يَحْذُو حَذْوَهُ)، من سلب الوجود أو الإمكان. (نَعَمْ، إِذَا لُوحِظُ) الأمر الكلي (بِاعْتِبَارِ جَمِيعِ مَوَارِدِ تَحَقُّقِهِ أَوْ بَعْضِهَا يَصِحُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ بِالِامْتِنَاعِ مَثَلًا) ؛ لكونه ثابتا لموارد تحققه بالذات، فله بالعرض، وذلك كاف في الحكم ، على ما تقدم. (فَالِامْتِنَاعُ ثَابِتُ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ) بالعرض، (وَذَلِكَ صَادِقٌ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوَارِدِ) : لكونه ثابتا لها بالذات، وثبوته لشيء مستلزم لانتفائه عن نفس الأمر، (وَحِينَئِذٍ)، أي حين تقرر ما ذكر (لَا إِشْكَالَ بِالْقَضَايَا الَّتِي مَحْمُولَاتُهَا مُنَافِيَةٌ لِلْوُجُودِ، نَحْو شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُمْتَنِعٌ ، وَاجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْن مُحَالٌ ، وَالْمَجْهُولُ الْمُطْلَقُ يَمْتَنِعُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ ، وَالْمَعْدُومُ الْمُطْلَقُ يُقَابِلُ الْمَوْجُودَ الْمُطلَقَ ) .
مع عدم صدق هذا النظير خارج عن المبحث على ما لا يخفى، وتحقيق المقام: أن ههنا ثلاث إشكالات الأول: أن هذه القضايا منعقدة مع أنه حكم فيها على ما ليس بمتصور ؛ لكون الموضوع محالا، والثاني: أنها منعقدة العنوان، لا بالفعل ولا بالإمكان؛ إذ ليس شيء في نفس الأمر متصفا بكونه شريك الباري واجتماع النقيضين الثالث: أن هذه الموجبات صادقة من غير وجود الموضوع .
فإن أراد المصنف الاعتذار عن الأول، كما يقتضيه ظاهر سوق عبارته في المسلم»، فالجواب صافٍ وتقريره أن الحكم كما سبق على الطبيعة، من حيث الانطباق على الأفراد الباطلة وهي حاصلة في الذهن بالذات، ومرآة لملاحظة الأفراد، وإن لم تكن حين الحصول متحدة معها، فتكون متصورة بالذات ؛ لحصول كنهه، وهو كاف للحكم عليه، ولا احتمال لكونه جوابا عن الثاني، كما هو ظاهر، وإن كان مقصوده الإجابة عن الثالث، كما ظنه أكثر الشراح، ويظهر من قوله: وأما الذين إلخ فلا يكاد يتجه؛ لأن الإشكال إنما كان من جهة أن هذه الموجبات صادقة، مع أنه ليس لما ثبت له محمولاتها وجود أصلا، وكون المحكوم عليه الطبيعة لا يفي لدفع ذلك، على أن لا وجود للطبيعة من حيث الاتحاد مع الأفراد أصلا، كما لا يخفى ، هذا، والله أعلم .
(وَأَمَّا الَّذينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الْأَفَرَادِ حَقِيقَةٌ فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ)، وهو شارح «المطالع» (إِنَّهَا) ، أي هذه القضايا (سَوَالِبُ)، ومعنى «شريك الباري أنه ليس موجودا بالضرورة) ، وهذا لو تم لكان جوابا عن الإيراد الثالث، لكن ظاهر عبارته أنه قصر الجواب عن الثاني. ولا يخفى أنه غير واف؛ لأن الموجبة والسالبة سيان في اقتضاء عقد الوضع، كيف ولو لم يشترط فيها ذلك لدخل الأحجار في نحو «لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر»، ولزم أن يكون أفراد موضوع السالبة أكثر من أفراد موضوع الموجبة، فارتفع التناقض بينهما، وفيه ما فيه .
والجواب الحق أن يقال: إن المعتبر في صدق العنوان أن يعتبر العقل
A note on verifying restricted propositions: One does not judge it—i.e., the impossible—affirmatively with impossibility, or negatively with existence, for example, unless one judges a universal matter when its conceptualization is among the possibles. And every judged thing in truth is the conceptualized nature, and every something [unclear] conceptualized is fixed, so that universal matter is fixed. Thus judgment of it in itself is not valid with impossibility and what follows its pattern—like negating existence or possibility. Yes, when the universal matter is considered with regard to all its instances of realization or some of them, judgment of it with impossibility, for example, becomes valid, because it is essentially established for its instances of realization, so it has it accidentally, and that suffices for the judgment, as previously. So impossibility is established for the nature accidentally, and that is true by the non-existence of the instances, because it is essentially established for them, and its establishment for something necessitates its negation from the thing itself. And then, i.e., when what was mentioned is settled, there is no problem with propositions whose predicates are incompatible with existence, such as 'The partner of God is impossible', 'The conjunction of two contradictories is impossible', 'The absolutely unknown cannot be judged', and 'The absolutely non-existent contrasts with the absolutely existent', despite the lack of truth of this type [of proposition] outside the subject matter, as is not hidden. And the verification of the position: there are three objections: first, that these propositions are composed while judgment is made in them of what is not conceptualized, because the subject is impossible; second, that they are composed as to the term, neither actually nor possibly, since there is nothing in reality qualified as being the partner of God or conjunction of two contradictories; third, that these affirmative propositions are true without the existence of the subject. If the author intended to apologize for the first, as is required by the apparent flow of his expression in 'al-Muslim', then the answer is clear, and its explanation is that the judgment, as previously, is on the nature, in respect of its conformity to the invalid individuals, and it is present in the mind essentially, and a mirror for observing the individuals, even if at the time of occurrence it is not united with them, so it becomes conceptualized essentially, because its quiddity is obtained, and that suffices for judging it. And there is no possibility of it being an answer for the second, as is apparent. And if he intended to answer the third, as most commentators thought, and appears from his saying 'As for those etc.,' it is hardly directed, because the objection was only that these affirmative propositions are true while there is no existence for that which their predicates are established for, and that the thing judged is the nature does not suffice to repel that, given that there is no existence for the nature in respect of union with individuals at all, as is not hidden. This, and God knows best. And as for those who said that judgment on individuals is real, among them is one—the commentator of 'al-Mutali''—who said that these propositions are negatives, and the meaning of 'the partner of God' is that it is necessarily non-existent. If this were complete, it would be an answer to the third objection, but the apparent meaning of his expression is that he restricted the answer to the second. It is not hidden that it is insufficient, because the affirmative and negative are equal in requiring the constitution of the position. Indeed, if that were not stipulated in them, stones would enter into something like 'No human is a stone', and it would follow that the individuals of the subject of the negative proposition are more than the individuals of the subject of the affirmative proposition, so the contradiction would be removed between them. And there is something in it. And the correct answer is to say: what is considered in the truth of the term is that the intellect considers...
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical investigation into propositions whose subjects are impossible or non-existent, such as 'The partner of God is impossible'. The author begins by stating the principle that one does not judge an impossible thing per se with impossibility or existence, unless it is a universal concept that is possible to conceive. The judgment is actually on the conceived nature, not on external particulars. When the universal nature is considered with respect to its instances, judgment becomes valid accidentally. Thus, impossibility belongs to the nature accidentally, and that is true even when there are no instances, because the impossibility is essentially for the instances. Then three objections are raised regarding such propositions (like 'Sharik al-Bari is impossible'): first, the subject is not conceivable; second, the term is not actual or possible; third, the affirmative proposition is true without the subject's existence. The author discusses whether the explanation given by the commentator of the Sullam answers the first, second, or third objection. He shows that the answer about the nature covers the first objection but not the second or third properly. Then he mentions the position of the commentator of al-Matale' who says these propositions are actually negative (sawalib), meaning the predicate is non-existence necessarily. This might answer the third objection, but the author points out that negative and affirmative propositions are alike in requiring a subject term; otherwise, logical relations like contradiction would break. Finally, he gives the correct answer: truth of the term depends on intellectual consideration. The passage demonstrates careful logical analysis of problematic propositions and their resolution within the framework of universal natures.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the object of judgment in the proposition 'Sharik al-Bari is impossible'?
A) The real individual 'Sharik al-Bari' if it existed
B) The universal nature 'partner of God' as conceived in the mind
C) The word 'Sharik al-Bari' itself
D) The impossibility of existence

Key Takeaway
Judgment on impossible subjects is valid because it falls on the conceived universal nature, not on extant particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the proposition 'The conjunction of two contradictories is impossible' (اجتماع النقيضين محال). Identify the universal nature and explain how impossibility is attributed accidentally according to the author's principle.

Propositions
advanced
10 min
The Three Objections and Their Resolutions
Author lists three objections and responds, critiquing alternative answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three objections to propositions with impossible subjects and evaluate the adequacy of the responses offered by logicians.

Core Idea
Three objections attack these propositions on grounds of conception, actuality, and existence; the correct answer ties judgment to the universal nature.

Having established the principle, the author now presents three objections that have been raised against propositions with impossible subjects. He then examines whether the earlier principle answers all of them, and critiques alternative responses. This lesson walks through the dialectical exchange and reveals the correct resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify each of the three objections
The author lists three objections: (1) the subject is not even conceivable; (2) the term (subject) is neither actual nor possible; (3) the affirmative proposition is true even though the subject has no existence. Locate these in the text.
Look for: 'ههنا ثلاث إشكالات' followed by their descriptions.
2
Assess the first objection and the author's response
The first objection is that we can't judge something inconceivable. The author's response is that the nature is conceived in the mind, so the subject is conceivable. This answers the first objection.
3
Test the response on the second and third objections
The second objection is about the term not being actual or possible. The third is about truth without subject existence. The author says the nature response does not suffice for these, because even the nature does not have existence in itself.
He says: 'فلا يكاد يتجه' for the third objection.
4
Examine an alternative response: converting to negative
Some logicians (e.g., commentator of al-Matale') treat these propositions as negatives: 'Sharik al-Bari is impossible' means 'No sharik al-Bari exists'. The author points out this conflates affirmative and negative, breaking logical relations.
5
Grasp the correct answer
The author ends by noting that the truth of the term depends on intellectual consideration (العقل). The correct answer is that we consider the term in the mind, not in reality.
The passage ends incomplete, but the direction is clear.

(وَأَمَّا الَّذينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الْأَفَرَادِ حَقِيقَةٌ فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ)، وهو شارح «المطالع» (إِنَّهَا) ، أي هذه القضايا (سَوَالِبُ)، ومعنى «شريك الباري أنه ليس موجودا بالضرورة) ، وهذا لو تم لكان جوابا عن الإيراد الثالث، لكن ظاهر عبارته أنه قصر الجواب عن الثاني. ولا يخفى أنه غير واف؛ لأن الموجبة والسالبة سيان في اقتضاء عقد الوضع، كيف ولو لم يشترط فيها ذلك لدخل الأحجار في نحو «لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر»، ولزم أن يكون أفراد موضوع السالبة أكثر من أفراد موضوع الموجبة، فارتفع التناقض بينهما، وفيه ما فيه .
والجواب الحق أن يقال: إن المعتبر في صدق العنوان أن يعتبر العقل
A note on verifying restricted propositions: One does not judge it—i.e., the impossible—affirmatively with impossibility, or negatively with existence, for example, unless one judges a universal matter when its conceptualization is among the possibles. And every judged thing in truth is the conceptualized nature, and every something [unclear] conceptualized is fixed, so that universal matter is fixed. Thus judgment of it in itself is not valid with impossibility and what follows its pattern—like negating existence or possibility. Yes, when the universal matter is considered with regard to all its instances of realization or some of them, judgment of it with impossibility, for example, becomes valid, because it is essentially established for its instances of realization, so it has it accidentally, and that suffices for the judgment, as previously. So impossibility is established for the nature accidentally, and that is true by the non-existence of the instances, because it is essentially established for them, and its establishment for something necessitates its negation from the thing itself. And then, i.e., when what was mentioned is settled, there is no problem with propositions whose predicates are incompatible with existence, such as 'The partner of God is impossible', 'The conjunction of two contradictories is impossible', 'The absolutely unknown cannot be judged', and 'The absolutely non-existent contrasts with the absolutely existent', despite the lack of truth of this type [of proposition] outside the subject matter, as is not hidden. And the verification of the position: there are three objections: first, that these propositions are composed while judgment is made in them of what is not conceptualized, because the subject is impossible; second, that they are composed as to the term, neither actually nor possibly, since there is nothing in reality qualified as being the partner of God or conjunction of two contradictories; third, that these affirmative propositions are true without the existence of the subject. If the author intended to apologize for the first, as is required by the apparent flow of his expression in 'al-Muslim', then the answer is clear, and its explanation is that the judgment, as previously, is on the nature, in respect of its conformity to the invalid individuals, and it is present in the mind essentially, and a mirror for observing the individuals, even if at the time of occurrence it is not united with them, so it becomes conceptualized essentially, because its quiddity is obtained, and that suffices for judging it. And there is no possibility of it being an answer for the second, as is apparent. And if he intended to answer the third, as most commentators thought, and appears from his saying 'As for those etc.,' it is hardly directed, because the objection was only that these affirmative propositions are true while there is no existence for that which their predicates are established for, and that the thing judged is the nature does not suffice to repel that, given that there is no existence for the nature in respect of union with individuals at all, as is not hidden. This, and God knows best. And as for those who said that judgment on individuals is real, among them is one—the commentator of 'al-Mutali''—who said that these propositions are negatives, and the meaning of 'the partner of God' is that it is necessarily non-existent. If this were complete, it would be an answer to the third objection, but the apparent meaning of his expression is that he restricted the answer to the second. It is not hidden that it is insufficient, because the affirmative and negative are equal in requiring the constitution of the position. Indeed, if that were not stipulated in them, stones would enter into something like 'No human is a stone', and it would follow that the individuals of the subject of the negative proposition are more than the individuals of the subject of the affirmative proposition, so the contradiction would be removed between them. And there is something in it. And the correct answer is to say: what is considered in the truth of the term is that the intellect considers...

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical investigation into propositions whose subjects are impossible or non-existent, such as 'The partner of God is impossible'. The author begins by stating the principle that one does not judge an impossible thing per se with impossibility or existence, unless it is a universal concept that is possible to conceive. The judgment is actually on the conceived nature, not on external particulars. When the universal nature is considered with respect to its instances, judgment becomes valid accidentally. Thus, impossibility belongs to the nature accidentally, and that is true even when there are no instances, because the impossibility is essentially for the instances. Then three objections are raised regarding such propositions (like 'Sharik al-Bari is impossible'): first, the subject is not conceivable; second, the term is not actual or possible; third, the affirmative proposition is true without the subject's existence. The author discusses whether the explanation given by the commentator of the Sullam answers the first, second, or third objection. He shows that the answer about the nature covers the first objection but not the second or third properly. Then he mentions the position of the commentator of al-Matale' who says these propositions are actually negative (sawalib), meaning the predicate is non-existence necessarily. This might answer the third objection, but the author points out that negative and affirmative propositions are alike in requiring a subject term; otherwise, logical relations like contradiction would break. Finally, he gives the correct answer: truth of the term depends on intellectual consideration. The passage demonstrates careful logical analysis of problematic propositions and their resolution within the framework of universal natures.

Checkpoint
According to the author's analysis, which objection does the principle of judging the universal nature adequately answer?
A) The first objection (subject not conceivable)
B) The second objection (term not actual or possible)
C) The third objection (truth without subject existence)
D) All three objections

Key Takeaway
The principle of the universal nature answers the first objection but not the second or third; additional considerations about intellectual conception are needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a munazara setting, propose the proposition 'The impossible is not known'. Another participant objects using the three objections from the text. The first participant responds using the principle of the universal nature. Evaluate whether the response is sufficient for each objection, and propose a refined answer if needed.

The Correct View on Judgments about Impossible Subjects
Author resolves the debate on affirmative propositions about non-existent subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why affirmative propositions about impossible subjects are true without requiring actual existence, and distinguish the correct view from incorrect ones.

Core Idea
Affirmative propositions about impossible subjects are true because the mind assumes the existence of their individuals for judgment.

This passage addresses a key debate in Islamic logic: do affirmative propositions require the actual existence of their subjects? Some argued that propositions about impossible subjects must be negative. The correct view, as argued here, is that the intellect assumes the existence of the subject's individuals for the purpose of truth evaluation. This lesson unpacks that argument and the contract of position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Consider the proposition 'Partner of Allah is non-existent'. The subject is impossible, yet the proposition is true. How can an affirmative proposition about a non-existent subject be true? This creates a dilemma for traditional truth conditions.
2
Understand the contract of position
The 'contract of position' (ʿaqd al-wadʿ) requires that the subject of any proposition (affirmative or negative) must have some form of existence—actual or assumed. Without it, contradictions would break down: e.g., 'No human is a stone' would include stones as subjects.
3
Analyze the correct solution
The intellect assumes the existence of the subject's individuals for the purpose of judgment. The proposition is about the nature (ṭabīʿa) in terms of its applicability to mentally assumed individuals. This is not arbitrary but follows from the nature of conceptual relations.
4
Evaluate competing views
Some claimed such propositions are negative (e.g., 'No partner of Allah is possible'), but this fails because affirmative and negative require equal subject existence. Others said only conception is needed, contradicting intuition. The correct view is the middle path: mental assumption of existence.

In response to the third objection, the answer is clear: the judgment, as previously mentioned, is about the nature (tabi'a) in terms of its applicability to invalid individuals, which are acquired in the mind essentially and serve as a mirror for observing individuals, even if they are not united with them at the time of acquisition. Thus they are conceived essentially, because their essence is attained, and that suffices for judging them. There is no possibility that this is an answer to the second objection, as is apparent, even if the commentator intended it as an answer to the third, as most commentators thought. It appears from his statement: 'As for those who said...' that it is hardly directed properly, because the problem was that these affirmative propositions are true despite the fact that what has its predicates has no existence at all, and the fact that the subject is the nature does not suffice to repel that, given that nature has no existence in terms of unity with individuals at all, as is clear. This, and God knows best. Impossible. (And as for those who said that the judgment is truly about individuals, among them is one who said)—he being the commentator of al-Matali'—(that they are negative propositions), meaning these propositions are negative. The meaning of 'partner of Allah' is that it is necessarily non-existent. If this were complete, it would be an answer to the third objection, but his apparent expression limits the answer to the second. It is obvious that it is insufficient, because affirmative and negative are equal in requiring the contract of the position. Indeed, if it were not required, stones would be included in statements like 'no human is a stone', and the individuals of the negative subject would become more than those of the affirmative subject, thus contradicting each other. There is much in it. The correct answer is to say: what is considered in the truth of the title is that the intellect considers the matter, as a caution in verifying quantified propositions, its truth regarding individuals after assuming their existence. If they are actually impossible in themselves, disregarding actual or possible invention, this is what we have here. There is no necessary qualification of false realities in themselves without mental effort in the assumption of the intellect, and no objection in it. There is no doubt that it is an arbitrary ruling, because whenever a concept is related to a concept, an affirmative proposition is formed, true or false. Then, when the intellect observes the relation of impossibility to the impossible, it judges the formation of the true proposition. Denying the formation absolutely or the true one is contrary to intuition. Also, impossibility is a necessary negation. If it were not established for it, then it would be negated by a simple negation, and necessity is necessarily negated, so it becomes possible—this is a contradiction. As in some commentaries, the truth does not go beyond what the commentator of al-Matali' said, so ponder. Similarly, among them is one who said that they are true affirmative propositions that only require the conception of the subject at the time of judgment, with no difference from negative propositions. It is obvious that this contradicts intuition, because affirmative linking absolutely requires the existence of the subject, and exempting one proposition over another is arbitrary. (Among them is one who said: the judgment is about hypothetical individuals assumed to exist), and the proposition is real (as if he said, for example: what is conceived under the title 'partner of Allah', and its truth is assumed), i.e., the truth of this title (is impossible in itself. And it should not escape you.)
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage discusses the correct answer to the third objection concerning affirmative propositions about impossible subjects (like 'partner of Allah'). The author begins by stating that the answer is clear: judgment is about the nature (tabi'a) in terms of its applicability to individuals that are mentally conceived as a mirror, even if they have no actual existence. This suffices for judgment. The author notes that some commentators mistakenly thought this was an answer to the third objection. Then, the author presents the view of the commentator of al-Matali', who claimed that such propositions are actually negative (salibah), meaning 'no partner of Allah is possible'. The author critiques this view, arguing that affirmative and negative propositions are equal in requiring the existence of the subject (contract of the position). If not, stones would fall under 'no human is a stone', and the subjects of negative propositions would be broader, destroying contradiction. The author then gives the correct answer: what matters for truth is that the intellect considers the truth of the title after assuming the existence of the individuals. If the subject is impossible in itself, the intellect still assumes its existence for the purpose of judgment. This does not require actual qualification with false realities, and there is no objection. The author supports this by noting that every concept related to another forms an affirmative proposition, true or false. The intellect then judges the impossibility as a necessary negation. Denying this is against intuition. The author also mentions two other opinions: one says such propositions are true affirmatives requiring only conception of the subject (like negatives), but this contradicts intuition because affirmative linking requires existence; another says judgment is about hypothetical individuals assumed to exist, and the proposition is real. The author concludes that the truth is what the commentator of al-Matali' said (after refining it).

According to the correct view in this passage, what is required for an affirmative proposition about an impossible subject (e.g., 'partner of Allah is non-existent') to be true?
A) The subject must have actual existence in the external world.
B) The subject must be conceived and its existence assumed in the mind.
C) The proposition must be interpreted as a negative proposition.
D) The proposition must be about hypothetical individuals with no assumption of existence.

Key Takeaway
Affirmative propositions about impossible subjects are true because the mind assumes the existence of the subject's individuals for the purpose of judgment, and denying this is arbitrary and contradicts intuition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the principle of 'aqd al-wad', evaluate the truth of the proposition 'The person who will never exist is obligated to pray'. Determine whether it is a meaningful proposition and explain why.
?
In the debate about rulings for non-existent individuals (ma'dum), construct an argument that rational obligation (taklif) can be addressed to them by assuming their existence in the mind, following the author's method.

Truth of Propositions About Impossible Subjects
Author investigates whether affirmative propositions about impossible subjects can be true.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify the truth of an affirmative proposition about an impossible subject (e.g., 'Shirk is impossible for Allah') by applying the principle of hypothetical existence.

Core Idea
Truth does not require actual existence; it requires only conceivability under an assumption.

In Islamic logic, propositions like 'The partner of Allah is impossible' involve non-existent subjects. Does this make them false? The author argues they can be true because truth is assessed after hypothetically assuming the subject's existence. This lesson unpacks that epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject term and the predicate
In the example 'The partner of the Creator is impossible', the subject is 'partner of the Creator' (non-existent), the predicate is 'impossible'. Determine what is being affirmed.
2
Understand the role of hypothetical existence
The proposition is evaluated not on actual existence but on a thought-experiment: if such a subject existed, would the predicate hold? The author says truth applies after assuming existence.
This is the key move: فرض الوجود (assuming existence) for the purpose of logical evaluation.
3
Apply the principle to an Islamic creedal statement
Consider 'The second god is impossible'. Even though no second god exists, the proposition is true because if a second god were assumed, it would be impossible due to Tawhid.
This is a standard argument in Kalam against shirk.
4
Contrast with propositions that require actual existence
In natural theology, some propositions require real subjects (e.g., 'The universe is created'). Here, the subject must exist. Differentiate between real (haqiqiyya) and hypothetical propositions.

A notice on the investigation of contradictories: their truth applies to individuals after assuming their existence, even if they are impossible in actuality in themselves, disregarding creation in actuality or possibility. This holds in the matter under discussion, and there is no necessary attribution of false realities in themselves, without any operation in the mind's assumption, and no absurdity in it. There is no doubt that it is arbitrary (1); because whenever a concept is related to a concept, an affirmative proposition is formed, whether true or false. Then afterward, when the mind observes the relation of impossibility to the impossible, it judges the formation of the true proposition. Denial of formation absolutely, or of the true proposition, contradicts intuition. Also, impossibility is the necessity of negation; if it were not established for it, it would be negated from it by a simple negation, and necessity is necessarily negated from it, so it becomes possible – this is a contradiction. So it is in some commentaries, and the truth does not exceed what the commentator of al-Mutali' said, so ponder. And among them are those who said: They are positive truths (2) that do not require except conceiving of the subject (3) at the time of judgment in the case of negatives without distinction. And it is not hidden that this contradicts intuition (4); because the affirmative copula absolutely requires the existence of the subject, and to exempt one proposition without another is arbitrary. (ε) And among them are those who said: The judgment is upon hypothetical individuals whose existence is assumed, and the proposition is real. As if he said, for example: What is conceived under the title 'partner of the Creator', and its truth is assumed, i.e., the truth of this title, it is impossible in itself. And it should not escape you that it would necessitate that the subsistence of the attribute be more than the subsistence of the described; for impossibility is realized and established in itself, unlike the individuals, which are merely hypothetical. This objection only applies if the proposition is made categorical; if it is made non-categorical, as is apparent from the words of al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani, then it does not apply. Rather, it is objected that it is contrary to what the mind grasps, so ponder.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and logical investigation into the truth conditions of propositions with impossible subjects. The author begins by stating that the truth of such propositions applies to individuals after assuming their existence, even if those individuals are impossible in actuality. This is not problematic because it does not require actual attribution of false realities. The author then refutes the view that this is arbitrary (tahakkum) by arguing that whenever a concept is related to another, a proposition is formed, whether true or false. When the mind observes the relation of impossibility to an impossible subject, it correctly judges a true proposition. Denying this contradicts intuition. A further argument is given: impossibility is the necessity of negation; if it were not established, the subject would be simply negated, making it possible—a contradiction. The author then discusses alternative views: some say these are true positive propositions requiring only conceptualization of the subject at the time of judgment, but this is rejected because affirmative copula requires existence. Others say the judgment is upon hypothetical individuals whose existence is assumed, treating the proposition as real (haqiqiyya). The author then notes that this leads to a difficulty: the subsistence of the attribute (impossibility) would be more than the subsistence of the described (the hypothetical individuals), since impossibility is real while the individuals are merely hypothetical. However, this objection only applies if the proposition is categorical (batniyya?); if it is non-categorical, as al-Dawani held, it does not apply. But then it goes against what the mind naturally grasps.

Key Takeaway
Affirmative propositions about impossible subjects are true because truth is assessed after assuming the subject's existence, not on actual existence.

?

The Logical Necessity of Impossibility Affirmations
Author provides an argument against the claim that affirming impossibility is arbitrary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that affirming impossibility is arbitrary by using the modal principle that impossibility entails the necessity of negation.

Core Idea
Denying the truth of an impossibility proposition leads to a logical contradiction, so it must be true.

Some argue that calling a non-existent subject impossible is arbitrary. The author counters with a rigorous logical argument: if impossibility were not established, it would be possible, contradicting its definition. This lesson teaches you to follow that argument.

Guided Path
1
State the definition of impossibility
Impossibility (imtina') is defined as the necessity of non-existence (al-salb al-daruri).
This is a standard modal definition in Islamic logic.
2
Formulate the conditional
If impossibility is not established for a subject, then the subject is simply negated (i.e., possibly existent). The author says: 'if it were not established for it, it would be negated from it by a simple negation'.
Simple negation (salb basit) here means the subject is not impossible, hence possible.
3
Apply modal logic and derive contradiction
From the conditional and the definition: if impossibility is not established, the subject would be possible. But impossibility means it is necessarily non-existent, so it cannot be possible. Thus the assumption leads to a contradiction (خلف).
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Conclude that the impossibility proposition is necessarily true
Since denying impossibility leads to contradiction, impossibility must be established. Therefore, the proposition 'X is impossible' is necessarily true.

كذا في بعض الشروح والحق لا يتجاوز عما قال شارح المطالع ، فتدبر .

وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ إِنَّهَا مُوجَبَاتٌ (۲)) صادقة (لَا تَقْتَضِي إِلَّا تَصَوُّرَ الْمَحْكُوم عَلَيْهِ (۳) حَالَ (٣) الْحُكْمِ في السَّوَالِبِ مِنْ غَيْرِ فَرْقٍ، وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يُصَادِمُ الْبَدَاهَةَ (٤)) ؛ لأن الربط الإيجابي مطلقا يستلزم وجود الموضوع، واستثناء قضية دون قضية تحكم.
(ε)
(وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ: إِنَّ الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الْأَفْرَادِ الْفَرْضِيَّةِ الْمُقَدَّرَةِ الْوُجُودِ)، والقضية حقيقية، (كَأَنَّهُ) قَالَ مَثَلاً : مَا يُتَصَوَّرُ بِعِنْوَانِ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي ، وَيُفْرَضُ صِدْقُهُ) ، أي صدق هذا العنوان (عَلَيْهِ مُمْتَنِعٌ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ. وَلَا يَذْهَبُ عَلَيْكَ
أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ يَكُونَ ثُبُوتُ الصَّفَةِ أَزْيَدَ مِنْ تُبُوتِ الْمَوْصُوفِ ; فَإِنَّ الاِمْتِنَاعَ مُتَحَقِّقُ وَثَابِتٌ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، بِخِلَافِ الْأَفْرَادِ) ; فإنها فرضية فحسب. وهذا إنما يرد لو جعل القضية بَنّيَّةً، ولو جعلت غير بتية، كما هو ظاهر كلام المحقق الدواني فلا يرد ، بل يرد عليه أنه خلاف ما ينساق إليه الذهن ، (فَتَدَبَّرْ).
A notice on the investigation of contradictories: their truth applies to individuals after assuming their existence, even if they are impossible in actuality in themselves, disregarding creation in actuality or possibility. This holds in the matter under discussion, and there is no necessary attribution of false realities in themselves, without any operation in the mind's assumption, and no absurdity in it. There is no doubt that it is arbitrary (1); because whenever a concept is related to a concept, an affirmative proposition is formed, whether true or false. Then afterward, when the mind observes the relation of impossibility to the impossible, it judges the formation of the true proposition. Denial of formation absolutely, or of the true proposition, contradicts intuition. Also, impossibility is the necessity of negation; if it were not established for it, it would be negated from it by a simple negation, and necessity is necessarily negated from it, so it becomes possible – this is a contradiction. So it is in some commentaries, and the truth does not exceed what the commentator of al-Mutali' said, so ponder. And among them are those who said: They are positive truths (2) that do not require except conceiving of the subject (3) at the time of judgment in the case of negatives without distinction. And it is not hidden that this contradicts intuition (4); because the affirmative copula absolutely requires the existence of the subject, and to exempt one proposition without another is arbitrary. (ε) And among them are those who said: The judgment is upon hypothetical individuals whose existence is assumed, and the proposition is real. As if he said, for example: What is conceived under the title 'partner of the Creator', and its truth is assumed, i.e., the truth of this title, it is impossible in itself. And it should not escape you that it would necessitate that the subsistence of the attribute be more than the subsistence of the described; for impossibility is realized and established in itself, unlike the individuals, which are merely hypothetical. This objection only applies if the proposition is made categorical; if it is made non-categorical, as is apparent from the words of al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani, then it does not apply. Rather, it is objected that it is contrary to what the mind grasps, so ponder.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and logical investigation into the truth conditions of propositions with impossible subjects. The author begins by stating that the truth of such propositions applies to individuals after assuming their existence, even if those individuals are impossible in actuality. This is not problematic because it does not require actual attribution of false realities. The author then refutes the view that this is arbitrary (tahakkum) by arguing that whenever a concept is related to another, a proposition is formed, whether true or false. When the mind observes the relation of impossibility to an impossible subject, it correctly judges a true proposition. Denying this contradicts intuition. A further argument is given: impossibility is the necessity of negation; if it were not established, the subject would be simply negated, making it possible—a contradiction. The author then discusses alternative views: some say these are true positive propositions requiring only conceptualization of the subject at the time of judgment, but this is rejected because affirmative copula requires existence. Others say the judgment is upon hypothetical individuals whose existence is assumed, treating the proposition as real (haqiqiyya). The author then notes that this leads to a difficulty: the subsistence of the attribute (impossibility) would be more than the subsistence of the described (the hypothetical individuals), since impossibility is real while the individuals are merely hypothetical. However, this objection only applies if the proposition is categorical (batniyya?); if it is non-categorical, as al-Dawani held, it does not apply. But then it goes against what the mind naturally grasps.

ضرورة السلب
خلف

Key Takeaway
Impossibility is the necessity of negation; denying impossibility leads to possibility, which is contradictory, so impossibility affirmations are necessarily true.

The Negative of the Predicate: A Phantom Category
Commentary critiques the invented proposition 'salibat al-mahmul' and its confusion with the 'ma'dula'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify 'salibat al-mahmul' and explain why it reduces to either a simple negative or a 'ma'dula' proposition.

Core Idea
The 'salibat al-mahmul' is a phantom category that collapses into existing proposition types.

The later logicians introduced a new proposition they called 'salibat al-mahmul' (negative of the predicate), claiming it was distinct from the 'ma'dula'. However, as our commentator shows, this invented category cannot stand on its own. In this lesson, you will learn to deconstruct this innovation and see how it either reduces to a standard negative or to a 'ma'dula', and why that matters for the coherence of propositional logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claimed novelty
Later logicians proposed 'salibat al-mahmul' as a proposition where negation is part of the predicate's predication, e.g., 'J is not-B' but with a specific reading different from 'ma'dula'.
They imagined it is distinct from both simple negative and 'ma'dula'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The commentator considers two possibilities: either the negation is external to the predicate (just a normal negative) or it is part of the predicate (forming a composite predicate).
These are exhaustive: negation must be either outside or inside the predicate.
3
Analyze the first interpretation
If the negation is external, then 'salibat al-mahmul' is simply a standard negative proposition, e.g., 'J is not B'. No new category is needed.
This is the simple negative (saliba basita).
4
Analyze the second interpretation
If the negation is part of the predicate, then it becomes identical to the 'ma'dula' proposition, e.g., 'J is not-B'. The only escape would be a forced restriction on 'ma'dula'.
Thus the invention reduces to an existing type.
5
Conclude on the innovation
Since both possibilities lead to known proposition types, the claim of a new category is unfounded. The commentator dismisses it as unnecessary.
This critique shows how logical analysis can prune redundant distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النكتة الرابعة: إن المتأخرين اخترعوا قضية سموها سالبة المحمول، ووهموا أنها مغايرة للمعدولة، وفرقوا بأن في السالبة يتصور الطرفان ويحكم بالسلب، أي سلب المحمول عن الموضوع، فمعناها: ج ليست ب. وفي السالبة المحمول يتصور الطرفان ويحكم بالسلب، أي سلب المحمول عن الموضوع، ويرجع ويحمل ذلك السلب على الموضوع، ومعناها: ج ليست ب أست. وما درى هذا العبد ماذا يريدون إن أرادوا أن السلب خارج عن المحمول، كما يقتضيه كلام شارح المطالع فمفهومها إيجابا غير متعقل، بل هي راجعة إلى السالبة، وإن أرادوا أنه جزء من المحمول، ويحمل مجموع النسبة السلبية والمحمول عليه فقد رجعت إلى المعدولة، اللهم إلا أن يخص المعدولة بما كان السلب غير النسبة السلبية جزء المحمول. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
The fourth point: The later scholars invented a proposition they called 'negative of the predicate', and they imagined it is different from the 'ma'dula' (modified proposition). And they distinguished by saying that in the simple negative, the two terms are conceived and a negation is judged, i.e., negation of the predicate from the subject, so its meaning is 'J is not B'. And in the negative of the predicate, the two terms are conceived and a negation is judged, i.e., negation of the predicate from the subject, and then one returns and predicates that negation on the subject, so its meaning is 'J is not B-ast'. And this servant does not know what they intend. If they intend that the negation is external to the predicate, as suggested by the commentary of al-Mutala', then its concept as an affirmative is unintelligible, rather it reduces to the simple negative. If they intend that it is part of the predicate, and the sum of the negative relation and the predicate is predicated on the subject, then it reduces to the 'ma'dula', unless they specify the 'ma'dula' as that in which the negation is not part of the predicate's relational aspect. This, and God knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a critical note on an innovation by later logicians. They introduced a new proposition called 'salibat al-mahmul' (negative of the predicate) claiming it is distinct from the 'ma'dula' (modified proposition, e.g., 'Zayd is not-seeing'). The commentator refutes this by analyzing two possible interpretations. If the negation is taken as external to the predicate (i.e., just a normal negative), then the proposition is simply a standard negative (e.g., 'J is not B'), and no new category is needed. If the negation is taken as part of the predicate (i.e., forming a composite predicate like 'not-B'), then it becomes identical to the 'ma'dula' (e.g., 'J is not-B'). The only escape would be to restrict 'ma'dula' to cases where the negation is not part of the predicate's relational aspect, but this is a forced distinction. The commentator uses the example 'J is not B' as the standard negative, and 'J is not-B' as the 'ma'dula'. The argument is grounded in the logical analysis of quantified propositions (mahsurat).

Key Terms
سالبة المحمول (salibat al-mahmul): An invented proposition where the negation is considered part of the predicate's predication, e.g., 'J is not-B' but with a specific claim of difference from standard negative and 'ma'dula'. [Note: This term is specific to later post-Avicennan logic; not found in earlier works.]
المعدولة (al-ma'dula): A proposition where the predicate is modified by a negation, e.g., 'Zayd is not-seeing', with the negation considered part of the predicate term. [Note: Stable term in Islamic logic, but its exact relation to 'salibat al-mahmul' is disputed.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the problem with the invented 'salibat al-mahmul' proposition?
A) It is not a valid proposition in any sense
B) It reduces either to a simple negative or to a 'ma'dula'
C) It requires the existence of both terms in the external world
D) It was rejected by all earlier logicians

Key Takeaway
The later logicians' 'salibat al-mahmul' does not introduce a genuinely new propositional type; it collapses into either a simple negative or a 'ma'dula'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyse the following proposition: 'الماء ليس ببارد' (Water is not cold). Determine whether it should be classified as a simple negative, a 'ma'dula', or a 'salibat al-mahmul', and justify your answer using the commentator's reasoning.

The Negative of the Predicate: Definition and Collapse
Defines and criticizes the salibat al-mahmul proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 'salibat al-mahmul' proposition and explain why it reduces to either a simple negative or a modified proposition.

Core Idea
Salibat al-mahmul is not a third type; it collapses into the negative or modified.

Later logicians introduced a new proposition type called the 'negative of the predicate' (salibat al-mahmul), claiming it differs from both the simple negative and the modified proposition. In this lesson, we will examine their definition and see why the author of this passage finds it untenable. By the end, you should be able to recognize this concept and articulate its logical collapse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two proposed interpretations
The later logicians described 'salibat al-mahmul' as a proposition where the negation is attached to the predicate. But they were ambiguous: is the negation outside the predicate or part of it? The author presents both possibilities.
2
Test the first interpretation: negation outside the predicate
If the negation is outside the predicate, then the proposition 'J is not-B' would be interpreted as 'J is not B', which is exactly a simple negative proposition. Thus it is not distinct.
3
Test the second interpretation: negation as part of the predicate
If the negation is part of the predicate, then 'J is not-B' means 'J is non-B', which is a modified proposition (ma'dula). So again it is not new.
4
Conclude the collapse
Since both interpretations lead to already recognized types, 'salibat al-mahmul' is not a genuine third type. The author dismisses it as a needless innovation.
This critique is an example of how later innovations in logic must be tested against established definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النكتة الرابعة : إن المتأخرين اخترعوا قضية سموها سالبة المحمول، ووهموا أنها مغايرة للمعدولة ، وفرقوا بين موجبتها والسالبة بأن في السالبة يتصور الطرفان ويحكم بالسلب، أي سلب المحمول عن الموضوع، فمعناها: ج ليست ب، وفي السالبة المحمول يتصور الطرفان ويحكم بالسلب ، أي سلب المحمول عن الموضوع، ويرجع ويحمل ذلك السلب على الموضوع ، ومعناها: ج ليست ب. وما درى هذا العبد ماذا يريدون إن أرادوا أن السلب خارج عن المحمول، كما يقتضيه كلام شارح المطالع فمفهومها إيجابا غير متعقل، بل هي راجعة إلى السالبة، وإن أرادوا أنه جزء من المحمول، ويحمل مجموع النسبة السلبية والمحمول عليه فقد رجعت إلى المعدولة، اللهم إلا أن يخص المعدولة بما كان السلب غير النسبة السلبية جزء المحمول. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب. وحكموا بأن صدق الإيجاب فيها لا يستدعي الوجود، أي وجود الموضوع، كالسلب لا يستدعيه، بل السلب، أي سلب السالبة المحمول يستدعيه كالإيجاب المحصل، وفريحتك حاكمة بأن الربط الإيجابي مطلقا يقتضي الوجود للموضوع، فالفرق بينها وبين الموجبات الأخر تحكم. قال الشيخ: إنما أوجبنا أن يكون الموضوع في القضايا الإيجابية المعدولة موجودا، لا لأن غير عادل يقتضي ذلك، لكن لأن الإيجاب ذلك يقتضي في أن يصدق، سواء كان نفس غير عادل يقع على الموجود والمعدوم، أو لا يقع إلا على الموجود، ومن ثم، أي من أجل أن مطلق الإيجاب يستدعي الوجود لم يذهب المحققون إلى عدم استدعاء تلك الموجبة الوجود، بل قيل في الحواشي القديمة: الحق إنها قضية ذهنية، الظاهر أنه أراد بها الحقيقية، وجميع المفاهيم التصورية موجودة في نفس الأمر تحقيقا أو تقديرا؛ لأن كل مفهوم يحكم عليها بأحكام إيجابية، وأقلها أنها مغايرة لما عداها، أو معلوم الباري، فلا بد له نحو من الوجود في نفس الأمر. وأيضا يمكن الاستدلال بأن كل مفهوم يتصور بكنهه للمفارقات، فلا بد
The fourth point: Indeed the later scholars invented a proposition which they called the negative of the predicate, and they imagined that it is different from the modified. They distinguished between its affirmative and its negative by [saying] that in the negative the two terms are conceived and a judgment of negation is made, i.e., negation of the predicate from the subject, so its meaning is: 'J is not B'. And in the negative of the predicate the two terms are conceived and a judgment of negation is made, i.e., negation of the predicate from the subject, and then that negation is returned and predicated of the subject, so its meaning is: 'J is not-B'. And this servant does not know what they want: if they intend that the negation is outside the predicate, as the commentary of al-Mutali' implies, then its concept as affirmative is unintelligible; rather it returns to the negative. If they intend that it is part of the predicate, and the aggregate of the negative relation and the predicate is predicated of the subject, then it returns to the modified, unless one restricts the modified to that in which the negation is not a part of the predicate? And God knows best what is correct. And they ruled that the truth of the affirmative in it does not require existence, i.e., existence of the subject, like the negative does not require it; rather the negative, i.e., the negation of the negative of the predicate, requires it like the straightforward affirmative. And your natural disposition judges that the positive copula absolutely necessitates existence for the subject; so the difference between it and other affirmatives is ruled. The Shaykh said: We only required that the subject in affirmative modified propositions be existent, not because 'unjust' necessitates that, but because affirmation itself necessitates that in order for it to be true, whether 'unjust' applies to existent and non-existent or applies only to existent. Hence, because absolute affirmation requires existence, the verifiers did not hold that that affirmative does not require existence; rather it was said in the old glosses: 'The truth is that it is a mental proposition' – apparently intending the real proposition. And all conceptual notions are existent in re in actuality or by estimation, because every concept is judged with affirmative judgments, the least of which is that it is different from others or known to the Eternal, so it must have some mode of existence in re. And also one can argue that every concept is conceived in its essence by the separated intellects, so it must...
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage defines and critiques the proposition type called 'salibat al-mahmul' (negative of the predicate) invented by later logicians. The author first states their claim that it is distinct from the modified proposition (al-ma'dula). He explains their distinction: in a simple negative, the judgment is that the subject does not have the predicate (J laysa B), while in the negative of the predicate, the negation itself is made part of the predicate and then attributed to the subject (J laysa bi-B, meaning J is not-B). The author expresses confusion over the intended meaning: if the negation is external to the predicate, then the proposition is no different from a simple negative; if the negation is part of the predicate, then it reduces to a modified proposition (ma'dula). He then reports their ruling that the truth of the affirmative version (the affirmative of the negative of the predicate) does not require existence of the subject, unlike simple negatives. But the author argues that the positive copula universally requires existence of the subject, so this distinction is arbitrary. He cites the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) who states that the requirement of existence in affirmative modified propositions is due to affirmation itself, not the predicate 'unjust'. Then he mentions that the verifiers said it is a 'mental proposition' (qadiyya dhihniyya), likely meaning a real proposition (haqiqiyya) whose subject is conceptual. Finally, the author argues that all conceptual notions have some mode of existence in re (actual or estimative), because they can be subjects of affirmative judgments, and he adds an argument from the fact that they are conceived by separate intellects.

Key Terms
سالبة المحمول
معدولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Modified proposition (ma'dula)
C) It remains distinct
D) It becomes unintelligible

Key Takeaway
The salibat al-mahmul proposition collapses into either the simple negative or the modified proposition; it is not a genuine third type.

The Existence Requirement for Affirmative Propositions
Discusses the existence requirement for affirmative propositions in the context of salibat al-mahmul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author insists that affirmative propositions, including the affirmative version of salibat al-mahmul, require the existence of the subject.

Core Idea
Affirmation always requires existence of the subject, regardless of the predicate form.

The later logicians claimed that the affirmative version of 'salibat al-mahmul' does not require the existence of the subject, unlike simple affirmatives. In this lesson, we will examine the author's response and the broader principle that affirmation itself necessitates subject existence. We will also explore the solution that such propositions are 'mental' or 'real' propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
They ruled that the truth of the affirmative in salibat al-mahmul does not require existence of the subject, similar to the simple negative. But they said the negation of salibat al-mahmul does require existence like straightforward affirmatives.
2
Understand the author's objection
The author argues that the positive copula (الربط الإيجابي) in any proposition necessitates existence of the subject. So there is no basis for distinguishing between affirmatives in this regard.
3
Examine the Shaykh's (Avicenna's) support
Avicenna is cited: affirmation itself requires existence, not the specific predicate. For example, 'Zayd is unjust' requires Zayd's existence even if 'unjust' could apply to non-existents.
4
Consider the mental proposition solution
Some old glosses said it is a 'mental proposition' (qadiyya dhihniyya), likely meaning a real proposition (haqiqiyya) whose subject is a concept. Since all concepts have some mode of existence in re (actual or estimative), the existence requirement is satisfied.
This shows the later logicians' attempt to avoid the requirement is unnecessary.
