النكتة الرابعة : إن المتأخرين اخترعوا قضية سموها سالبة المحمول، ووهموا أنها مغايرة للمعدولة ، وفرقوا بين موجبتها والسالبة بأن في السالبة يتصور الطرفان ويحكم بالسلب، أي سلب المحمول عن الموضوع، فمعناها: ج ليست ب، وفي السالبة المحمول يتصور الطرفان ويحكم بالسلب ، أي سلب المحمول عن الموضوع، ويرجع ويحمل ذلك السلب على الموضوع ، ومعناها: ج ليست ب. وما درى هذا العبد ماذا يريدون إن أرادوا أن السلب خارج عن المحمول، كما يقتضيه كلام شارح المطالع فمفهومها إيجابا غير متعقل، بل هي راجعة إلى السالبة، وإن أرادوا أنه جزء من المحمول، ويحمل مجموع النسبة السلبية والمحمول عليه فقد رجعت إلى المعدولة، اللهم إلا أن يخص المعدولة بما كان السلب غير النسبة السلبية جزء المحمول. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب. وحكموا بأن صدق الإيجاب فيها لا يستدعي الوجود، أي وجود الموضوع، كالسلب لا يستدعيه، بل السلب، أي سلب السالبة المحمول يستدعيه كالإيجاب المحصل، وفريحتك حاكمة بأن الربط الإيجابي مطلقا يقتضي الوجود للموضوع، فالفرق بينها وبين الموجبات الأخر تحكم. قال الشيخ: إنما أوجبنا أن يكون الموضوع في القضايا الإيجابية المعدولة موجودا، لا لأن غير عادل يقتضي ذلك، لكن لأن الإيجاب ذلك يقتضي في أن يصدق، سواء كان نفس غير عادل يقع على الموجود والمعدوم، أو لا يقع إلا على الموجود، ومن ثم، أي من أجل أن مطلق الإيجاب يستدعي الوجود لم يذهب المحققون إلى عدم استدعاء تلك الموجبة الوجود، بل قيل في الحواشي القديمة: الحق إنها قضية ذهنية، الظاهر أنه أراد بها الحقيقية، وجميع المفاهيم التصورية موجودة في نفس الأمر تحقيقا أو تقديرا؛ لأن كل مفهوم يحكم عليها بأحكام إيجابية، وأقلها أنها مغايرة لما عداها، أو معلوم الباري، فلا بد له نحو من الوجود في نفس الأمر. وأيضا يمكن الاستدلال بأن كل مفهوم يتصور بكنهه للمفارقات، فلا بد
The fourth point: Indeed the later scholars invented a proposition which they called the negative of the predicate, and they imagined that it is different from the modified. They distinguished between its affirmative and its negative by [saying] that in the negative the two terms are conceived and a judgment of negation is made, i.e., negation of the predicate from the subject, so its meaning is: 'J is not B'. And in the negative of the predicate the two terms are conceived and a judgment of negation is made, i.e., negation of the predicate from the subject, and then that negation is returned and predicated of the subject, so its meaning is: 'J is not-B'. And this servant does not know what they want: if they intend that the negation is outside the predicate, as the commentary of al-Mutali' implies, then its concept as affirmative is unintelligible; rather it returns to the negative. If they intend that it is part of the predicate, and the aggregate of the negative relation and the predicate is predicated of the subject, then it returns to the modified, unless one restricts the modified to that in which the negation is not a part of the predicate? And God knows best what is correct. And they ruled that the truth of the affirmative in it does not require existence, i.e., existence of the subject, like the negative does not require it; rather the negative, i.e., the negation of the negative of the predicate, requires it like the straightforward affirmative. And your natural disposition judges that the positive copula absolutely necessitates existence for the subject; so the difference between it and other affirmatives is ruled. The Shaykh said: We only required that the subject in affirmative modified propositions be existent, not because 'unjust' necessitates that, but because affirmation itself necessitates that in order for it to be true, whether 'unjust' applies to existent and non-existent or applies only to existent. Hence, because absolute affirmation requires existence, the verifiers did not hold that that affirmative does not require existence; rather it was said in the old glosses: 'The truth is that it is a mental proposition' – apparently intending the real proposition. And all conceptual notions are existent in re in actuality or by estimation, because every concept is judged with affirmative judgments, the least of which is that it is different from others or known to the Eternal, so it must have some mode of existence in re. And also one can argue that every concept is conceived in its essence by the separated intellects, so it must...
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage defines and critiques the proposition type called 'salibat al-mahmul' (negative of the predicate) invented by later logicians. The author first states their claim that it is distinct from the modified proposition (al-ma'dula). He explains their distinction: in a simple negative, the judgment is that the subject does not have the predicate (J laysa B), while in the negative of the predicate, the negation itself is made part of the predicate and then attributed to the subject (J laysa bi-B, meaning J is not-B). The author expresses confusion over the intended meaning: if the negation is external to the predicate, then the proposition is no different from a simple negative; if the negation is part of the predicate, then it reduces to a modified proposition (ma'dula). He then reports their ruling that the truth of the affirmative version (the affirmative of the negative of the predicate) does not require existence of the subject, unlike simple negatives. But the author argues that the positive copula universally requires existence of the subject, so this distinction is arbitrary. He cites the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) who states that the requirement of existence in affirmative modified propositions is due to affirmation itself, not the predicate 'unjust'. Then he mentions that the verifiers said it is a 'mental proposition' (qadiyya dhihniyya), likely meaning a real proposition (haqiqiyya) whose subject is conceptual. Finally, the author argues that all conceptual notions have some mode of existence in re (actual or estimative), because they can be subjects of affirmative judgments, and he adds an argument from the fact that they are conceived by separate intellects.

Key Terms
سالبة المحمول
معدولة
قضية ذهنية
قضية حقيقية
وجود في نفس الأمر

Existential Import and the Affirmative-Negative Entailment
Author argues for existential import of affirmatives and refines entailment with negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why affirmative propositions require subject existence and evaluate the mutual entailment between affirmatives and negatives in terms of truth conditions.

Core Idea
Affirmation itself, not the predicate, demands subject existence for truth.

This lesson tackles a foundational issue in Islamic logic: do affirmative propositions carry an existential commitment? The passage from Bahr al-Ulum refines the classical view, arguing that the positive copula entails existence, and this leads to a mutual entailment between affirmatives and negatives. However, the author critically examines objections involving impossible subjects and external negatives, showing that the entailment holds only for 'true' (haqiqiyya) propositions under a condition of possibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence where the author states that the positive copula (al-rabṭ al-ījābī) necessarily requires existence for the subject. This is the foundational principle of the lesson.
The claim is at the beginning: 'الربط الإيجابي مطلقا يقتضي الوجود للموضوع'.
2
Differentiate the reason for existence
Note that the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) distinguishes that it is not the predicate (e.g., 'non-just') but the affirmation itself that requires existence. This refines a common misunderstanding.
The passage says: 'إنما أوجبنا... لا لأن غير عادل يقتضي ذلك، لكن لأن الإيجاب ذلك يقتضي'.
3
Interpret the 'mental proposition'
The Old Glosses describe such a proposition as a 'mental proposition' (qadiyya dhihniyya). The author interprets this as synonymous with a 'true proposition' (haqiqiyya), meaning the subject is a concept with actual or virtual existence.
This connects to the broader ontological framework of nafs al-amr.
4
Analyze the mutual entailment claim
The author asserts that between the affirmative and negative there is a mutual entailment (talāzum) in terms of truth: one is true iff the other is false. Understand this as a form of immediate inference (contradiction).
The text says: 'بينها وبين السالبة تلازم بحسب الصدق'.
5
Evaluate the objections and refinement
Consider the objections: (1) the reasoning applies to modified propositions, (2) the entailment might require external negative to match true affirmative, and (3) impossible subjects (like phoenix) challenge the truth of the affirmative. The author responds that the entailment holds for true propositions (haqiqiyya) only if the subject's individuals are possible.
This critical refinement ensures the doctrine is consistent despite counterexamples.

شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم. الموضوع، (كَالسَّلْبِ لَا يَسْتَدْعِيهِ ) ، بَلْ السَّلْبُ)، أي سلب السالبة المحمول (يَسْتَدْعِيهِ كَالْإِيجَابِ الْمُحَصَّل] ، وَفَرِيحَتُكَ حَاكِمَةٌ بِأَنَّ الرَّبْطَ الْإِيجَابِيَّ مُطْلَقًا يَقْتَضِي الْوُجُودَ للموضوع، فالفرق بينها وبين الموجبات الأخر تحكم. قال الشيخ: إنما أوجبنا أن يكون الموضوع في القضايا الإيجابية المعدولة موجودا، لا لأن «غير عادل» يقتضي ذلك، لكن لأن الإيجاب ذلك يقتضي في أن يصدق، سواء كان نفس غير عادل» يقع على الموجود والمعدوم، أو لا يقع إلا على الموجود ، (وَمِنْ ثَمَّ)، أي من أجل أن مطلق الإيجاب يستدعي الوجود لم يذهب المحققون إلى عدم استدعاء تلك الموجبة الوجود، بل (قيل) في «الحواشي القديمة»: ([الْحَقُّ ] إِنَّهَا قَضِيَّةٌ ذِهْنِيَّةٌ) ، الظاهر أنه أراد بها الحقيقية، (وَجَمِيعُ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ مَوْجُودَةٌ في نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ تَحْقِيقَا أَوْ تَقْدِيرًا)؛ لأن كل مفهوم يحكم عليها بأحكام إيجابية، وأقلها أنها مغايرة لما عداها ، أو معلوم الباري، فلا بد له نحو من الوجود في نفس الأمر. وأيضا يمكن الاستدلال بأن كل مفهوم يتصور بكنهه للمفارقات، فلا بد من وجودها في أذهانها، وكل موجود في الذهن موجود في نفس الأمر، (قَبَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ السَّالِبَةِ تَلَازُمُ بِحَسَبِ الصِّدْقِ) ؛ لأن صدق السالبة بدونها إنما كان في مادة موضوعها معدوم، وقد ثبت وجود كل موضوع، فلا مانع من صدق تلك الموجبة ، (وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ) . قيل :أولا: إن ذلك جار في المعدولة أيضا الجواب: أن ذلك لا يضر ؛ إذ لا نقول بالاختصاص. وثانيا : أن مال ذلك أن بين السالبة الخارجية وتلك الموجبة الحقيقية تلازم، وهو لا يصح إلا إذا ثبت سلب كل ما يسلب عن الأفراد الخارجية لأفراده المقدرة، وذا في حين الخفاء؛ فإن الظاهر أن سلب الطيران عن الأفراد الموجودة للعنقاء لا يثبت لأفراده المقدرة. ولا يخفى ما فيه ؛ فإن المحقق لم يصرح في الحواشي القديمة بتلازم السالبة الخارجية وتلك الموجبة الحقيقية، بل لعل مراده تلازم الحقيقيتين، والدليل منطبق عليه وهو كاف لتصحيح القواعد. لا يقال : السالبة الحقيقية قد تصدق بانتفاء الموضوع عن نفس الأمر، محققا أو مقدرا، فكيف يصدق الموجبة ؛ لأن البرهان قد دل على وجود المفهومات ، وفيه ما فيه . وثالثا: بأن أفراد الموضوع قد تكون ممتنعة، فكيف يصدق الموجبة الحقيقية ؛ لاشتراط إمكان الأفراد ؟ وما قال : إن ذلك لا ينافي انعقاد الحقيقية، غاية ما يلزم عدم صدق نحو كل حيوان ماش»، وهو لا يضر ، كما لا يضر عدم صدق كل إنسان كاتب بالفعل غير سديد ؛ لأنه يلزم عدم صدق كل موجبة ؛ فإن من أفراد (ج) الذي ليس (ب) دائما ، فكيف يكون (ب)؟ فلا بد من اشتراط إمكان الأفراد وما يساويه.
Explanation of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum. The subject (like the negative does not require it, but the negative, i.e., the negation of the negative predicate, requires it like the positive [asserted])? Actually, your nature judges that the positive copula absolutely entails existence for the subject, so the difference between it and other affirmatives is judged. The Shaykh said: We only require that the subject in affirmative modified propositions exist, not because 'non-just' entails that, but because affirmation entails that for it to be true, whether 'non-just' applies to existing and nonexistent or applies only to what exists. And therefore, i.e., because absolute affirmation entails existence, the verifiers did not go to the non-entailment of that affirmative of existence, but it was said in the 'Old Glosses': 'The truth is that it is a mental proposition' – apparently he meant by it the true proposition. And all conceptual concepts exist in the external world actually or virtually; because every concept is judged by positive judgments, the least of which is that it is different from what is other than it, or known to the Creator, so it must have some mode of existence in the external world. And also it can be argued that every concept is conceived in its essence by the separate [intellects?], so it must have existence in their minds, and everything existing in the mind exists in the external world. And between it and the negative there is a mutual entailment in terms of truth; because the truth of the negative without it was only in the matter where its subject is nonexistent, and it has been established that every subject exists, so there is no obstacle to the truth of that affirmative. And in it is what is in it. It is said: First, that also applies to the modified [proposition]. The answer: that does not harm, because we do not claim specificity. Second, that what follows from that is that there is an entailment between the external negative and that true affirmative, and it is not valid unless it is established that the negation of everything that is negated of external individuals applies to their estimated individuals, and that is at the time of concealment; for it is apparent that the negation of flight from the existing individuals of the phoenix does not establish for its estimated individuals. And it is not hidden what is in it; for the verifier did not explicitly state in the 'Old Glosses' the entailment of the external negative and that true affirmative, but perhaps his intention is the entailment of the two true [propositions], and the proof applies to it and it is sufficient to correct the rules. It is not said: The true negative may be true by the non-existence of the subject in the external world, actually or virtually, so how can the affirmative be true? Because the proof has indicated the existence of concepts. And in it is what is in it. Third, that the individuals of the subject may be impossible, so how can the true affirmative be true, given the condition of possibility of individuals? And he said: That does not negate the formation of the true [proposition]; the utmost that follows is the non-truth of something like 'Every animal is walking', and that does not harm, just as the non-truth of 'Every human is a writer' actually is not sound; because it follows the non-truth of every affirmative; for among the individuals of 'j' that is not 'b' always, so how can it be 'b'? So the condition of possibility of individuals and what equals it is necessary.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and logical commentary on the conditions for the truth of categorical propositions, specifically focusing on the existential import of affirmative propositions and the relationship between affirmative and negative propositions. The author begins by asserting that the positive copula (الربط الإيجابي) necessarily requires the existence of the subject. He then cites Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) who explains that this requirement is not due to the predicate (e.g., 'non-just') but because affirmation itself demands existence for the proposition to be true. The author notes that the 'Old Glosses' (الحواشي القديمة) describe such a proposition as a 'mental proposition' (قضية ذهنية), which he interprets as a 'true proposition' (قضية حقيقية) that deals with concepts existing in the mind and externally. He then argues that all conceptual concepts have some mode of existence in the external world (نفس الأمر), either actually or virtually, which allows positive judgments about them to be true. He provides a proof: every concept is conceived by the separate intellects, so it exists in their minds, and whatever exists in the mind exists in the external world. From this, he derives a mutual entailment (تلازم) between affirmative and negative propositions in terms of truth: if the negative is true only when the subject is nonexistent, but since every subject exists, the affirmative must be true. However, the author acknowledges that this reasoning has its problems ('وفيه ما فيه'). He then presents objections and answers. The first objection states that the same reasoning applies to modified propositions (المعدولة), but the author responds that there is no harm as they do not claim exclusivity. The second objection concerns the entailment between an external negative (سالبة خارجية) and a true affirmative (موجبة حقيقية), arguing that it requires proving that the negation of properties from actual individuals applies to estimated individuals, which is not obvious (e.g., the negation of flight from actual phoenixes does not apply to estimated ones). The author responds that the verifier (المحقق) intended the entailment between two true propositions (حقيقيتين), not between an external and a true proposition. The third objection raises the case of impossible subjects (e.g., phoenix), asking how a true affirmative can be true if the subject has no possible individuals. The author explains that a true affirmative only requires possibility (إمكان) of individuals, not actual existence, and that the failure of some universal affirmatives (like 'Every animal is walking') does not invalidate the doctrine because such propositions are false anyway. He concludes that the condition of possibility of individuals is necessary for true affirmative propositions.

The Condition of Individuals in Universal Affirmative Propositions
Author critiques the condition of individuals in universal affirmative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the condition of individuals (afrād) in a universal affirmative proposition and evaluate al-Dawānī's argument about natural propositions.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative does not require actual existence of individuals; potential existence suffices.

This lesson delves into the precise meaning of 'individuals' (al-afrād) in universal affirmative propositions. The author clarifies that individuals are those to which the subject term applies actually or potentially, not just actually existing entities. He then critiques al-Dawānī's attempt to prove entailment between natural negative and negative propositions. Understanding this condition is crucial for correctly interpreting universal propositions in Islamic logic and their application in other sciences.

Guided Path
1
Clarify the definition of individuals
Identify the passage where the author defines 'individuals' as that which the subject term applies to actually or potentially (بالفعل أو بالإمكان). Note that this includes potential instances, not just actual existents.
2
Understand the role of potential individuals
Recognize that the author argues that a universal affirmative can be true even if some individuals exist only potentially. The example of a term (C) that is not (B) shows that impossibility of actual existence does not invalidate the proposition.
3
Analyze al-Dawānī's reasoning
Examine al-Dawānī's claim that natures (طبائع) exist in reality, allowing a 'natural' affirmative proposition. His aim is to prove entailment between a negative of the nature and a negative proposition.
4
Evaluate the objection
Consider the author's objection: that al-Dawānī's claim depends on proving that what is negated of individuals is also negated of the nature itself, which is not always true (e.g., 'no individual of man is universal' vs. the nature 'man').

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن المراد بالأفراد ما يصدق عليه العنوان بالفعل أو بالإمكان، فيجوز أن يكون (ج) الذي ليس (ب) مما يمتنع صدق (ج) عليه في نفس الأمر، وذلك لا يستدعي إمكان الأفراد، فتأمل فيه؛ فإن للمناقشة فيه مجالا. قال المحقق الدواني في الحواشي الجديدة: إن طبائع كل مفهوم موجودة في نفس الأمر، فيمكن انعقاد الموجبة ولو طبعية، وإنما المقصود إثبات التلازم بين طبيعية سالبة المحمول والسالبة، ويرد عليه ورودا ظاهرا أنه إنما يتم إذا ثبت سلب كل ما يسلب عن الأفراد للطبيعية، وهو في حيز الخفاء، كيف وسلب الكلية عن أفراد الإنسان صادق مع امتناع ثبوته للطبيعة. وأيضا ذلك لا يفي لتصحيح القواعد هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب. ولما فرغ عن تحقيق الموجبة الكلية أشار إلى تحقيق بواقي المحصورات، وقال: (وَإِذَا حَقَّقْتَ الْإِيجَابَ الْكُلَّيَّ فَقِسْ عَلَيْهِ سَائِرَ الْمَحْصُورَاتِ)، من الجزئيتين والكلية السالبة؛ فإن كل ما اعتبر في الكلية كلا اعتبر في الجزئية بعضا، وكل ما اعتبر في الموجبة إيجابا اعتبر في السالبة سلبا.
And it is not hidden what is in it; for what is meant by individuals is that which the title applies to actually or potentially, so it is possible that (C) which is not (B) is such that it is impossible for (C) to be true of it in reality, and that does not require the possibility of individuals, so reflect on it; for there is room for discussion in it. The investigator al-Dawani said in the New Glosses: The natures of every concept exist in reality, so the affirmative can be formed even if natural, and the intended point is to prove the entailment between the natural negative of the predicate and the negative, and there occurs to it an apparent objection: that it is only complete if it is established that every negation that is negated of individuals is negated of the nature, and that is obscure; how is it that the negation of universality over individuals of man is true while its affirmation to the nature is impossible? And also that does not suffice to correct these rules, and God knows best. And when he finished the verification of the universal affirmative, he pointed to the verification of the remaining quantified propositions, and said: 'And when you have verified the universal affirmative, measure upon it the rest of the quantified propositions,' namely the two particular propositions and the universal negative; for everything considered in the universal as universal is considered in the particular as particular, and everything considered in the affirmative as affirmative is considered in the negative as negative.

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the universal affirmative proposition and its extension to other quantified propositions. The author begins by critiquing a previous claim, clarifying that 'individuals' in the context of a universal proposition refer to things that the subject term applies to either actually or potentially. He notes that it is possible for a term like (C) which is not (B) to exist without entailing that (B) cannot be true of it, and this does not require the actual existence of individuals. He then cites al-Dawani's New Glosses, which argues that the natures of concepts exist in reality, allowing an affirmative proposition even if only in a natural sense. Al-Dawani's aim is to prove the entailment between a natural negative and a negative proposition. The author raises an apparent objection: al-Dawani's claim only works if one can prove that everything negated of individuals is also negated of the nature itself, which is dubious. For example, the statement 'no individual of man is universal' is true, but the same cannot be said of the nature 'man' itself. After this critique, the author notes that once the universal affirmative is properly understood, the same reasoning applies to the other quantified propositions: the two particular propositions and the universal negative. In the universal, what is considered as universal is considered as particular in the particular; and in the affirmative, what is considered as affirmative is considered as negative in the negative. This symmetrical relationship allows the extension of logical principles across all quantifiers.

Key Terms
الأفراد (al-afrād): Individuals to which the subject term applies actually or potentially, not necessarily actual existents. [Note: In earlier logic, 'afrād' often meant actual individuals; here it includes potential instances, broadening the condition.]
الموجبة الكلية (al-mūjiba al-kulliyya): A universal affirmative proposition, e.g., 'Every A is B'. [Note: No significant shift; stable term.]
طبعية (tabī‘iyya): A natural universal proposition, one that holds based on the nature of things regardless of actual individuals. [Note: This term is specific to post-Avicennan logic, often contrasting with 'actual' (fi‘liyya).]
المحصورات (al-mahṣūrāt): Quantified propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. [Note: Standard term in logic; no significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only actually existing entities.
B) Entities that exist both actually and potentially.
C) Entities that the subject term applies to actually or potentially.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
A universal affirmative proposition holds based on potential application of the subject to individuals, not just actual existents.

Extending Verification to Other Quantified Propositions
Author extends verification from universal affirmative to other quantifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of qiyās (analogical extension) to transfer the verification method from universal affirmative to the other three quantified propositions.

Core Idea
The logical treatment of universal affirmative sets the pattern for all quantified propositions by analogy.

After establishing the verification of the universal affirmative, the author directs us to apply the same reasoning to the remaining quantified propositions: the universal negative and both particulars. The principle is simple: what is considered universally in the universal is considered particularly in the particular, and affirmation in the affirmative corresponds to negation in the negative. This symmetry streamlines the entire logical system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of qiyās
Find the sentence: 'وَإِذَا حَقَّقْتَ الْإِيجَابَ الْكُلَّيَّ فَقِسْ عَلَيْهِ سَائِرَ الْمَحْصُورَاتِ' which commands to measure the rest of the quantified propositions upon the universal affirmative.
2
Understand the correspondences
The author states two correspondences: (1) what is considered as universal (kullī) in the universal becomes particular (juz’ī) in the particular; (2) what is considered as affirmative (ījāb) in the affirmative becomes negative (salab) in the negative.
3
Apply to universal negative
For the universal negative (e.g., 'No A is B'), the condition regarding individuals (actual or potential) remains the same as in universal affirmative, but the copula is negated.
4
Apply to particular propositions
For particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some A is B'), the universal condition is scaled down to a part. For particular negative (e.g., 'Some A is not B'), both the particularization and negation are applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن المراد بالأفراد ما يصدق عليه العنوان بالفعل أو بالإمكان، فيجوز أن يكون (ج) الذي ليس (ب) مما يمتنع صدق (ج) عليه في نفس الأمر، وذلك لا يستدعي إمكان الأفراد، فتأمل فيه؛ فإن للمناقشة فيه مجالا. قال المحقق الدواني في الحواشي الجديدة: إن طبائع كل مفهوم موجودة في نفس الأمر، فيمكن انعقاد الموجبة ولو طبعية، وإنما المقصود إثبات التلازم بين طبيعية سالبة المحمول والسالبة، ويرد عليه ورودا ظاهرا أنه إنما يتم إذا ثبت سلب كل ما يسلب عن الأفراد للطبيعية، وهو في حيز الخفاء، كيف وسلب الكلية عن أفراد الإنسان صادق مع امتناع ثبوته للطبيعة. وأيضا ذلك لا يفي لتصحيح القواعد هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب. ولما فرغ عن تحقيق الموجبة الكلية أشار إلى تحقيق بواقي المحصورات، وقال: (وَإِذَا حَقَّقْتَ الْإِيجَابَ الْكُلَّيَّ فَقِسْ عَلَيْهِ سَائِرَ الْمَحْصُورَاتِ)، من الجزئيتين والكلية السالبة؛ فإن كل ما اعتبر في الكلية كلا اعتبر في الجزئية بعضا، وكل ما اعتبر في الموجبة إيجابا اعتبر في السالبة سلبا.
And it is not hidden what is in it; for what is meant by individuals is that which the title applies to actually or potentially, so it is possible that (C) which is not (B) is such that it is impossible for (C) to be true of it in reality, and that does not require the possibility of individuals, so reflect on it; for there is room for discussion in it. The investigator al-Dawani said in the New Glosses: The natures of every concept exist in reality, so the affirmative can be formed even if natural, and the intended point is to prove the entailment between the natural negative of the predicate and the negative, and there occurs to it an apparent objection: that it is only complete if it is established that every negation that is negated of individuals is negated of the nature, and that is obscure; how is it that the negation of universality over individuals of man is true while its affirmation to the nature is impossible? And also that does not suffice to correct these rules, and God knows best. And when he finished the verification of the universal affirmative, he pointed to the verification of the remaining quantified propositions, and said: 'And when you have verified the universal affirmative, measure upon it the rest of the quantified propositions,' namely the two particular propositions and the universal negative; for everything considered in the universal as universal is considered in the particular as particular, and everything considered in the affirmative as affirmative is considered in the negative as negative.

Muhassala and Ma'dula Propositions
Defines ma'dula and muhassala with subtypes and contrasts views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muhassala and ma'dula propositions and identify the three subtypes of ma'dula according to the later scholars.

Core Idea
The classification hinges on whether the negation particle is part of a term.

This passage introduces a fundamental distinction: simple (muhassala) vs. modified (ma'dula) propositions. Understanding this classification is crucial for analyzing logical form, especially when negation is embedded within terms. The author also contrasts his definition with earlier scholars and illustrates with 'Zayd is blind'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for ma'dula
Find where the author states the negation particle is made part of a term (subject or predicate).
2
Note the three subtypes
Write down: ma'dulat al-mawdu', ma'dulat al-mahmul, ma'dulat al-tarafayn.
3
Contrast with earlier scholars
Earlier scholars only considered predicate modifications; later scholars extended to subject and both.
4
Analyze 'Zayd is blind'
Understand why it is ma'dula in meaning (conceptual negation) but muhassala in wording (no explicit particle).
5
Recognize terminological specification
Note that 'affirmative' may be restricted to muhassala, and 'negative' to basita (simple negative).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ قَدْ يُجْعَلُ حَرْفُ السَّلْبِ جُزْءًا فِي طَرَفٍ، مَوْضُوعًا كَانَ أَوْ مَحْمُولًا، فَسُمِّيَتِ الْقَضِيَّةُ مَعْدُولَةً، وَهِيَ مَعْدُولَةُ الْمَوْضُوعِ إِنْ كَانَ جُزْءًا مِنْهُ، أَوْ مَعْدُولَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ إِنْ كَانَ جُزْءًا مِنْهُ، أَوْ مَعْدُولَةُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ إِنْ كَانَ جُزْءًا مِنْهُمَا. وَإِلَّا فَمُحَصَّلَةٌ. هَذَا مَا عَلَيْهِ الْمُتَأَخِّرُونَ. وَالْقُدَمَاءُ قَالُوا: إِنْ كَانَ جُزْءًا مِنَ الْمَحْمُولِ فَمَعْدُولَةٌ وَإِلَّا فَمُحَصَّلَةٌ. وَمَا دَرَى هَذَا الْعَبْدُ فَائِدَةً فِي تَغْيِيرِ الِاصْطِلَاحِ، كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى. وَزَيْدٌ أَعْمَى مَعْدُولَةٌ مَعْقُولَةٌ؛ لِكَوْنِ السَّلْبِ جُزْءًا مِنْ مَفْهُومِ مَحْمُولِهِ. وَمُحَصَّلَةٌ مَلْفُوظَةٌ؛ لِعَدَمِ كَوْنِهِ جُزْءًا مِنْ لَفْظِهِ. وَقَدْ يُخَصُّ اسْمُ الْمُوجَبَةِ بِالْمُحَصَّلَةِ، وَالسَّالِبَةُ بِالْبَسِيطَةِ.
Then, the particle of negation may be made a part of a term, whether subject or predicate, so the proposition is called 'modified' (ma'dula). It is modified in subject if [the negation particle] is part of it, or modified in predicate if it is part of it, or modified in both terms if it is part of both. Otherwise, it is 'simple' (muhassala). This is the position of the later scholars. The earlier scholars said: If it is part of the predicate, then it is modified; otherwise it is simple. And this slave [i.e., the author] does not know any benefit in changing the terminology, as is not hidden. And 'Zayd is blind' is conceptually modified because the negation is part of the concept of its predicate, but verbally simple because it is not part of its wording. And the name 'affirmative' may be specified for the simple proposition, and 'negative' for the simple [i.e., unmodified negative].
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies categorical propositions as either 'muhassala' (simple/unmodified) or 'ma'dula' (modified). It begins by stating that when the negation particle (like 'la' or 'ghayr') is made part of either the subject or predicate term, the proposition is called ma'dula. Three subtypes are recognized: ma'dulat al-mawdu' (modified subject), ma'dulat al-mahmul (modified predicate), and ma'dulat al-tarafayn (both terms modified). If negation is not part of any term, the proposition is muhassala. The author notes that this is the classification of the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun). The earlier scholars (qudama') held a simpler view: only if negation is part of the predicate is the proposition ma'dula; otherwise it is muhassala. The author expresses puzzlement at why the later scholars changed this, implying the earlier view is more coherent. An example is given: 'Zayd is blind' (Zayd a'maa). Here, the predicate 'blind' conceptually contains negation (it means 'one who does not see'), so the proposition is ma'dula in meaning (ma'qula), but since the word 'blind' does not explicitly contain a negation particle, it is muhassala in wording (lafz). Finally, the author mentions that the term 'affirmative' (mujiba) may be restricted to muhassala affirmatives, and the term 'negative' (saliba) may be called 'simple' (basita) when it is not modified.

Key Terms
مَعْدُولَة
مُحَصَّلَة
بَسِيطَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) 'Zaydun a'ma.'
D) 'La shay'a min al-mumkinati qadimun.'

Key Takeaway
The later scholars define ma'dula as a proposition where the negation particle is part of any term, while the earlier scholars limited it to the predicate.

Understanding Modified and Unmodified Propositions
Author defines modified and unmodified propositions with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given proposition is modified (معدولة) or unmodified (محصلة) by analyzing whether a negation is embedded in the concept of the subject or predicate.

Core Idea
Negation can be hidden inside a term's meaning, creating a modified proposition without a visible negation word.

This lesson tackles a subtle but crucial distinction in propositional logic. You already know the difference between affirmative and negative. But what about propositions like 'Zayd is blind'? The author shows that the negation can be built into the concept of a term, changing how we classify the proposition. Understanding this will prevent errors when applying logical operations like conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Take a proposition like 'زيد أعمى' (Zayd is blind). The subject is 'زيد' (Zayd), the predicate is 'أعمى' (blind).
2
Check the predicate's meaning for embedded negation
Ask: does the predicate inherently contain a negation? 'أعمى' literally means 'not-seeing' (لا يبصر). So negation is part of its concept.
This makes it conceptually modified (معدولة معقولة).
3
Check the wording for an explicit negation particle
Is there a 'لا' or 'ليس' in the expression? In 'زيد أعمى', there is no negation particle in the wording, so it is verbally unmodified (محصلة لفظية).
A proposition can be conceptually modified but verbally unmodified.
4
Apply the terminological specialization
Note that the author says 'affirmative' (موجبة) may be restricted to unmodified propositions, and 'negative' (سالبة) to simple (بسيطة) propositions. This avoids confusion with modified affirmatives.
Simple here means a negative with no term embedding negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمحصلة، وما درى هذا العبد فائدة في تغيير الاصطلاح، كما لا يخفى وَزَيْدٌ أَعْمَى مَعْدُولَةٌ مَعْقُولَةٌ؛ لكون السلب جزء من مفهوم محموله، وَمُحَصَّلَةٌ مَلْفُوظَةٌ؛ لعدم كونه جزء من لفظه. وَقَدْ يُخَصُّ اسْمُ الْمُوجَبَةِ بِالْمُحَصَّلَةِ، وَالسَّالِبَةُ بِالْبَسِيطَةِ.
So it is unmodified, and this servant does not know any benefit in changing the terminology, as is not hidden. And 'Zayd is blind' is a conceptually modified [proposition] because the negation is part of the concept of its predicate, and a verbally unmodified [proposition] because it is not part of its wording. And the name 'affirmative' may be specified for the unmodified, and 'negative' for the simple.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms: 'معدولة' (modified proposition) and 'محصلة' (unmodified proposition). The author first dismisses any benefit in altering the conventional terminology. Then, using the example 'زيد أعمى' (Zayd is blind), he explains that this proposition is 'معدولة معقولة' (conceptually modified) because the negation (سلب) is part of the meaning of the predicate 'blind' (i.e., not-seeing). However, it is 'محصلة لفظية' (verbally unmodified) because the negation particle is not explicitly present in the wording of the predicate. The passage then notes that the term 'موجبة' (affirmative) may be restricted to unmodified propositions, and 'سالبة' (negative) to simple propositions (بسيطة), i.e., those with no negation embedded in a term. This distinction is important in later discussions of propositional logic, particularly regarding how negation interacts with subject and predicate terms.

Key Terms
معدولة (muʿaddala): A proposition in which the negation particle is part of the concept of either the subject or the predicate, e.g., 'Zayd is blind' where 'blind' means 'not-seeing'. [Note: In later texts, 'معدولة' may also refer to propositions with negative subjects; here it focuses on the predicate.]
محصلة (muḥaṣṣala): A proposition whose terms are free from an embedded negation, whether affirmative or negative, e.g., 'Zayd is seeing' or 'Zayd is not seeing'. [Note: Sometimes 'محصلة' is used specifically for affirmative propositions; here it includes both affirmative and negative if the negation is not part of a term.]
بسيطة (basīṭa): A simple negative proposition, i.e., a negative that is not modified (e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing'). [Note: In some contexts, 'بسيطة' can refer to simple affirmative; here it is specifically for negative.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A proposition is modified (معدولة) when a negation is embedded in the concept of its subject or predicate, even if no negation word appears in the wording.

Distinction Between Modified Affirmative and Simple Negative
Expansion on the distinction between two proposition types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a modified affirmative (al-mūjaba al-muʿaddala al-maḥmūl) and a simple negative (al-sālaba al-basīṭa) by analyzing their semantic scope, existential import, and syntactic structure.

Core Idea
The simple negative is more general because it holds even when the subject does not exist.

When studying propositions in logic, you will encounter two types that look similar but behave very differently: the modified affirmative (e.g., 'Zayd is blind' = 'Zayd is not-seeing') and the simple negative (e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing'). Both involve negation, but they differ in meaning and truth conditions. This lesson will clarify the difference using the author's explanation, helping you avoid confusion in parsing and inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Locate examples in the Arabic passage: the modified affirmative has a predicate that inherently includes negation (like 'غير بصير' – non-seeing), while the simple negative has a separate negation (like 'ليس ببصير' – is not seeing). Note that the author uses variables (ب and ج) to represent terms.
The modified affirmative is affirmative in form but negative in predicate concept.
2
Compare logical scope
The author says the simple negative is more general (أعم) than the modified affirmative. For example, if the subject is non-existent (e.g., 'The partner of God'), the simple negative is true (there is no such subject to have the predicate), but the modified affirmative would be false because it assumes the subject exists.
This is because the modified affirmative asserts a real predicate, while the simple negative only denies.
3
Analyze syntactic structure
In tripartite propositions, the simple negative places the copula after the negation word (e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing' has the order: subject + negation + copula + predicate). In the modified affirmative, the negation is part of the predicate, so there is only one copula. In bipartite propositions, this distinction is understood implicitly (تقديراً).
Contrast this with the affirmative with a negative predicate (الموجبة السالبة المحمول), which has two copulas and the negation between them.
4
Test your understanding with examples
Take a sample proposition like 'ليس في الجسم جهل' (There is no ignorance in the body). Is it a simple negative or modified affirmative? Since the negation is separate and the copula is absent (bipartite), it is a simple negative. Try to convert it to a modified affirmative: 'الجسم غير جاهل' (The body is non-ignorant) – but note the shift in meaning.
Practice with non-existent subjects to see the difference in truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة في تحقيق المحصورات. كان بين الموجبة المعدولة والسالبة البسيطة نوع اشتباه أراد بيان الفرق، وقال: (وَهِيَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْمَعْدُولَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ); إذ كل ما ثبت سلب (ب) لـ(ج) فيصدق سلب (ب) عنه، وإلا فثبت (ب)، وهو جمع بين المتنافيين، ولا عكس; لجواز كون (ج) معدوما. (وَيَتَأَخَّرُ فِيهَا الرَّابِطُ عَنْ لَفْظِ السَّلْبِ لَفْظًا) في الثلاثية، (أَوْ تَقْدِيرًا) في الثنائية. وأشار إلى الفرق بينهما وبين الموجبة السالبة المحمول بقوله: (وفي الْمُوجَبَةِ السَّالِبَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ رَابِطَانِ) لفظًا أو تقديرا، (وَالسَّلْبُ مُتَوَسِّطٌ بَيْنَهُمَا). بخلاف المعدولة.
Insight into the verification of quantified propositions. There was a type of confusion between the modified affirmative and the simple negative, so he wanted to clarify the difference, and he said: 'And it (the simple negative) is more general than the modified affirmative in the predicate'; because whenever the negation of (B) from (J) is established, the negation of (B) from it (the subject) is true, otherwise (B) would be established, which is a combination of two contradictories, and there is no converse, because it is possible that (J) is non-existent. 'And in it (the simple negative) the copula follows the word of negation in wording' in tripartite propositions, 'or in estimation' in bipartite ones. And he pointed to the difference between them and the affirmative with negative predicate by his saying: 'And in the affirmative with negative predicate there are two copulas' in wording or estimation, 'and the negation is mediate between them.' Unlike the modified.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the distinction between two types of propositions: the modified affirmative (الموجبة المعدولة المحمول) and the simple negative (السالبة البسيطة). The author begins by noting that there is confusion between them, so he clarifies. First, he states that the simple negative is more general than the modified affirmative. He explains using logical variables: If it is established that B is negated from J (i.e., J is not B), then the simple negative 'J is not B' is true. If it were not true, then B would be established for J, which would combine two contradictories (affirming and negating B for J). However, the converse does not hold, because J might be non-existent (e.g., a nonexistent subject). For a nonexistent subject, the simple negative is true (since there is no subject to possess the predicate), but the modified affirmative would be false because it requires the subject to exist. Next, the author explains the syntactic difference: In tripartite propositions (subject, copula, predicate), the simple negative has the copula after the negation explicitly in wording (e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing'), while in the modified affirmative, the negation is part of the predicate and the copula is not after negation. In bipartite propositions (which lack an explicit copula), this distinction is in estimation (intention). Finally, the author distinguishes both from a third type: the affirmative with a negative predicate (الموجبة السالبة المحمول), which has two copulas (one in the concept and one in the predication) with the negation between them, unlike the modified affirmative which has only one copula. This helps in parsing propositions correctly.

Key Terms
موجبة معدولة المحمول (mūjaba muʿaddala al-maḥmūl): An affirmative proposition in which the predicate concept inherently includes negation, e.g., 'Zayd is blind' means 'Zayd is not-seeing'. [Note: This is a specific subtype of 'معدولة' focusing on the predicate.]
سالبة بسيطة (sālaba basīṭa): A negative proposition where the negation is not part of any term, e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing'. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
رابط (rābiṭ): The copula that connects subject and predicate in a proposition. [Note: In tripartite propositions, the copula is explicit (e.g., 'is'); in bipartite, it is implicit.]
ثلاثية (thulāthiyya): A tripartite proposition consisting of subject, copula, and predicate. [Note: Opposed to 'ثنائية' (bipartite) which has only subject and predicate.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the simple negative more general than the modified affirmative in the predicate?
A) Because the simple negative is always true
B) Because the simple negative can be true when the subject does not exist
C) Because the modified affirmative cannot be false
D) Because they are actually equivalent

Key Takeaway
The simple negative is logically more general and syntactically distinct from the modified affirmative; always check existential import when distinguishing them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: The proposition 'ليس في الجسم جهل' (There is no ignorance in the body) is a simple negative. Rewrite it as a modified affirmative (with a negative predicate) and explain whether its truth conditions change.
?

Another objects that this is a modified affirmative that falsely implies existence. Reformulate the objection using the distinction between simple negative and modified affirmative.

The Three Modal Materials of Propositions
Definition of the three modal materials: necessary, impossible, possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition from an Islamic text into one of the three modal materials: necessity, impossibility, or possibility.

Core Idea
Every proposition's truth is either always, never, or sometimes — that is its modal material.

In this passage, the author defines the three fundamental ways a predicate can relate to a subject in reality. This classification is essential for understanding the strength of claims in Islamic arguments, from fiqh rulings to kalam proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify subject and predicate
Take a proposition and extract the subject (mawḍūʿ) and predicate (maḥmūl). For example, in 'Human is an animal', subject is 'human', predicate is 'animal'.
2
Determine the constant relation in reality
Ask: In reality, is the predicate always true of the subject? If always true, the material is necessity (wujūb). If always false, it is impossibility (imtināʿ). If neither, it is possibility (imkān).
The material is based on the real relation, not on linguistic form.
3
Apply to examples
For 'Human is an animal', the predicate 'animal' is always true of human → necessary. For 'Human is a stone', always false → impossible. For 'Human is writing', sometimes true, sometimes false → possible.
4
Note the definition of 'matter'
The Shaykh clarifies that 'matter' is the state of the predicate in itself with respect to the subject regarding permanent truth or falsehood, not due to our statement.
This distinguishes the material from linguistic accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَضْلُ فِي الْمُوَجَهَاتِ ) (كُلُّ نِسْبَةٍ في نَفْسٍ الْأَمْرِ إِمَّا وَاجِبَةٌ ، إن كانت ضرورية التحقق، (أَوْ مُمْتَنِعَةٌ)، إن كانت ضرورية اللاتحقق، (أَوْ مُمْكِنَةٌ)، إن لم تكن ضرورية التحقق واللاتحقق ، (وَتِلْكَ الْكَيْفِيَّاتُ) إذا كانت للإيجاب (الْمَوَادُّ) وعناصر . قال الشيخ واعلم أن حال المحمول في نفسه عند الموضوع لا التي تجب ببياننا وتصريحنا بالفعل أنه كيف هي ،له ولا التي تكون في كل نسبة، بل الحال التي للمحمول عند الموضوع بالنسبة الإيجابية من دوام صدق أو كذب أو لادوامهما يسمى «مادة»، فإما أن يكون الحال هو أن المحمول يدوم ويجب صدق إيجابه ، فيسمى مادة الوجوب ، كحال الحيوان عند الإنسان، أو يدوم ويجب كذب إيجابه ، ويسمى مادة الامتناع كحال الحجر عند الإنسان، أو لا يدوم ولا يجب مادة الإمكان، كحال الكتابة عند الإنسان. وهذه الحال لا تختلف في الإيجاب والسلب؛ فإن السالبة توجد لمحمولها هذه الحال بعينها ؛ فإن محمولها يكون مستحقا عند الإيجاب، وإن لم يكن أوجب. وقد زل صاحب «الأفق المبين»؛ حيث قال: إن النسبة السلبية قطع النسبة ، والقطع ليس له كيفية في الواقع، بل الكيفيات في السالبة للمسلوب، ففي السالبة الضرورية ليس النسبة ضرورية، بل رفع النسبة الضرورية، وقال: فعلى هذا لا يجب في التناقض من اختلاف الجهة، إنما هي بل نقيض كل موجبة مثلها.
A benefit on modal propositions. Every relation in reality is either necessary, if it is necessarily actualized, or impossible, if it is necessarily not actualized, or possible, if it is neither necessarily actualized nor necessarily not actualized. And those qualities, when they are for affirmation, are the 'materials' and elements. The Shaykh said: Know that the state of the predicate in itself with respect to the subject—not that which is necessitated by our statement and our explicit act [of affirming] how it is for it, nor that which occurs in every relation—but the state that the predicate has with respect to the subject in terms of the affirmative relation, of permanent truth or falsehood or neither, is called the 'matter'. So either the state is that the predicate persists and its affirmation is necessarily true, and it is called the matter of necessity, like the state of 'animal' with respect to 'human'; or it persists and its affirmation is necessarily false, and it is called the matter of impossibility, like the state of 'stone' with respect to 'human'; or it does not persist nor is it necessary, and it is called the matter of possibility, like the state of 'writing' with respect to 'human'. And this state does not differ between affirmation and negation; for the negative has the same state for its predicate; for its predicate is deserving of that state in the affirmative, even if it is not affirmed. And the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' slipped where he said: 'The negative relation is the cutting off of the relation, and cutting off has no actual quality, rather qualities in the negative are for the negated predicate; so in necessary negatives, the relation is not necessary, rather it is the removal of the necessary relation.' And he said: On this basis, in contradiction there is no requirement of difference in modality; rather the contradictory of every affirmative is its like.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on modal propositions. It first defines three kinds of relations in reality: necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), and possible (mumkin). These are the 'materials' (mawadd) of propositions. The author then quotes the Shaykh who clarifies that the 'matter' is the state of the predicate relative to the subject in terms of permanent truth or falsehood. For example, 'human is an animal' is necessary because it is always true; 'human is a stone' is impossible because it is always false; 'human is writing' is possible because it can be true or false. This state remains the same for both affirmative and negative; the negative proposition shares the same material. Then a criticism is leveled at the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' who mistakenly claimed that in negative propositions the relation is cut off and has no modality, leading to the incorrect conclusion that the contradictory of a necessary affirmative is a necessary affirmative—which violates the rules of contradiction. For instance, if 'Every human is an animal' is necessary, its contradictory 'Some human is not an animal' should be possible, not necessary. The correct view is that the material applies to both affirmative and negative.

Key Terms
الموجَهات
المادة
مادة الوجوب
مادة الامتناع
مادة الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) الإنسان ليس حيوانا (Human is not an animal)

Key Takeaway
The modal material of a proposition is determined by the constant truth-value of the predicate in reality.

The Invariance of Modal Material Across Affirmation and Negation
Clarification that modal material applies to negatives and refutation of a mistaken view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modal material of a negative proposition given the affirmative, and identify the error in claiming that negative propositions have no modality.

Core Idea
The modal material of a proposition is the same for its affirmation and negation; the error of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' would break the law of contradiction.

This passage extends the concept of modal material to negative propositions and corrects a serious mistake. Some logicians thought that negation removes modality, but the author shows this leads to absurd consequences for the logical principle of contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Review the definition of matter
Recall that 'matter' is the state of the predicate in reality—whether it is always true or always false of the subject. This state is independent of whether we affirm or deny it.
2
Apply to negative propositions
For a negative proposition like 'Human is not an animal', the predicate 'animal' still has the same relation (always true of human). Thus the material of the predicate is unchanged.
The negative proposition has a modal material: it is impossible (since it is always false).
3
Identify al-Ufuq al-Mubin's error
The author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' claimed that negation cuts off the relation, so a negative proposition has no modality. This is incorrect because the predicate's state remains.
4
Understand the consequence for contradiction
If a negative had no modality, then the contradictory of a necessary affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is an animal') would also be necessary (since no modality change). But contradictories must have opposite truth-values and different modalities. Thus the error leads to a violation of the rules of contradiction.
The correct contradictory of a necessary affirmative is impossible (e.g., 'Some human is not an animal' is impossible).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A benefit on modal propositions. Every relation in reality is either necessary, if it is necessarily actualized, or impossible, if it is necessarily not actualized, or possible, if it is neither necessarily actualized nor necessarily not actualized. The Three Materials of Propositions
The author defines the three materials of propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three materials (mawadd) of any proposition and classify a given proposition as necessary, impossible, or possible based on the predicate-subject relation.

Core Idea
The material of a proposition is the inherent modal quality of the predicate with respect to the subject, independent of expression.

The author distinguishes between the material (mādda) and the modality (jiha) of a proposition. The material is the underlying reality—whether the predicate is constantly true, constantly false, or neither—while the modality is the linguistic indicator. In this lesson, we focus on the three materials and their definitions as presented in the source.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate-subject relation
In any proposition, locate the subject and predicate. For example, in 'Every human is an animal', 'human' is the subject and 'animal' is the predicate.
2
Determine constancy of truth or falsehood
Ask: Is the predicate always true of the subject, always false, or neither? For 'human' and 'animal', the predicate is always true; for 'human' and 'stone', it is always false; for 'human' and 'writing', it is sometimes true and sometimes false.
3
Assign the material
If constant truth, the material is necessity (wujūb). If constant falsehood, the material is impossibility (imtinā‘). If neither, the material is possibility (imkān).
4
Practice with Islamic examples
Take propositions from fiqh or kalam. For instance, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is necessity; 'No human is a stone' is impossibility; 'Some people pray' is possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ: كُلُّ نِسْبَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ إِمَّا وَاجِبَةٌ إِنْ كَانَتْ ضَرُورِيَّةَ التَّحَقُّقِ، أَوْ مُمْتَنِعَةٌ إِنْ كَانَتْ ضَرُورِيَّةَ اللَّاتَحَقُّقِ، أَوْ مُمْكِنَةٌ إِنْ لَمْ تَكُنْ ضَرُورِيَّةَ التَّحَقُّقِ وَاللَّاتَحَقُّقِ. وَتِلْكَ الْكَيْفِيَّاتُ إِذَا كَانَتْ لِلْإِيجَابِ الْمَوَادُّ. قَالَ الشَّيْخُ: اعْلَمْ أَنَّ حَالَ الْمَحْمُولِ فِي نَفْسِهِ عِنْدَ الْمَوْضُوعِ لَا الَّتِي تَجِبُ بِبَيَانِنَا وَتَصْرِيحِنَا بِالْفِعْلِ أَنَّهُ كَيْفَ هِيَ لَهُ، وَلَا الَّتِي تَكُونُ فِي كُلِّ نِسْبَةٍ، بَلِ الْحَالُ الَّتِي لِلْمَحْمُولِ عِنْدَ الْمَوْضُوعِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ الْإِيجَابِيَّةِ مِنْ دَوَامِ صِدْقٍ أَوْ كَذِبٍ أَوْ لَا دَوَامِهِمَا يُسَمَّى «مَادَّةً». فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْحَالُ هُوَ أَنَّ الْمَحْمُولَ يَدُومُ وَيَجِبُ صِدْقُ إِيجَابِهِ، فَيُسَمَّى مَادَّةَ الْوُجُوبِ، كَحَالِ الْحَيَوَانِ عِنْدَ الْإِنْسَانِ، أَوْ يَدُومُ وَيَجِبُ كَذِبُ إِيجَابِهِ، وَيُسَمَّى مَادَّةَ الِامْتِنَاعِ كَحَالِ الْحَجَرِ عِنْدَ الْإِنْسَانِ، أَوْ لَا يَدُومُ وَلَا يَجِبُ مَادَّةَ الْإِمْكَانِ، كَحَالِ الْكِتَابَةِ عِنْدَ الْإِنْسَانِ. وَهَذِهِ الْحَالُ لَا تَخْتَلِفُ فِي الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ؛ فَإِنَّ السَّالِبَةَ تُوجَدُ لِمَحْمُولِهَا هَذِهِ الْحَالَ بِعَيْنِهَا؛ فَإِنَّ مَحْمُولَهَا يَكُونُ مُسْتَحِقًّا عِنْدَ الْإِيجَابِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُوجِبْ. وَقَدْ زَلَّ صَاحِبُ «الْأُفُقِ الْمُبِينِ»؛ حَيْثُ قَالَ: إِنَّ النِّسْبَةَ السَّلْبِيَّةَ قَطْعُ النِّسْبَةِ، وَالْقَطْعُ لَيْسَ لَهُ كَيْفِيَّةٌ فِي الْوَاقِعِ، بَلِ الْكَيْفِيَّاتُ فِي السَّالِبَةِ لِلْمَسْلُوبِ، فَفِي السَّالِبَةِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ لَيْسَ النِّسْبَةُ ضَرُورِيَّةً، بَلْ رَفْعُ النِّسْبَةِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ، وَقَالَ: فَعَلَى هَذَا لَا يَجِبُ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ اخْتِلَافُ الْجِهَةِ، بَلْ نَقِيضُ كُلِّ مُوجَبَةٍ مِثْلُهَا. فَصْلٌ فِي الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ وَيُرَدُّ عَلَيْهِ وُرُودًا ظَاهِرًا أَنَّ بَعْدَ تَسْلِيمِ كَوْنِ السَّلْبِ قَطْعًا لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ الْقَطْعَ لَيْسَ لَهُ كَيْفِيَّةٌ، كَيْفَ وَلَيْسَ مَفْهُومُ مَا خَالِيًا عَنِ الْمَوَادِّ الثَّلَاثِ. قَالَ الشَّيْخُ فِي الْإِشَارَاتِ: وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ السَّالِبَةَ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ غَيْرُ سَالِبَةِ الضَّرُورَةِ، وَالسَّالِبَةَ الْمُمْكِنَةَ غَيْرُ سَالِبَةِ الْإِمْكَانِ، وَالسَّالِبَةَ الْوُجُودِيَّةَ الَّتِي بِلَا دَوَامٍ غَيْرُ سَالِبَةِ الْوُجُودِ بِلَا دَوَامٍ، فَالْمُتَّبَعُ الْحَقُّ أَنَّ كُلَّ نِسْبَةٍ مُتَكَيِّفَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ بِإِحْدَى الثَّلَاثِ، لَكِنِ اصْطِلَاحُهُمْ عَلَى تَسْمِيَتِهَا إِذَا كَانَتْ لِلْإِيجَابِ مَوَادَّ. هَذَا، وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ بِالصَّوَابِ. (وَالدَّالُّ عَلَيْهَا)، أَيْ عَلَى تِلْكَ الْكَيْفِيَّاتِ، سَوَاءً كَانَتْ لِلْإِيجَابِ أَوِ السَّلْبِ (الْجِهَةُ، وَمَا اشْتَمَلَتْ عَلَيْهَا)، أَيْ عَلَى الْجِهَةِ (يُسَمَّى مُوَجَّهَةً). وَرُبَاعِيَّةٌ بَسِيطَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ حَقِيقَتُهَا إِيجَابًا فَقَطْ أَوْ سَلْبًا فَقَطْ، وَمُرَكَّبَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ مُلْتَئِمَةً مِنْهُمَا، أَيْ مِنَ الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ، (وَالْعِبْرَةُ فِي التَّسْمِيَةِ لِلْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ)، فَإِنْ كَانَ مُوجِبًا سُمِّيَتْ مُوجَبَةً وَإِلَّا سَالِبَةً، (وَإِلَّا) يَشْتَمِلُ عَلَيْهَا (فَمُطْلَقَةٌ) وَمُهْمَلَةٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْجِهَةُ، (وَهِيَ)، أَيِ الْجِهَةُ (إِنْ وَافَقَتِ الْمَادَّةَ)، بِأَنْ تَكُونَ عَيْنًا لَهَا أَوْ مُلَازِمَةً لَهَا وَلَا تَكُونَ مُنَافِيَةً لَهَا، كَمَا فِي قَوْلِنَا «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ بِالْوُجُوبِ»، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ بِالْوُجُوبِ»، (صَدَقَتِ الْقَضِيَّةُ، وَإِلَّا) تُوَافِقْ كَذَبَتْ. وَالتَّحْقِيقُ أَنَّ الْمَوَادَّ الْحِكَمِيَّةَ هِيَ الْجِهَاتُ الْمَنْطِقِيَّةُ بِحَسَبِ الْمَفْهُومِ، إِلَّا أَنَّ فِي الْمَنْطِقِ يُعْتَبَرُ عُمُومًا بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى كُلِّ قَضِيَّةٍ، وَفِي الْحِكْمَةِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى....
Section on modal propositions: Every relation in reality is either necessary if it is necessarily actualized, or impossible if it is necessarily not actualized, or possible if it is neither necessarily actualized nor necessarily not actualized. And those qualities, when they are for affirmation, are the 'materials'. The Shaykh said: Know that the state of the predicate in itself with respect to the subject—not that which is necessitated by our statement and explicit declaration of how it is for it, nor that which exists in every relation—but the state that the predicate has with respect to the subject in the affirmative relation of either constant truth, constant falsehood, or non-constancy of both, is called 'matter'. Either the state is that the predicate persists and its affirmative is necessarily true, so it is called the matter of necessity, like the state of 'animal' with respect to 'human'; or it persists and its affirmative is necessarily false, and it is called the matter of impossibility, like the state of 'stone' with respect to 'human'; or it neither persists nor is necessary, the matter of possibility, like the state of 'writing' with respect to 'human'. And this state does not differ in affirmation and negation; for the negative proposition has for its predicate exactly this state; for its predicate would be deserving of it in affirmation, even if it does not affirm. And the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' erred when he said: The negative relation is a cutting of the relation, and cutting has no quality in reality; rather, the qualities in the negative belong to the negated; so in the necessary negative, the relation is not necessary, but rather the removal of a necessary relation. He said: Thus, in contradiction, difference of modality is not required; rather, the contradictory of every affirmative is its like. Section on modal propositions: And it is refuted in an obvious refutation that even if we grant that negation is a cutting, we do not grant that the cutting has no quality; how so, when there is no concept empty of the three materials? The Shaykh said in al-Isharat: Know that the necessary negative is not the same as the negation of necessity, and the possible negative is not the same as the negation of possibility, and the existential negative that is without duration is not the same as the negation of existence without duration. The correct view is that every relation is qualified in reality by one of the three, but their convention is to call them 'materials' when they are for affirmation. This is what is correct, and God knows best. That which indicates them—that is, those qualities, whether for affirmation or negation—is the 'modality', and that which contains it—that is, contains the modality—is called a 'modal proposition'. And it is fourfold: simple if its essence is only affirmation or only negation, and compound if it is composed of both, i.e., of affirmation and negation. The criterion in naming is the first part; if it is affirmative, it is called affirmative, otherwise negative. If it does not contain a modality, then it is absolute and unrestricted with respect to modality. And the modality, if it agrees with the matter—by being identical to it or concomitant with it and not contrary to it, as in our statement 'Every human is necessarily an animal' and 'No human is necessarily a stone'—the proposition is true; otherwise, if it does not agree, it is false. The verification is that the philosophical materials are the logical modalities in concept, except that in logic they are considered generally with respect to every proposition, and in philosophy with respect to...
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and expository section on modal propositions. The author begins by stating the three basic modalities: necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), and possible (mumkin), defined in terms of necessary realization in reality (nafs al-amr). These qualities, when they belong to affirmative propositions, are called 'materials' (mawadd). The author then expands on the concept of 'matter' (madda), explaining that it is the inherent state of the predicate relative to the subject in an affirmative proposition, independent of our explicit expression. This state is either constant truth (necessity), constant falsehood (impossibility), or neither (possibility). Examples from natural kinds illustrate: 'animal' is necessarily true of 'human'; 'stone' is necessarily false; 'writing' is possible. Crucially, the author asserts that the same material state applies equally to negative propositions; the predicate of a negative would have the same material status if affirmed. A criticism is then directed at the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin', who wrongly claimed that negative propositions lack a modality because negation is a 'cutting' of the relation. The response rejects this: every concept partakes in the three materials. The passage then cites al-Isharat to distinguish between different types of negative modalities (e.g., necessary negative vs. negation of necessity). The correct view is that every relation is qualified by one of the three. The term 'jiha' (modality) is introduced as the indicator of these qualities, and a proposition containing a modality is called 'muwajjaha'. Modal propositions are divided into simple (basita) and compound (murakkaba) based on whether their nature is purely affirmative or negative, or mixed. The naming follows the first component. Propositions without a modality are 'mutlaqa' (absolute). Finally, a truth condition is given: a modal proposition is true if the expressed modality agrees with the actual material — either identical or concomitant, not contrary. Examples from fiqh-like contexts illustrate. The passage concludes with a note that philosophical materials (mawadd hikmiyya) are conceptually the same as logical modalities (jiha mantiqiyya), but logic considers them generally for all propositions while philosophy considers them specifically.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the material of a proposition like 'No human is a stone'?
A) Necessity of affirmation
B) Impossibility of affirmation
C) Possibility of affirmation
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The material of a proposition is the inherent modal quality—necessity, impossibility, or possibility—of the predicate with respect to the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following proposition into one of the three materials: 'Every believer is destined for Paradise.' Explain your reasoning.
?

Propositions
beginner
6 min
Modalities, Modal Propositions, and Their Classification
The author defines modality and classifies modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between material (mādda) and modality (jiha), identify a modal proposition (muwajjaha), and classify it as simple or compound.

Core Idea
The modality is the linguistic indicator of the material; a proposition containing a modality is modal; it is simple if purely affirmative or negative, and compound if mixed.

After defining materials, the author introduces the term 'jiha' (modality) as the signifier of those qualities. A proposition that includes a modality is called 'muwajjaha' (modal). Modal propositions are further divided into simple and compound. This lesson clarifies these terms and their relationships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality indicator
Look for words like 'necessarily', 'possibly', 'impossibly' or their Arabic equivalents (بالوجوب, بالإمكان, بالامتناع). These are the modalities (jihāt).
2
Check if the proposition contains a modality
If it does, it is a modal proposition (muwajjaha). If not, it is absolute (muṭlaqa). For example, 'Every human is an animal' without a modality word is absolute; 'Every human is necessarily an animal' is modal.
3
Determine if it is simple or compound
Examine the essence of the proposition: if it is purely affirmative or purely negative, it is simple (basīṭa). If it combines affirmation and negation (e.g., conditional or disjunctive statements), it is compound (murakkaba).
The naming follows the first part: if the first component is affirmative, the whole is called affirmative, etc.

فَصْلٌ فِي الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ: كُلُّ نِسْبَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ إِمَّا وَاجِبَةٌ إِنْ كَانَتْ ضَرُورِيَّةَ التَّحَقُّقِ، أَوْ مُمْتَنِعَةٌ إِنْ كَانَتْ ضَرُورِيَّةَ اللَّاتَحَقُّقِ، أَوْ مُمْكِنَةٌ إِنْ لَمْ تَكُنْ ضَرُورِيَّةَ التَّحَقُّقِ وَاللَّاتَحَقُّقِ. وَتِلْكَ الْكَيْفِيَّاتُ إِذَا كَانَتْ لِلْإِيجَابِ الْمَوَادُّ. قَالَ الشَّيْخُ: اعْلَمْ أَنَّ حَالَ الْمَحْمُولِ فِي نَفْسِهِ عِنْدَ الْمَوْضُوعِ لَا الَّتِي تَجِبُ بِبَيَانِنَا وَتَصْرِيحِنَا بِالْفِعْلِ أَنَّهُ كَيْفَ هِيَ لَهُ، وَلَا الَّتِي تَكُونُ فِي كُلِّ نِسْبَةٍ، بَلِ الْحَالُ الَّتِي لِلْمَحْمُولِ عِنْدَ الْمَوْضُوعِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ الْإِيجَابِيَّةِ مِنْ دَوَامِ صِدْقٍ أَوْ كَذِبٍ أَوْ لَا دَوَامِهِمَا يُسَمَّى «مَادَّةً». فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْحَالُ هُوَ أَنَّ الْمَحْمُولَ يَدُومُ وَيَجِبُ صِدْقُ إِيجَابِهِ، فَيُسَمَّى مَادَّةَ الْوُجُوبِ، كَحَالِ الْحَيَوَانِ عِنْدَ الْإِنْسَانِ، أَوْ يَدُومُ وَيَجِبُ كَذِبُ إِيجَابِهِ، وَيُسَمَّى مَادَّةَ الِامْتِنَاعِ كَحَالِ الْحَجَرِ عِنْدَ الْإِنْسَانِ، أَوْ لَا يَدُومُ وَلَا يَجِبُ مَادَّةَ الْإِمْكَانِ، كَحَالِ الْكِتَابَةِ عِنْدَ الْإِنْسَانِ. وَهَذِهِ الْحَالُ لَا تَخْتَلِفُ فِي الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ؛ فَإِنَّ السَّالِبَةَ تُوجَدُ لِمَحْمُولِهَا هَذِهِ الْحَالَ بِعَيْنِهَا؛ فَإِنَّ مَحْمُولَهَا يَكُونُ مُسْتَحِقًّا عِنْدَ الْإِيجَابِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُوجِبْ. وَقَدْ زَلَّ صَاحِبُ «الْأُفُقِ الْمُبِينِ»؛ حَيْثُ قَالَ: إِنَّ النِّسْبَةَ السَّلْبِيَّةَ قَطْعُ النِّسْبَةِ، وَالْقَطْعُ لَيْسَ لَهُ كَيْفِيَّةٌ فِي الْوَاقِعِ، بَلِ الْكَيْفِيَّاتُ فِي السَّالِبَةِ لِلْمَسْلُوبِ، فَفِي السَّالِبَةِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ لَيْسَ النِّسْبَةُ ضَرُورِيَّةً، بَلْ رَفْعُ النِّسْبَةِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ، وَقَالَ: فَعَلَى هَذَا لَا يَجِبُ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ اخْتِلَافُ الْجِهَةِ، بَلْ نَقِيضُ كُلِّ مُوجَبَةٍ مِثْلُهَا. فَصْلٌ فِي الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ وَيُرَدُّ عَلَيْهِ وُرُودًا ظَاهِرًا أَنَّ بَعْدَ تَسْلِيمِ كَوْنِ السَّلْبِ قَطْعًا لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ الْقَطْعَ لَيْسَ لَهُ كَيْفِيَّةٌ، كَيْفَ وَلَيْسَ مَفْهُومُ مَا خَالِيًا عَنِ الْمَوَادِّ الثَّلَاثِ. قَالَ الشَّيْخُ فِي الْإِشَارَاتِ: وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ السَّالِبَةَ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ غَيْرُ سَالِبَةِ الضَّرُورَةِ، وَالسَّالِبَةَ الْمُمْكِنَةَ غَيْرُ سَالِبَةِ الْإِمْكَانِ، وَالسَّالِبَةَ الْوُجُودِيَّةَ الَّتِي بِلَا دَوَامٍ غَيْرُ سَالِبَةِ الْوُجُودِ بِلَا دَوَامٍ، فَالْمُتَّبَعُ الْحَقُّ أَنَّ كُلَّ نِسْبَةٍ مُتَكَيِّفَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ بِإِحْدَى الثَّلَاثِ، لَكِنِ اصْطِلَاحُهُمْ عَلَى تَسْمِيَتِهَا إِذَا كَانَتْ لِلْإِيجَابِ مَوَادَّ. هَذَا، وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ بِالصَّوَابِ. (وَالدَّالُّ عَلَيْهَا)، أَيْ عَلَى تِلْكَ الْكَيْفِيَّاتِ، سَوَاءً كَانَتْ لِلْإِيجَابِ أَوِ السَّلْبِ (الْجِهَةُ، وَمَا اشْتَمَلَتْ عَلَيْهَا)، أَيْ عَلَى الْجِهَةِ (يُسَمَّى مُوَجَّهَةً). وَرُبَاعِيَّةٌ بَسِيطَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ حَقِيقَتُهَا إِيجَابًا فَقَطْ أَوْ سَلْبًا فَقَطْ، وَمُرَكَّبَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ مُلْتَئِمَةً مِنْهُمَا، أَيْ مِنَ الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ، (وَالْعِبْرَةُ فِي التَّسْمِيَةِ لِلْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ)، فَإِنْ كَانَ مُوجِبًا سُمِّيَتْ مُوجَبَةً وَإِلَّا سَالِبَةً، (وَإِلَّا) يَشْتَمِلُ عَلَيْهَا (فَمُطْلَقَةٌ) وَمُهْمَلَةٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْجِهَةُ، (وَهِيَ)، أَيِ الْجِهَةُ (إِنْ وَافَقَتِ الْمَادَّةَ)، بِأَنْ تَكُونَ عَيْنًا لَهَا أَوْ مُلَازِمَةً لَهَا وَلَا تَكُونَ مُنَافِيَةً لَهَا، كَمَا فِي قَوْلِنَا «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ بِالْوُجُوبِ»، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ بِالْوُجُوبِ»، (صَدَقَتِ الْقَضِيَّةُ، وَإِلَّا) تُوَافِقْ كَذَبَتْ. وَالتَّحْقِيقُ أَنَّ الْمَوَادَّ الْحِكَمِيَّةَ هِيَ الْجِهَاتُ الْمَنْطِقِيَّةُ بِحَسَبِ الْمَفْهُومِ، إِلَّا أَنَّ فِي الْمَنْطِقِ يُعْتَبَرُ عُمُومًا بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى كُلِّ قَضِيَّةٍ، وَفِي الْحِكْمَةِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى....
Section on modal propositions: Every relation in reality is either necessary if it is necessarily actualized, or impossible if it is necessarily not actualized, or possible if it is neither necessarily actualized nor necessarily not actualized. And those qualities, when they are for affirmation, are the 'materials'. The Shaykh said: Know that the state of the predicate in itself with respect to the subject—not that which is necessitated by our statement and explicit declaration of how it is for it, nor that which exists in every relation—but the state that the predicate has with respect to the subject in the affirmative relation of either constant truth, constant falsehood, or non-constancy of both, is called 'matter'. Either the state is that the predicate persists and its affirmative is necessarily true, so it is called the matter of necessity, like the state of 'animal' with respect to 'human'; or it persists and its affirmative is necessarily false, and it is called the matter of impossibility, like the state of 'stone' with respect to 'human'; or it neither persists nor is necessary, the matter of possibility, like the state of 'writing' with respect to 'human'. And this state does not differ in affirmation and negation; for the negative proposition has for its predicate exactly this state; for its predicate would be deserving of it in affirmation, even if it does not affirm. And the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' erred when he said: The negative relation is a cutting of the relation, and cutting has no quality in reality; rather, the qualities in the negative belong to the negated; so in the necessary negative, the relation is not necessary, but rather the removal of a necessary relation. He said: Thus, in contradiction, difference of modality is not required; rather, the contradictory of every affirmative is its like. Section on modal propositions: And it is refuted in an obvious refutation that even if we grant that negation is a cutting, we do not grant that the cutting has no quality; how so, when there is no concept empty of the three materials? The Shaykh said in al-Isharat: Know that the necessary negative is not the same as the negation of necessity, and the possible negative is not the same as the negation of possibility, and the existential negative that is without duration is not the same as the negation of existence without duration. The correct view is that every relation is qualified in reality by one of the three, but their convention is to call them 'materials' when they are for affirmation. This is what is correct, and God knows best. That which indicates them—that is, those qualities, whether for affirmation or negation—is the 'modality', and that which contains it—that is, contains the modality—is called a 'modal proposition'. And it is fourfold: simple if its essence is only affirmation or only negation, and compound if it is composed of both, i.e., of affirmation and negation. The criterion in naming is the first part; if it is affirmative, it is called affirmative, otherwise negative. If it does not contain a modality, then it is absolute and unrestricted with respect to modality. And the modality, if it agrees with the matter—by being identical to it or concomitant with it and not contrary to it, as in our statement 'Every human is necessarily an animal' and 'No human is necessarily a stone'—the proposition is true; otherwise, if it does not agree, it is false. The verification is that the philosophical materials are the logical modalities in concept, except that in logic they are considered generally with respect to every proposition, and in philosophy with respect to...
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and expository section on modal propositions. The author begins by stating the three basic modalities: necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), and possible (mumkin), defined in terms of necessary realization in reality (nafs al-amr). These qualities, when they belong to affirmative propositions, are called 'materials' (mawadd). The author then expands on the concept of 'matter' (madda), explaining that it is the inherent state of the predicate relative to the subject in an affirmative proposition, independent of our explicit expression. This state is either constant truth (necessity), constant falsehood (impossibility), or neither (possibility). Examples from natural kinds illustrate: 'animal' is necessarily true of 'human'; 'stone' is necessarily false; 'writing' is possible. Crucially, the author asserts that the same material state applies equally to negative propositions; the predicate of a negative would have the same material status if affirmed. A criticism is then directed at the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin', who wrongly claimed that negative propositions lack a modality because negation is a 'cutting' of the relation. The response rejects this: every concept partakes in the three materials. The passage then cites al-Isharat to distinguish between different types of negative modalities (e.g., necessary negative vs. negation of necessity). The correct view is that every relation is qualified by one of the three. The term 'jiha' (modality) is introduced as the indicator of these qualities, and a proposition containing a modality is called 'muwajjaha'. Modal propositions are divided into simple (basita) and compound (murakkaba) based on whether their nature is purely affirmative or negative, or mixed. The naming follows the first component. Propositions without a modality are 'mutlaqa' (absolute). Finally, a truth condition is given: a modal proposition is true if the expressed modality agrees with the actual material — either identical or concomitant, not contrary. Examples from fiqh-like contexts illustrate. The passage concludes with a note that philosophical materials (mawadd hikmiyya) are conceptually the same as logical modalities (jiha mantiqiyya), but logic considers them generally for all propositions while philosophy considers them specifically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is a 'muwajjaha' proposition?
A) A proposition that is necessarily true.
B) A proposition that contains a modality.
C) A proposition that is compound.
D) A proposition that is absolute.

Key Takeaway
A modal proposition includes a linguistic modality; it is simple if purely affirmative or negative, and compound if mixed; without a modality, it is absolute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Convert the absolute proposition 'Every believer enters Paradise' into a necessary modal proposition using an explicit modality. Then classify it as simple or compound.
?
Find a modal proposition in a classical kalam text and identify its modality and whether it is simple or compound.
