Propositions
intermediate
8 min
Truth Conditions for Modal Propositions and a Refutation on Negatives
The author gives truth conditions for modal propositions and refutes a mistake about negative modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the truth condition for modal propositions (agreement of modality with material) and refute the claim that negative propositions lack modality.

Core Idea
A modal proposition is true only if the expressed modality agrees with the actual material; negative propositions also have materials and modalities.

After defining materials and modalities, the author gives a criterion for the truth of modal propositions: the modality must match the material. He then criticizes the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' who erroneously claimed that negative propositions lack modality. This lesson unpacks both the positive rule and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expressed modality and the material
Given a modal proposition, first extract the modality (e.g., 'necessarily') and then determine the material (the inherent state of predicate to subject).
2
Check for agreement
The modality is true if it is either identical to the material or at least not contrary to it. For example, 'Every human is necessarily an animal': the modality 'necessarily' agrees with the material of necessity, so true. 'Every human is possibly a stone' would be false because the modality 'possibly' conflicts with the material of impossibility.
3
Understand the refutation against al-Ufuq al-Mubin
He claimed negative propositions have no modality because negation is a 'cutting' of the relation, which cannot be qualified. The response: every concept partakes in the three materials; even cutting itself has a quality. Negative propositions do have modalities, as shown by distinctions like 'necessary negative' vs 'negation of necessity'.
The source cites al-Isharat for the correct distinctions.
4
Apply the distinction to Islamic examples
Consider 'No human is a stone' – the material is impossibility, so the modality 'necessarily' in 'No human is necessarily a stone' is true. The negative proposition does have a modality.

Source Text

فَصْلٌ فِي الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ: كُلُّ نِسْبَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ إِمَّا وَاجِبَةٌ إِنْ كَانَتْ ضَرُورِيَّةَ التَّحَقُّقِ، أَوْ مُمْتَنِعَةٌ إِنْ كَانَتْ ضَرُورِيَّةَ اللَّاتَحَقُّقِ، أَوْ مُمْكِنَةٌ إِنْ لَمْ تَكُنْ ضَرُورِيَّةَ التَّحَقُّقِ وَاللَّاتَحَقُّقِ. وَتِلْكَ الْكَيْفِيَّاتُ إِذَا كَانَتْ لِلْإِيجَابِ الْمَوَادُّ. قَالَ الشَّيْخُ: اعْلَمْ أَنَّ حَالَ الْمَحْمُولِ فِي نَفْسِهِ عِنْدَ الْمَوْضُوعِ لَا الَّتِي تَجِبُ بِبَيَانِنَا وَتَصْرِيحِنَا بِالْفِعْلِ أَنَّهُ كَيْفَ هِيَ لَهُ، وَلَا الَّتِي تَكُونُ فِي كُلِّ نِسْبَةٍ، بَلِ الْحَالُ الَّتِي لِلْمَحْمُولِ عِنْدَ الْمَوْضُوعِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ الْإِيجَابِيَّةِ مِنْ دَوَامِ صِدْقٍ أَوْ كَذِبٍ أَوْ لَا دَوَامِهِمَا يُسَمَّى «مَادَّةً». فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْحَالُ هُوَ أَنَّ الْمَحْمُولَ يَدُومُ وَيَجِبُ صِدْقُ إِيجَابِهِ، فَيُسَمَّى مَادَّةَ الْوُجُوبِ، كَحَالِ الْحَيَوَانِ عِنْدَ الْإِنْسَانِ، أَوْ يَدُومُ وَيَجِبُ كَذِبُ إِيجَابِهِ، وَيُسَمَّى مَادَّةَ الِامْتِنَاعِ كَحَالِ الْحَجَرِ عِنْدَ الْإِنْسَانِ، أَوْ لَا يَدُومُ وَلَا يَجِبُ مَادَّةَ الْإِمْكَانِ، كَحَالِ الْكِتَابَةِ عِنْدَ الْإِنْسَانِ. وَهَذِهِ الْحَالُ لَا تَخْتَلِفُ فِي الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ؛ فَإِنَّ السَّالِبَةَ تُوجَدُ لِمَحْمُولِهَا هَذِهِ الْحَالَ بِعَيْنِهَا؛ فَإِنَّ مَحْمُولَهَا يَكُونُ مُسْتَحِقًّا عِنْدَ الْإِيجَابِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُوجِبْ. وَقَدْ زَلَّ صَاحِبُ «الْأُفُقِ الْمُبِينِ»؛ حَيْثُ قَالَ: إِنَّ النِّسْبَةَ السَّلْبِيَّةَ قَطْعُ النِّسْبَةِ، وَالْقَطْعُ لَيْسَ لَهُ كَيْفِيَّةٌ فِي الْوَاقِعِ، بَلِ الْكَيْفِيَّاتُ فِي السَّالِبَةِ لِلْمَسْلُوبِ، فَفِي السَّالِبَةِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ لَيْسَ النِّسْبَةُ ضَرُورِيَّةً، بَلْ رَفْعُ النِّسْبَةِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ، وَقَالَ: فَعَلَى هَذَا لَا يَجِبُ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ اخْتِلَافُ الْجِهَةِ، بَلْ نَقِيضُ كُلِّ مُوجَبَةٍ مِثْلُهَا. فَصْلٌ فِي الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ وَيُرَدُّ عَلَيْهِ وُرُودًا ظَاهِرًا أَنَّ بَعْدَ تَسْلِيمِ كَوْنِ السَّلْبِ قَطْعًا لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ الْقَطْعَ لَيْسَ لَهُ كَيْفِيَّةٌ، كَيْفَ وَلَيْسَ مَفْهُومُ مَا خَالِيًا عَنِ الْمَوَادِّ الثَّلَاثِ. قَالَ الشَّيْخُ فِي الْإِشَارَاتِ: وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ السَّالِبَةَ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ غَيْرُ سَالِبَةِ الضَّرُورَةِ، وَالسَّالِبَةَ الْمُمْكِنَةَ غَيْرُ سَالِبَةِ الْإِمْكَانِ، وَالسَّالِبَةَ الْوُجُودِيَّةَ الَّتِي بِلَا دَوَامٍ غَيْرُ سَالِبَةِ الْوُجُودِ بِلَا دَوَامٍ، فَالْمُتَّبَعُ الْحَقُّ أَنَّ كُلَّ نِسْبَةٍ مُتَكَيِّفَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ بِإِحْدَى الثَّلَاثِ، لَكِنِ اصْطِلَاحُهُمْ عَلَى تَسْمِيَتِهَا إِذَا كَانَتْ لِلْإِيجَابِ مَوَادَّ. هَذَا، وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ بِالصَّوَابِ. (وَالدَّالُّ عَلَيْهَا)، أَيْ عَلَى تِلْكَ الْكَيْفِيَّاتِ، سَوَاءً كَانَتْ لِلْإِيجَابِ أَوِ السَّلْبِ (الْجِهَةُ، وَمَا اشْتَمَلَتْ عَلَيْهَا)، أَيْ عَلَى الْجِهَةِ (يُسَمَّى مُوَجَّهَةً). وَرُبَاعِيَّةٌ بَسِيطَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ حَقِيقَتُهَا إِيجَابًا فَقَطْ أَوْ سَلْبًا فَقَطْ، وَمُرَكَّبَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ مُلْتَئِمَةً مِنْهُمَا، أَيْ مِنَ الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ، (وَالْعِبْرَةُ فِي التَّسْمِيَةِ لِلْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ)، فَإِنْ كَانَ مُوجِبًا سُمِّيَتْ مُوجَبَةً وَإِلَّا سَالِبَةً، (وَإِلَّا) يَشْتَمِلُ عَلَيْهَا (فَمُطْلَقَةٌ) وَمُهْمَلَةٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْجِهَةُ، (وَهِيَ)، أَيِ الْجِهَةُ (إِنْ وَافَقَتِ الْمَادَّةَ)، بِأَنْ تَكُونَ عَيْنًا لَهَا أَوْ مُلَازِمَةً لَهَا وَلَا تَكُونَ مُنَافِيَةً لَهَا، كَمَا فِي قَوْلِنَا «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ بِالْوُجُوبِ»، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ بِالْوُجُوبِ»، (صَدَقَتِ الْقَضِيَّةُ، وَإِلَّا) تُوَافِقْ كَذَبَتْ. وَالتَّحْقِيقُ أَنَّ الْمَوَادَّ الْحِكَمِيَّةَ هِيَ الْجِهَاتُ الْمَنْطِقِيَّةُ بِحَسَبِ الْمَفْهُومِ، إِلَّا أَنَّ فِي الْمَنْطِقِ يُعْتَبَرُ عُمُومًا بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى كُلِّ قَضِيَّةٍ، وَفِي الْحِكْمَةِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى....
Section on modal propositions: Every relation in reality is either necessary if it is necessarily actualized, or impossible if it is necessarily not actualized, or possible if it is neither necessarily actualized nor necessarily not actualized. And those qualities, when they are for affirmation, are the 'materials'. The Shaykh said: Know that the state of the predicate in itself with respect to the subject—not that which is necessitated by our statement and explicit declaration of how it is for it, nor that which exists in every relation—but the state that the predicate has with respect to the subject in the affirmative relation of either constant truth, constant falsehood, or non-constancy of both, is called 'matter'. Either the state is that the predicate persists and its affirmative is necessarily true, so it is called the matter of necessity, like the state of 'animal' with respect to 'human'; or it persists and its affirmative is necessarily false, and it is called the matter of impossibility, like the state of 'stone' with respect to 'human'; or it neither persists nor is necessary, the matter of possibility, like the state of 'writing' with respect to 'human'. And this state does not differ in affirmation and negation; for the negative proposition has for its predicate exactly this state; for its predicate would be deserving of it in affirmation, even if it does not affirm. And the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' erred when he said: The negative relation is a cutting of the relation, and cutting has no quality in reality; rather, the qualities in the negative belong to the negated; so in the necessary negative, the relation is not necessary, but rather the removal of a necessary relation. He said: Thus, in contradiction, difference of modality is not required; rather, the contradictory of every affirmative is its like. Section on modal propositions: And it is refuted in an obvious refutation that even if we grant that negation is a cutting, we do not grant that the cutting has no quality; how so, when there is no concept empty of the three materials? The Shaykh said in al-Isharat: Know that the necessary negative is not the same as the negation of necessity, and the possible negative is not the same as the negation of possibility, and the existential negative that is without duration is not the same as the negation of existence without duration. The correct view is that every relation is qualified in reality by one of the three, but their convention is to call them 'materials' when they are for affirmation. This is what is correct, and God knows best. That which indicates them—that is, those qualities, whether for affirmation or negation—is the 'modality', and that which contains it—that is, contains the modality—is called a 'modal proposition'. And it is fourfold: simple if its essence is only affirmation or only negation, and compound if it is composed of both, i.e., of affirmation and negation. The criterion in naming is the first part; if it is affirmative, it is called affirmative, otherwise negative. If it does not contain a modality, then it is absolute and unrestricted with respect to modality. And the modality, if it agrees with the matter—by being identical to it or concomitant with it and not contrary to it, as in our statement 'Every human is necessarily an animal' and 'No human is necessarily a stone'—the proposition is true; otherwise, if it does not agree, it is false. The verification is that the philosophical materials are the logical modalities in concept, except that in logic they are considered generally with respect to every proposition, and in philosophy with respect to...
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and expository section on modal propositions. The author begins by stating the three basic modalities: necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), and possible (mumkin), defined in terms of necessary realization in reality (nafs al-amr). These qualities, when they belong to affirmative propositions, are called 'materials' (mawadd). The author then expands on the concept of 'matter' (madda), explaining that it is the inherent state of the predicate relative to the subject in an affirmative proposition, independent of our explicit expression. This state is either constant truth (necessity), constant falsehood (impossibility), or neither (possibility). Examples from natural kinds illustrate: 'animal' is necessarily true of 'human'; 'stone' is necessarily false; 'writing' is possible. Crucially, the author asserts that the same material state applies equally to negative propositions; the predicate of a negative would have the same material status if affirmed. A criticism is then directed at the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin', who wrongly claimed that negative propositions lack a modality because negation is a 'cutting' of the relation. The response rejects this: every concept partakes in the three materials. The passage then cites al-Isharat to distinguish between different types of negative modalities (e.g., necessary negative vs. negation of necessity). The correct view is that every relation is qualified by one of the three. The term 'jiha' (modality) is introduced as the indicator of these qualities, and a proposition containing a modality is called 'muwajjaha'. Modal propositions are divided into simple (basita) and compound (murakkaba) based on whether their nature is purely affirmative or negative, or mixed. The naming follows the first component. Propositions without a modality are 'mutlaqa' (absolute). Finally, a truth condition is given: a modal proposition is true if the expressed modality agrees with the actual material — either identical or concomitant, not contrary. Examples from fiqh-like contexts illustrate. The passage concludes with a note that philosophical materials (mawadd hikmiyya) are conceptually the same as logical modalities (jiha mantiqiyya), but logic considers them generally for all propositions while philosophy considers them specifically.

Key Takeaway
A modal proposition is true only when the expressed modality matches the actual material; negative propositions have modalities and are not exempt from this rule.

Definition of Jiha and Types of Modal Propositions
Author defines al-jiha and classifies modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the modality (jiha) in a proposition and classify it as simple or composite.

Core Idea
Modality indicates the quality of a proposition; truth requires agreement with matter.

In this passage, the author defines al-jiha (modality) and explains how modal propositions are classified. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the truth of modal statements in Islamic logic, especially in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality (jiha) in a proposition
Look for words indicating necessity, possibility, impossibility, or other qualifications. These are modalities that color the relation between subject and predicate.
2
Determine if the proposition contains a modality
If the proposition includes a modality, it is called muwajjaha (modal); if not, it is mutlaqa (absolute).
3
Classify the modality as simple or composite
Simple (basita) if the modality is purely affirmative (e.g., necessarily) or purely negative (e.g., necessarily not). Composite (murakkaba) if it includes both affirmation and negation (e.g., possible).
4
Verify truth condition: modality must agree with matter
The modality must be identical to or concomitant with the actual state of affairs (madda) and not contrary. Example: 'Every human is necessarily an animal' is true because the necessity agrees with the essential property.

Source Text

ويرد عليه ورودا ظاهرا أن بعد تسليم كون السلب قطعا لا نسلم أن القطع ليس له كيفية ، كيف وليس مفهوم ما خاليا عن المواد الثلاث
قال الشيخ في الإشارات واعلم أن السالبة الضرورية غير سالبة الضرورة والسالبة الممكنة غير سالبة الإمكان، والسالبة الوجودية التي بلا دوام غير سالبة الوجود بلا دوام، فالمتبع الحق أن كل نسبة متكيفة في نفس الأمر بإحدى الثلاث، لكن اصطلاحهم على تسميتها إذا كانت للإيجاب مواد. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
(وَالدَّالُّ عَلَيْهَا)، أي على تلك الكيفيات، سواء كانت للإيجاب أو السلب (الْجِهَةُ، وَمَا اشْتَمَلَتْ عَلَيْهَا)، أي على الجهة ([يُسَمَّى مُوَجَّهَةً [ وَرُبَاعِيَّةٌ ]، بَسِيطَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ حَقِيقَتُهَا إِيجَابًا فَقَطْ أَوْ سَلْبًا فَقَطْ، وَمُرَكَّبَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ مُلْتَئِمَةً مِنْهُمَا، أي من الإيجاب والسلب، (وَالْعِبْرَةُ فِي التَّسْمِيَةِ لِلْجُزْء الْأَوَّلِ)، فإن كان موجبا سميت موجبة وإلا سالبة، (وَإِلَّا) يشتمل عليها (فَمُطْلَقَةٌ) وَمُهْمَلَةٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْجِهَةُ، وَهِيَ، أي الجهة (إِنْ وَافَقَتِ الْمَادَّةَ)، بأن تكون عينا لها أو ملازمة لها ولا تكون منافية لها، كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان بالوجوب»، ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالوجوب»، (صَدَقَتِ الْقَضِيَّةُ، وَإِلَّا تُوافِقُ كَذَبَتْ)
Chapter on modal propositions. And it is objected against it in an apparent way that after conceding that negation is absolute, we do not concede that the absolute has no quality; how is it not a concept devoid of the three matters? The Shaykh said in al-Isharat: Know that the necessary negative is not the negative of necessity, and the possible negative is not the negative of possibility, and the existential negative that is without perpetuity is not the negative of existence without perpetuity. So the correct view is that every relation is qualified in reality by one of the three, but their convention is to call them 'matters' when they are for affirmation. This, and God knows best what is correct. (And that which indicates them), i.e., those qualities, whether for affirmation or negation, (is al-jiha [modality], and what contains it), i.e., the modality, (is called al-muwajjaha [modalized], and [quadripartite?]), simple if its reality is only affirmation or only negation, and composite if it is composed of both, i.e., affirmation and negation. (And consideration in naming is given to the first part): if it is affirmative, it is called affirmative; otherwise negative. (And if it) does not contain it (i.e., the modality), (then it is mutlaqa [absolute]) and unqualified with respect to modality. And it, i.e., the modality, (if it agrees with the matter), by being identical to it or concomitant with it and not contrary to it, as in our statement 'Every human is necessarily an animal' and 'Nothing of human is necessarily a stone', (the proposition is true; and if it does not) agree, (it is false).
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage defines the key terms for modal propositions. It opens with a brief objection and a quotation from Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) clarifying that negative modal propositions are not simply the negation of the modality; e.g., 'necessary negative' is not the same as 'negative of necessity'. The author then defines al-jiha (modality) as that which indicates the quality of a proposition's matter, whether affirmative or negative. A proposition containing a modality is called muwajjaha. The text then classifies modal propositions into simple (basita) if the modality is purely affirmative or purely negative, and composite (murakkaba) if the modality is composed of both affirmation and negation. The naming of the proposition follows the first part of the modality: if the first part is affirmative, the proposition is called affirmative; otherwise negative. If a proposition does not contain a modality, it is called mutlaqa (absolute) or muhmala (unqualified) with respect to modality. Finally, the passage explains the condition for truth: the modality must agree with the matter (madda), either by being identical to it or concomitant with it and not contrary. Examples are given: 'Every human is necessarily an animal' (the modality 'necessarily' agrees with the matter of essential property) and 'Nothing of human is necessarily a stone' (the negation of necessity agrees with the impossibility). If the modality disagrees with the matter, the proposition is false.

Key Takeaway
Modality indicates the quality of a proposition; simple modalities are purely affirmative or negative, while composite modalities combine both; a modal proposition is true if its modality agrees with its matter.

Distinguishing Logical and Metaphysical Necessity: Objection and Answer
Author presents and refutes an objection against the distinction between logical and metaphysical necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of necessity distinguished in Islamic logic and apply this distinction to refute a common objection against modal propositions.

Core Idea
Logical necessity for another is not the same as metaphysical necessity in itself.

This lesson walks you through a classic dialectical exchange in which an objection against the distinction between logical and metaphysical modalities is presented and refuted. By understanding the objection and the precise answer, you will gain clarity on how Islamic logicians carefully differentiate between necessity that applies to created essences and necessity that belongs to God alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's conditional claim
Locate the conditional statement: 'if logical and metaphysical necessity were the same, then the necessary consequences of an essence would be necessary in themselves.' This is the core of the attack.
The objector assumes that if the two modalities are identical, then anything that is logically necessary would also be metaphysically necessary.
2
Extract the absurd consequence
The objector argues that consequences like evenness (for four) would then be necessary in themselves, which is absurd because they depend on the essence. Recognize why this is unacceptable in Islamic metaphysics.
Only God is necessary in Himself; any created attribute cannot share that status.
3
Analyze the answer's distinction
The answer introduces a distinction between 'necessity of existence in itself' (وجوب الوجود في نفسه) and 'necessity of provenness for another' (وجوب الثبوت لغيره). Note how this is presented as the key move.
The first type is exclusive to God; the second applies to logical consequences of essences.
4
Apply the distinction to the objection
Test both interpretations: if the objector means 'necessity of existence in itself', the conditional is denied (the antecedent does not entail that). If he means 'necessity for another', the consequence is not absurd and the objection fails.
5
Summarize the logical structure
The objection is a modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, so not P. The answer shows that Q is ambiguous; the modus tollens fails because the minor premise (not Q) is not clearly false in the intended sense.
This illustrates a sophisticated refutation by disambiguation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قضية محمولها الوجود فقط . (وَقِيلَ) في «المواقف» (إِنَّهَا غَيْرُهَا ، وَإِلَّا لَكَانَتْ لَوَازِمُ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَاجِبَةً لِذَاتِهَا)، والتالي باطل فالمقدم مثله. بيان الملازمة أن اللوازم ـ كالزوجية - واجبة بالوجوب المنطقي، ولو كان عين الحكمي لكانت واجبة لذاتها . (وَالْجَوَابُ : أَنَّهُ فُرِّقَ بَيْنَ وُجُوبِ الْوُجُودِ فِي نَفْسِهِ)؛ فإنه مختص بالباري تعالى، (وَبَيْنَ وُجُوبِ النُّبُوتِ لِغَيْرِهِ) ؛ فإنه يوجد في غيره تعالى أيضا، (وَالْأَوَّلُ مُحَالٌ) في اللوازم (غَيْرُ لَازِم) ، فإن أراد ذلك فالملازمة ممنوعة، ( وَالثَّانِي لَازِمٌ) في لوازم الماهية غَيْرُ مُحَالٍ، فإن أراد ذلك فالملازمة مسلمة ، وبطلان التالي ممنوع.
A proposition whose predicate is existence only. And it was said in al-Mawaqif: 'It is other than it (i.e., the modal concept), otherwise the necessary consequences of the essence would be necessary in themselves.' The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. Explanation of the implication: the consequences—like evenness—are necessary by logical necessity, and if it (the logical necessity) were the same as the metaphysical, they would be necessary in themselves. The answer: that a distinction is made between the necessity of existence in itself—for it is specific to the Exalted Creator—and the necessity of provenness for another—for it exists in others besides Him. The first is impossible in the consequences and is not necessary; so if He intended that, the implication is denied. The second is necessary in the consequences of the essence without being impossible; so if He intended that, the implication is granted, and the falsity of the consequent is denied.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange. The author presents an objection from the Mawaqif against those who distinguish between the logical modal concept (like 'necessary') and the metaphysical one (like 'existence necessary in itself'). The objection argues that if they were the same, then accidental attributes that necessarily follow from an essence (like evenness following from four) would be necessary in themselves, which is absurd. The answer distinguishes between two types of necessity: (1) necessity of existence in itself, which is exclusive to God, and (2) necessity of provenness for another, which applies to such consequences. The first type is impossible for consequences; the second type is necessary. Therefore, if the objection intended the first, it does not follow; if the second, the falsity is not proven. Thus the objection is refuted.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between necessity in itself (God) and necessity for another (logical) is essential for preserving the uniqueness of God's necessary existence while allowing logical necessity for other beings.

Ancient and Modern Definitions of Modal Matter
Author distinguishes ancient and modern views on modal propositions and supports the matn with Ibn Sina's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the ancient and modern definitions of modal matter and identify the three modalities according to Ibn Sina's definition.

Core Idea
The dispute is not about the number of modalities, but about what the modality indicates.

This passage introduces two competing views on the nature of modal propositions in Islamic logic. The ancients, including Ibn Sina, hold that the matter of a proposition is one of three fixed qualities: necessity, impossibility, or possibility. The moderns, however, expand the matter to include any quality of the relation, such as permanence or temporality, leading to infinite modalities. The author sides with the ancients and supports their view with a direct quote from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, which defines modality as the relation of the predicate to the subject indicating necessity or non-necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two schools
Locate where the author introduces the views of the ancients (القدماء) and the moderns (المحدثين). The ancients are associated with three specific modal qualities, while the moderns define matter as any quality of the relation.
2
Understand the modern definition
Read how the moderns define 'matter' as every quality belonging to the relation, including non-finite qualities like permanence and temporality. This results in infinite modal propositions.
The moderns' view is presented as a contrast to the earlier discussion.
3
Extract the ancient definition from Ibn Sina
Focus on the quote from al-Shifa. Ibn Sina states that modality is a word indicating the relation of the predicate to the subject, and that it is either a relation of necessity (emphasis) or non-necessity (permissibility). He then lists three modalities: necessary, impossible, and possible.
The three modalities correspond to permanent existence, permanent non-existence, and neither.
4
Resolve the dispute
The author clarifies that the dispute is not merely about the number of modal propositions, but about the definition of matter itself. The ancients restrict modality to three, while the moderns consider it open-ended. The author supports the ancient view with the authority of Ibn Sina.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
([ هَذَا ] (۱) كله عَلَى رَأْيِ الْقُدَمَاءِ ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الْمُحْدَثِينَ فَالْمَادَّةُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ كُلِّ كَيْفِيَّةٍ كَانَتْ لِلنَّسْبَةِ)، أية نسبة كانت إيجابية أو سلبية، (كَدَوام وَتَوْقِيتٍ إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ من الكيفيات الغير المتناهية، (وَمِنْ ثَمَّ)، أي من أجل أن المادة عامة (كَانَتِ الْمُوَجَّهَاتُ غَيْرَ مُتَنَاهِيَةِ) ، لصيرورة الجهات غير متناهية .
وهذا كالنص على أن الجهات عند القدماء هي الكيفيات الثلاثة، والموجهة ما اشتملت على إحداىها ، لا كما زعم الأكثرون أن النزاع إنما هو المادة دون الجهة، فالجهة عند الكل الدال على الكيفية، أية كيفية كانت . ويظن هذا العبد - غفر الله له - الحق ما ذكره المصنف، ويؤيده كلام الشيخ ؛ حيث قال في «الشفاء»: الجهة لفظ يدل على النسبة التي للمحمول عند الموضوع، فتعين أنها نسبة ضرورة أو لا ضرورة فيدل على تأكد أو جواز، وقال: الجهات ثلاث ، واحدة تدل على استحقاق دوام الوجود، وهي الواجبة، وأخرى تدل على دوام استحقاق اللاوجود ، وهي الممتنعة، وأخرى تدل على أنه لا استحقاق دوام الوجود واللاوجود، وهي الجهة الممكنة.
([ This ] all is according to the view of the ancients. But according to the school of the moderns, the matter (madda) is an expression for every quality that belongs to the relation—any relation whether affirmative or negative—such as permanence, temporality, and other infinite qualities. And therefore—because the matter is general—the modal propositions are infinite, due to the modalities becoming infinite. And this is like a text that the modalities according to the ancients are the three qualities, and a modal proposition is one that includes one of them, not as the majority claimed that the dispute is about the matter, not the modality. For the modality according to all is that which indicates the quality, any quality it may be. And this servant—may God forgive him—thinks the truth is what the author mentioned, and it is supported by the words of the Shaykh, where he said in al-Shifa: 'The modality is a word that indicates the relation that the predicate has to the subject. So it is determined that it is a relation of necessity or non-necessity, so it indicates emphasis or permissibility.' And he said: 'The modalities are three: one indicates the entitlement of permanence of existence, which is the necessary; another indicates the entitlement of permanence of non-existence, which is the impossible; and another indicates that there is no entitlement of permanence of existence and non-existence, which is the possible modality.'
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of modal propositions (موجهات) according to two schools. The author first states that the preceding discussion was based on the ancients' view, which holds that the matter (مادة) of a proposition is one of three fixed qualities: necessity, impossibility, or possibility. In contrast, the moderns (محدثون) consider the matter to be any quality of the relation, such as permanence or temporality, leading to infinite modalities. The author then clarifies that the dispute is not merely about the matter but about the nature of modality itself, quoting Ibn Sina from al-Shifa to support the claim that modalities are three, defined as the relation of the predicate to the subject, indicating necessity (emphasis) or non-necessity (permissibility). The modalities are further defined as entitlement to permanent existence (necessary), permanent non-existence (impossible), or neither (possible).

Key Takeaway
The ancient view, supported by Ibn Sina, defines modal matter as one of three fixed modalities (necessary, impossible, possible), while the moderns expand it to any quality of the relation, leading to infinite modalities.

Major Types of Modal Propositions and Their Defining Conditions
Classification of modal proposition sub-types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the main types of modal propositions (necessary, conditioned, temporal, distributed, perpetual, absolute, possible) by identifying the specific condition that defines each type.

Core Idea
Modal propositions are classified by the condition governing the relation between subject and predicate.

Modal propositions (al-muwajjihāt) are not all the same — they differ in the strength and duration of the judgment. This passage from the commentary on al-Sullam lays out a detailed taxonomy: from absolute necessity to mere possibility, and from perpetual to merely factual. Understanding these distinctions is essential for constructing and evaluating syllogisms in Islamic logic, especially in kalam arguments about divine attributes and the temporal origination of the world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall purpose of the passage
Read the opening lines to see that the author is classifying modal propositions by the condition of the judgment. Note the reference to the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) and al-Isharat for context.
2
Locate the definition of absolute necessary
Find where the judgment is 'impossibility of separation absolutely' (استحالة انفكاك النسبة مطلقا). This defines the absolute necessary (ضرورية مطلقة). Contrast with eternal necessary.
3
Separate the 'as long as the description exists' types
Two types share the condition 'as long as the description exists' (ما دام الوصف): general conditioned (مشروطة عامة) and general 'urfiyya (عرفية عامة). Note the distinction in how the description is understood (condition vs container).
4
Distinguish temporal and perpetual modals
Identify the definitions for temporal (وقتية مطلقة), distributed (منتشرة مطلقة), and perpetual (دائمة مطلقة). Each has a specific condition regarding time or essence.
5
Understand the possibility spectrum
The passage defines general possible (ممكنة عامة) and special possible (ممكنة خاصة), then expands to true possibility and future possibility. Trace how each is built by negating necessity.

وأيضا قد أدرج الشيخ العرفية العامة في المطلقة؛ حيث قال: فالسالبة الكلية من المطلقة إذا أخذت ما يفهم في المتعارف، من قول القائل «لا من (د) (ب)»، المستعملة وهي في العلوم ؛ فإنها تنعكس، وإن أخذت على ما شيء يجب في نفس الأمر فإنها لا تنعكس. ثم فسر المنعكسة بما حاصله العرفي العام . وهي وقال في «الإشارات»: وأما سائر ما فيه شرط الضرورة، والذي هو دائم من غير ضرورة فهو أصناف المطلق الغير الضروري، وقد ذكر في التعليم الأول القضايا إما مطلقة أو ضرورية أو ممكنة. هذا ، والله أعلم بمراد عباده. (فَهِيَ)، إما راجع إلى الموجهة، فهو تقسيم على قول المتأخرين، أو إلى القضية، فيكون تقسيما على كلا المذهبين (إنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِاسْتِحَالَةِ انْفِكَاكِ النِّسْبَةِ مُطْلَقًا)، أي من غير تقييد بالوصف والوقت (فَضَرُورِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ) ، غير الضرورية الأزلية، وهي ما حكم فيها بضرورة النسبة أزلا وأبدا، (أَوْ مَا دَامَ الْوَصْفُ) ، وهو يمكن على ثلاثة أوجه الأول ما يكون الوصف فيه شرطا، الثاني ما يكون الوصف فيه علة موجبة، الثالث ما يكون زمانه ظرفها، والمعتبر الأول (فَمَشْرُوطَةٌ عَامَّةٌ، أَوْ هو طَةٌ عَامَّةٌ، أَوْ فِي وَقْتٍ مُعَيَّنِ فَوَقْتِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ، أَوْ) في وقت غَيْرِ مُعَيَّنٍ فَمُنْتَشِرَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ ، أَوْ بِعَدَمِ انْفِكَاكِهَا مُطْلَقًا) من التقييد بوصف ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة (فَدَائِمَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ)، وهي غير الدائمة الأزلية، وهي حكم فيها بعدم انفكاك النسبة أزلا وأبدا. ما أَوْ مَا دَامَ الْوَصْفُ فَعُرْفِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ ، أَوْ بِفِعْلِيَّتِهَا)، أي كون النسبة واقعة في نفس الأمر (فَمُطْلَقَةٌ عَامَّةٌ ، أَوْ بِعَدَمِ اسْتِحَالَتِهَا) ، ويلزمه سلب ضرورة الجانب المخالف (فَمُمْكِنَةٌ عَامَّةٌ ، أَوْ بِعَدَمِ اسْتِحَالَةِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ)، ويلزمه سلب الضرورة عن الطرفين (فَمُمْكِنَةٌ خَاصَّةٌ ، وَلَا فَرْقَ بَيْنَ الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ فِيهَا إِلَّا فِي اللَّفْظِ). اعلم أن الإمكان يجيئ على معان سلب ضرورة الطرف المقابل، وهو العام والعامي، فيشمل الواجب والضروري بشرط الوصف، وسلب ضرورة الطرفين، وهو الخاص والخاصي، وهو لا يشمل الواجب، بل الضروري بشرط الوصف، وسلب الضرورة مطلقا، ذاتية كانت أو وصفية، وقتية كانت أو منتشرة، وهو الإمكان الحق، وسلب الضرورة الذاتية من الطرفين في المستقبل، وهو الإمكان الاستقبالي. ثم إن مقابل كل ضرورة إمكان هو نقيضها، وسيرد عليك تفصيل منه . (وَقَدِ اعْتُبِرَ تَقْيِيدُ الْعَامَّتَيْنِ وَالْوَقْتِيَّتَيْنِ الْمُطْلَقَتَيْنِ بِاللَّادَوَامِ الذَّاتِي، [فَتُسَمَّى] الْمَشْرُوطَةَ الْخَاصَّةَ وَالْعُرْفِيَّةَ الْخَاصَّةَ وَالْوَقْتِيَّةَ وَالْمُنْتَشِرَةَ) ، لف ونشر مرتب، (و) قد اعْتبر تَفْسِيدُ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْعَامَّةِ بِاللَّاضَرُورَةِ أَوِ اللَّادَوَامِ الذَّاتِيَّيْن، فَتُسَمَّى الْوُجُودِيَّةَ اللَّاضَرُورِيَّةَ وَالْوُجُودِيَّةَ اللَّادَائِمَةَ، وَهِيَ الْمُطْلَقَةُ الْإِسْكَنْدَرِيَّةُ؛ لأن الإسكندر فهم من المطلقة في كلام المعلم الأول هذه القضية.
And also the Shaykh included the general 'urfiyya in the absolute; for he said: The universal negative of the absolute, if taken as understood in common usage, from the statement 'No D is B', which is used in the sciences, then it converts; and if taken as what must be in actual reality, then it does not convert. Then he interpreted the convertible as being in effect the general 'urfiyya. And he said in al-Isharat: As for the rest that contains a condition of necessity, and that which is perpetual without necessity, they are types of non-necessary absolute. And it was mentioned in the First Teaching that propositions are either absolute, necessary, or possible. This, and Allah knows best the intended meaning of His servants. (So they) refer either to the modal proposition, then it is a division according to the later scholars, or to the proposition, then it is a division in both schools. If the judgment in it is the impossibility of the separation of the relation absolutely, i.e., without restriction by description or time, then it is an absolute necessary, not the eternal necessary, and it is that which is judged to have necessity of the relation eternally and forever. Or as long as the description exists — and this can be in three ways: first, the description is a condition; second, the description is a cause; third, its time is a container; and the first is considered — then it is a general conditioned. Or in a specific time, then it is an absolute temporal. Or in an unspecified time, then it is an absolute distributed. Or by the non-separation of it absolutely, i.e., without restriction by a description as long as the essence of the subject exists, then it is an absolute perpetual, which is not the eternal perpetual, and it is that which is judged to have non-separation of the relation eternally and forever. Or as long as the description exists, then it is a general 'urfiyya. Or by its actuality, i.e., the relation occurring in actual reality, then it is a general absolute. Or by the non-impossibility of it, which necessitates the negation of the necessity of the opposite side, then it is a general possible. Or by the non-impossibility of both sides, which necessitates the negation of necessity from both sides, then it is a special possible. And there is no difference between affirmation and negation in it except in wording. Know that possibility comes in meanings: negation of necessity of the opposite side, which is the general and the 'ammi, and it includes the obligatory and the necessary by condition of description; and negation of necessity of both sides, which is the special and the khassi, and it does not include the obligatory but includes the necessary by condition of description; and negation of necessity absolutely, whether essential or descriptive, temporal or distributed, which is the true possibility; and negation of essential necessity from both sides in the future, which is the future possibility. Then for every necessity, its corresponding possibility is its contradictory. And detailed discussion will come to you. (And the restriction of the two general and the two absolute temporal/distributed propositions by self non-perpetuity has been considered, [so they are called] the special conditioned, special 'urfiyya, temporal, and distributed) — an ordered wrapping and unwrapping. And the restriction of the general absolute by the negation of self-necessity or self-non-perpetuity has been considered, so they are called the existenceal non-necessary and the existenceal non-perpetual, and that is the Alexandrian absolute, because Alexander understood from 'the absolute' in the words of the First Teacher this proposition.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of modal propositions (al-muwajjihaat). The commentator begins by noting that the author (al-Sullam) included the general 'urfiyya under the absolute, citing a passage on conversion of universal negatives. Then he quotes from al-Isharat that propositions beyond necessity and perpetuity are types of non-necessary absolute. The main classification proceeds: if the judgment is that the relation cannot be separated absolutely (without restriction), it is an absolute necessary (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa), distinct from the eternal necessary. If the judgment is 'as long as the description exists', it is a general conditioned (mashrūṭa ‘āmma), with the description being a condition, cause, or container (the first is meant). If the relation holds in a specific time, it is an absolute temporal (waqtiyya muṭlaqa); in an unspecified time, it is an absolute distributed (muntashira muṭlaqa). If it holds without separation absolutely while the subject's essence exists, it is an absolute perpetual (dā’ima muṭlaqa), distinct from the eternal perpetual. 'As long as the description exists' yields a general 'urfiyya. 'By its actuality' yields a general absolute (muṭlaqa ‘āmma). 'By its non-impossibility' (i.e., negation of the opposite's necessity) yields a general possible (mumkina ‘āmma). 'By the non-impossibility of both sides' (negation of necessity from both) yields a special possible (mumkina khāṣṣa), with no difference between affirmation and negation except wording. The commentator then elaborates on possibility: (1) negation of necessity of the opposite side (general possibility) includes the necessary with a description; (2) negation of necessity of both sides (special possibility) excludes the necessary but includes the necessary with description; (3) negation of necessity absolutely (true possibility); (4) negation of essential necessity in the future (future possibility). Each necessity has a corresponding possibility as its contradictory. Finally, the restriction of the two general and two absolute temporal/distributed propositions by self-non-perpetuity yields the special conditioned, special 'urfiyya, temporal, and distributed. Restriction of the general absolute by negation of self-necessity or self-non-perpetuity yields the existential non-necessary and non-perpetual (Alexandrian absolute), attributed to Alexander's understanding of Aristotle's 'absolute'. All examples here draw from the logic tradition's own technical vocabulary.

The Four Types of Possibility in Modal Logic
Author classifies types of possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of possibility (الإمكان العام, الإمكان الخاص, الإمكان الحق, الإمكان الاستقبالي) based on the scope of necessity negated.

Core Idea
Possibility is graded: which necessity is negated determines the type.

This passage from Bahr al-Ulum classifies al-imkān (possibility) into four distinct types based on what kind of necessity (ḍarūra) is negated. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly analysing modal propositions in Islamic logic, especially when defining terms like 'necessary conditional on the description' and 'absolute general'. This lesson will guide you through the definitions of the four types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four definitions of possibility
Locate the four phrases: العام والعامي, الخاص والخاصي, الإمكان الحق, الإمكان الاستقبالي. Each is a distinct meaning of possibility.
2
Determine the scope of negation for each
For each type, note which necessity is negated: opposite side (for general), both sides (for special), any necessity (for true), future essential necessity (for future).
The negation is of necessity (ḍarūra), not of existence.
3
Note what each type includes
General possibility includes the necessary and the necessary conditional on the description. Special possibility includes only the necessary conditional on the description. True and future possibilities exclude the necessary.
4
Recognize the relation to necessity
Each type of possibility is the contradictory (نقيض) of the corresponding necessity. This will be detailed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الموجهات
عن الطرفين (فَمُمْكِنَةٌ خَاصَّةٌ ، وَلَا فَرْقَ بَيْنَ الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ فِيهَا إِلَّا فِي اللَّفْظِ). اعلم أن الإمكان يجيئ على معان سلب ضرورة الطرف المقابل، وهو العام والعامي، فيشمل الواجب والضروري بشرط الوصف، وسلب ضرورة الطرفين، وهو الخاص والخاصي، وهو لا يشمل الواجب، بل الضروري بشرط الوصف، وسلب الضرورة مطلقا، ذاتية كانت أو وصفية، وقتية كانت أو منتشرة، وهو الإمكان الحق، وسلب الضرورة الذاتية من الطرفين في المستقبل، وهو الإمكان الاستقبالي. ثم إن مقابل كل ضرورة إمكان هو نقيضها، وسيرد عليك تفصيل منه . (وَقَدِ اعْتُبِرَ تَقْيِيدُ الْعَامَّتَيْنِ وَالْوَقْتِيَّتَيْنِ الْمُطْلَقَتَيْنِ بِاللَّادَوَامِ الذَّاتِي، [فَتُسَمَّى] الْمَشْرُوطَةَ الْخَاصَّةَ وَالْعُرْفِيَّةَ الْخَاصَّةَ وَالْوَقْتِيَّةَ وَالْمُنْتَشِرَةَ) ، لف ونشر مرتب، (و) قد اعْتبر تَفْسِيدُ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْعَامَّةِ بِاللَّاضَرُورَةِ أَوِ اللَّادَوَامِ الذَّاتِيَّيْن، فَتُسَمَّى الْوُجُودِيَّةَ اللَّاضَرُورِيَّةَ وَالْوُجُودِيَّةَ اللَّادَائِمَةَ، وَهِيَ الْمُطْلَقَةُ الْإِسْكَنْدَرِيَّةُ)؛ لأن الإسكندر فهم من المطلقة في كلام المعلم الأول هذه القضية .
Chapter on Modalities. Concerning the two ends, (then it is a special possible, and there is no difference between affirmation and negation in it except in wording). Know that possibility comes with meanings: the negation of the necessity of the opposite side, and it is the general (al-‘ām) and the general-by-way-of (al-‘āmmī), and it includes the necessary and the necessary conditional on the description; and the negation of the necessity of both sides, and it is the special (al-khāṣṣ) and the special-by-way-of (al-khāṣṣī), and it does not include the necessary, but rather the necessary conditional on the description; and the negation of necessity absolutely, whether essential or descriptive, temporal or diffuse, and it is the true possibility; and the negation of essential necessity from both sides in the future, and it is the future possibility. Then for every necessity there is a possibility which is its contradictory, and a detailed account of it will come to you. And the restriction of the two general and the two absolute temporals with non-permanence has been considered, so they are called the special conditional, the special customary, the temporal, and the diffuse, in an ordered folding and unfolding. And the restriction of the absolute general with non-necessity or non-permanence has been considered, so they are called the existential non-necessary and the existential non-permanent, and it is the Alexandrian absolute; because Alexander understood from the absolute in the words of the First Teacher this proposition.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of different types of possibility (imkan) and how they relate to modal propositions. The author begins by noting that 'special possible' (ممكنة خاصة) has no difference between affirmation and negation except in wording. Then he lists four meanings of possibility based on what necessity (darura) is negated: (1) general possibility (العام والعامي): negation of the necessity of the opposite side; includes the necessary and the necessary conditional on the description. (2) special possibility (الخاص والخاصي): negation of the necessity of both sides; includes only the necessary conditional on the description. (3) true possibility (الإمكان الحق): negation of necessity absolutely, whether essential or descriptive, temporal or diffuse. (4) future possibility (الإمكان الاستقبالي): negation of essential necessity from both sides in the future. Each necessity has a contradictory possibility. The author then explains how certain modal propositions are formed by restricting the general and temporal propositions with 'non-permanence' (اللادوام الذاتي), yielding: special conditional (المشروطة الخاصة), special customary (العرفية الخاصة), temporal (الوقتية), and diffuse (المنتشرة), in an ordered chiasmus. Finally, restricting the absolute general with non-necessity or non-permanence yields the existential non-necessary and existential non-permanent, known as the Alexandrian absolute, so named because Alexander (of Aphrodisias) understood this from the absolute proposition in Aristotle's words. The lesson is a precise breakdown of modal terminology, essential for understanding later discussions of syllogistic modalities.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام
الإمكان الخاص
الإمكان الحق
الإمكان الاستقبالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) الإمكان العام (general possibility)
B) الإمكان الخاص (special possibility)
C) الإمكان الحق (true possibility)
D) الإمكان الاستقبالي (future possibility)

Key Takeaway
The four types of possibility are defined by the scope of necessity negated, with general being the broadest and future being the most specific.

Formation of Special Modal Propositions
Author defines special modal propositions via restriction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the restriction of general and temporal propositions with non-permanence yields special modal propositions such as المشروطة الخاصة, العرفية الخاصة, الوقتية, المنتشرة, and the Alexandrian absolute (المطلقة الإسكندرية).

Core Idea
Restricting modal propositions with non-permanence creates derived modalities with precise logical force.

After defining the types of possibility, the author explains how specific modal propositions are generated by restricting the general (العامة) and temporal (الوقتية) propositions with the condition of non-permanence (اللادوام الذاتي). This yields four propositions in a chiasmus, plus the Alexandrian absolute from restricting the absolute general. This lesson will help you understand these derived forms and their importance in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base propositions being restricted
The text mentions العامتين (the two general) and الوقتيتين المطلقتين (the two absolute temporals). These are unrestricted modal propositions.
2
Identify the restriction: non-permanence (اللادوام الذاتي)
This means the predicate is not permanently true of the subject. It is a condition that weakens the modality.
3
List the four resulting propositions
The restricted forms are called المشروطة الخاصة (special conditional), العرفية الخاصة (special customary), الوقتية (temporal), and المنتشرة (diffuse). They are presented in an ordered chiasmus (لف ونشر مرتب).
4
Identify the second restriction on the absolute general
The المطلقة العامة (absolute general) is restricted by non-necessity (اللاضرورة) or non-permanence (اللادوام الذاتي), yielding الوجودية اللاضرورية (existential non-necessary) and الوجودية اللادائمة (existential non-permanent), collectively known as المطلقة الإسكندرية (Alexandrian absolute).
This is named after Alexander of Aphrodisias, who interpreted the absolute in Aristotle's words as this type.

فصل في الموجهات
عن الطرفين (فَمُمْكِنَةٌ خَاصَّةٌ ، وَلَا فَرْقَ بَيْنَ الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ فِيهَا إِلَّا فِي اللَّفْظِ). اعلم أن الإمكان يجيئ على معان سلب ضرورة الطرف المقابل، وهو العام والعامي، فيشمل الواجب والضروري بشرط الوصف، وسلب ضرورة الطرفين، وهو الخاص والخاصي، وهو لا يشمل الواجب، بل الضروري بشرط الوصف، وسلب الضرورة مطلقا، ذاتية كانت أو وصفية، وقتية كانت أو منتشرة، وهو الإمكان الحق، وسلب الضرورة الذاتية من الطرفين في المستقبل، وهو الإمكان الاستقبالي. ثم إن مقابل كل ضرورة إمكان هو نقيضها، وسيرد عليك تفصيل منه . (وَقَدِ اعْتُبِرَ تَقْيِيدُ الْعَامَّتَيْنِ وَالْوَقْتِيَّتَيْنِ الْمُطْلَقَتَيْنِ بِاللَّادَوَامِ الذَّاتِي، [فَتُسَمَّى] الْمَشْرُوطَةَ الْخَاصَّةَ وَالْعُرْفِيَّةَ الْخَاصَّةَ وَالْوَقْتِيَّةَ وَالْمُنْتَشِرَةَ) ، لف ونشر مرتب، (و) قد اعْتبر تَفْسِيدُ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْعَامَّةِ بِاللَّاضَرُورَةِ أَوِ اللَّادَوَامِ الذَّاتِيَّيْن، فَتُسَمَّى الْوُجُودِيَّةَ اللَّاضَرُورِيَّةَ وَالْوُجُودِيَّةَ اللَّادَائِمَةَ، وَهِيَ الْمُطْلَقَةُ الْإِسْكَنْدَرِيَّةُ)؛ لأن الإسكندر فهم من المطلقة في كلام المعلم الأول هذه القضية .
Chapter on Modalities. Concerning the two ends, (then it is a special possible, and there is no difference between affirmation and negation in it except in wording). Know that possibility comes with meanings: the negation of the necessity of the opposite side, and it is the general (al-‘ām) and the general-by-way-of (al-‘āmmī), and it includes the necessary and the necessary conditional on the description; and the negation of the necessity of both sides, and it is the special (al-khāṣṣ) and the special-by-way-of (al-khāṣṣī), and it does not include the necessary, but rather the necessary conditional on the description; and the negation of necessity absolutely, whether essential or descriptive, temporal or diffuse, and it is the true possibility; and the negation of essential necessity from both sides in the future, and it is the future possibility. Then for every necessity there is a possibility which is its contradictory, and a detailed account of it will come to you. And the restriction of the two general and the two absolute temporals with non-permanence has been considered, so they are called the special conditional, the special customary, the temporal, and the diffuse, in an ordered folding and unfolding. And the restriction of the absolute general with non-necessity or non-permanence has been considered, so they are called the existential non-necessary and the existential non-permanent, and it is the Alexandrian absolute; because Alexander understood from the absolute in the words of the First Teacher this proposition.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of different types of possibility (imkan) and how they relate to modal propositions. The author begins by noting that 'special possible' (ممكنة خاصة) has no difference between affirmation and negation except in wording. Then he lists four meanings of possibility based on what necessity (darura) is negated: (1) general possibility (العام والعامي): negation of the necessity of the opposite side; includes the necessary and the necessary conditional on the description. (2) special possibility (الخاص والخاصي): negation of the necessity of both sides; includes only the necessary conditional on the description. (3) true possibility (الإمكان الحق): negation of necessity absolutely, whether essential or descriptive, temporal or diffuse. (4) future possibility (الإمكان الاستقبالي): negation of essential necessity from both sides in the future. Each necessity has a contradictory possibility. The author then explains how certain modal propositions are formed by restricting the general and temporal propositions with 'non-permanence' (اللادوام الذاتي), yielding: special conditional (المشروطة الخاصة), special customary (العرفية الخاصة), temporal (الوقتية), and diffuse (المنتشرة), in an ordered chiasmus. Finally, restricting the absolute general with non-necessity or non-permanence yields the existential non-necessary and existential non-permanent, known as the Alexandrian absolute, so named because Alexander (of Aphrodisias) understood this from the absolute proposition in Aristotle's words. The lesson is a precise breakdown of modal terminology, essential for understanding later discussions of syllogistic modalities.

Key Terms
المشروطة الخاصة
العرفية الخاصة
الوقتية
المنتشرة
المطلقة الإسكندرية
الوجودية اللاضرورية
الوجودية اللادائمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, restricting the absolute general (المطلقة العامة) with non-necessity or non-permanence yields which proposition?
A) المشروطة الخاصة (special conditional)
B) العرفية الخاصة (special customary)
C) المطلقة الإسكندرية (Alexandrian absolute)
D) الوقتية (temporal)

Key Takeaway
Special modal propositions are formed by restricting general and temporal modalities with non-permanence, and the Alexandrian absolute is a key derived type.

Refining the Definition of Absolute Necessity
Author defines absolute necessary proposition, raises an objection, answers it, then critiques the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection to the definition of absolute necessity when the predicate is existence, and evaluate the response and the author's critique.

Core Idea
The definition of absolute necessity fails when the predicate is existence; necessity must be distinguished as conditional or unconditional.

This lesson examines a classic dialectical refinement of the definition of 'absolute necessary' propositions. The author presents the standard definition, then raises an objection that exposes a subtle flaw when the predicate is existence itself. After considering an answer, he critiques it, deepening our understanding of necessity in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the standard definition
The absolute necessary proposition is defined as one where the predicate is affirmed or negated with necessity as long as the subject's essence exists. Write this definition in your own words.
2
Identify the objection
The objector notes: if the predicate is existence, then according to the definition, existence is necessary for the subject as long as it exists. But that subject is a special possible (imkan khass), so necessity would not contradict possibility – an absurd consequence.
3
Examine the respondent's answer
The respondent distinguishes between 'necessity in the time of existence' (the definition's intent) and 'necessity conditional on existence' (the stronger type that applies when the predicate is existence). The objection mixes them up.
4
Analyze the author's critique
The author raises two problems: (1) The existing possible has two obligations – from its cause and conditional on existence – and the definition should consider absolute necessity, not just one type. (2) Ibn Sina in al-Isharat lists several categories of necessity, showing the answer is too simplistic.
5
Reflect on the types of necessity
Consider Ibn Sina's categories: absolute, conditional on permanence of essence, on description of subject, on predicate description, on specific time, etc. Determine which aligns with the original definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم
(تَكْمِلَةُ) لمباحث الموجهات
فِيهَا مَبَاحِثُ الْأَوَّلُ : اشْتَهَرَ تَعْرِيفُ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ بِأَنَّهَا الَّتِي يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ ثُبُوتِ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ أَوْ سَلْبِهِ عَنْهُ، مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ الْمَوْضُوعِ مَوْجُودًا. وَفِيهِ شَكٍّ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ ، الْأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ إِذَا كَانَ الْمَحْمُولُ هُوَ الْوُجُودُ لَزِمَ عَدَمُ مُنَافَاةِ الضَّرُورَةِ لِلْإِمْكَانِ الْخَاصَّ)؛ لأن الوجود ضروري لموضوعه ما دام موجودا، وهو ممکن خاص ، وإلا صار العالم كله واجبا. (وَأُجِيبَ بِالْفَرْقِ بَيْنَ الضَّرُورَةِ في زَمَانِ الْوُجُودِ وَبَيْنَهَا) بِشَرْطِ الْوُجُودِ)، والمتحقق فيما محموله الوجود الثاني، والمعتبر في الضرورية هو الأول ، فلا نسلم صدق تلك القضايا ضرورية .
وفيه نظر ، أما أولا فلأن الممكن الموجود محفوف بوجوبين: وجوب العلة في زمان وجوده، ووجوب له بشرط الوجود، والمعتبر في الضرورية من مطلق الضرورة، ذاتية كانت أو غيرية من علة.
وأما ثانيا: فلأن الشيخ قال في «الإشارات»: الضرورة قد تكون على الإطلاق، كقولنا الله تعالى حي ، وقد تكون متعلقة بشرط ، والشرط إما دوام وجود الذات، أو دوام كون ذات الموضوع موصوفا بما وضع معه، أو بشرط وصف المحمول، أو وقت معين أو غير معين والضرورة بالشرط الأول وإن
Bahr al-Ulum commentary on Sullam al-Ulum. (Supplement) on the investigations of modalities. In it are investigations. The first: The definition of the absolute necessary has become well-known as that in which judgment is made with necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject or its negation, as long as the essence of the subject exists. And there is a doubt about it from two aspects. The first: that if the predicate is existence, then it follows that necessity does not contradict special possibility; because existence is necessary for its subject as long as it exists, and it is a special possible, otherwise the entire world would become necessary. And it was answered by the distinction between necessity in the time of existence and necessity conditional on existence. And what is actualized in that whose predicate is existence is the second, and what is considered in the necessary proposition is the first, so we do not concede that those propositions are necessary. And there is a critique. First, because the existing possible is surrounded by two obligations: the obligation of the cause in the time of its existence, and an obligation for it conditional on existence, and what is considered in the necessary proposition is absolute necessity, whether essential or derived from a cause. Second, because the Shaykh said in al-Isharat: necessity can be absolute, like our saying 'Allah is living', and it can be conditional on a condition, and the condition is either the permanence of the existence of the essence, or the permanence of the essence of the subject being described with what it is posited with, or conditional on the description of the predicate, or a specific or non-specific time. And necessity with the first condition, and ...
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical investigation (mabahith) in the supplement to the discussion of modalities. It begins by stating the standard definition of the absolute necessary proposition (الضرورية المطلقة): a judgment made with necessity of the predicate's affirmation or negation as long as the subject's essence exists. An objection (shakk) is raised: if the predicate is existence itself, then this definition would imply that necessity does not contradict special possibility (الإمكان الخاص), because existence is necessary for the subject as long as it exists, yet existence itself is possible (otherwise everything would be necessary). The answer distinguishes between 'necessity in the time of existence' (ضرورة في زمان الوجود) and 'necessity conditional on existence' (ضرورة بشرط الوجود). The first is what the definition considers; the second is what applies when the predicate is existence. Therefore, those propositions are not truly necessary. The author then critiques this answer (وفيه نظر). First, the existing possible (الممكن الموجود) is surrounded by two obligations: the obligation from the cause (وجوب العلة) in the time of its existence, and an obligation conditional on existence. The definition of necessary should consider absolute necessity, whether essential (ذاتية) or derived (غيرية من علة). Second, Ibn Sina in al-Isharat lists types of necessity: absolute (like 'Allah is living') and conditional on various conditions (permanence of essence, permanence of description, predicate description, specific or non-specific time). The passage ends abruptly with 'necessity with the first condition' (الضرورة بالشرط الأول), likely continuing to discuss which condition aligns with the definition. The lesson is a critical refinement of the definition, showing the complexity of modal necessity.

Key Terms
الضرورية المطلقة
الإمكان الخاص
ضرورة في زمان الوجود
ضرورة بشرط الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Necessity would not contradict special possibility.
C) The subject is necessarily existent.
D) The proposition is a tautology.

Key Takeaway
The definition of absolute necessity must account for the type of necessity (unconditional vs. conditional) to avoid contradictions, especially when the predicate is existence.

The Objection that Absolute Necessity Requires Eternal Subjects and its Refutation
Second objection to the definition of absolute necessary proposition and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that absolute necessary propositions are restricted to eternal subjects and evaluate the author's response using the concept of general necessity.

Core Idea
Absolute necessity does not require an eternal subject; general necessity suffices, allowing temporal subjects.

This lesson examines a second objection raised against the definition of the absolute necessary proposition. The objector argues that the definition would only apply to propositions with eternal subjects, thereby undermining its generality. We will analyze the logical structure of the objection and the author's refutation, which hinges on the concept of general necessity (al-ḍarūra al-ʿāmma).

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's claim
What does the objector claim follows from the definition of absolute necessary proposition? Find the claim that the definition would restrict such propositions to eternal subjects.
The objector says: the definition entails restricting it to eternal propositions.
2
Extract the objection's reasoning
Why does the objector think the subject must be necessary in itself? The reasoning is: if the subject's existence is not necessary in itself, then nothing can be necessary for it at the time of its existence, because if the subject can cease, the predicate's necessity can cease.
The key phrase: 'from the possibility of its removal, the removal of the affirmation is possible'.
3
Locate the author's response
What is the author's reply? He says that what is necessary in the necessary proposition is the necessity of the subject, even if that necessity is derived from a cause, and the criterion is general necessity.
The author points out that the necessity considered is general necessity (al-ḍarūra al-ʿāmma).
4
Apply general necessity to the objection
How does general necessity allow the subject to be originated? General necessity only requires the predicate to be necessary as long as the subject exists, not forever. Thus, the predicate can be necessary for a temporal subject during its existence.
This preserves the generality of absolute necessary propositions, including those with temporal subjects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأُورِدَ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ حَصْرُهَا فِي الْأَزَلِيَّةِ الَّتِي يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ أَزَلًا وَأَبَدًا، فَلَا تَكُونُ الضَّرُورِيَّةُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ أَعَمَّ مِنْهَا؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَمَّا لَمْ يَجِبْ وُجُودُ الْمَوْضُوعِ فِي نَفْسِهِ لَمْ يَجِبْ لَهُ شَيْءٌ فِي وَقْتِ وُجُودِهِ؛ إِذْ مِنْ إِمْكَانِ ارْتِفَاعِهِ يُمْكِنُ ارْتِفَاعُ الثُّبُوتِ، فَتَكُونُ الضَّرُورِيَّةُ مُنْحَصِرَةً فِيمَا مَوْضُوعُهُ الْوَاجِبُ. وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ؛ فَإِنَّ الْوَاجِبَ فِي الضَّرُورِيَّةِ وُجُوبُ الْمَوْضُوعِ وَلَوْ عِلَّةً؛ فَإِنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِيهَا الضَّرُورَةُ الْعَامَّةُ، فَيَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَوْضُوعُ حَادِثًا، فَيَكُونَ الْمَحْمُولُ وَاجِبًا مَا دَامَ مَوْضُوعُهُ مَوْجُودًا.
It was objected to it that it [the definition] entails restricting it [the absolute necessary proposition] to eternal propositions in which it is judged with necessity of the relation forever and ever, so the absolute necessary proposition would not be more general than it; because when the existence of the subject is not necessary in itself, nothing is necessary for it at a time of its existence; since from the possibility of its removal, the removal of the affirmation is possible, so the necessary proposition is restricted to what its subject is necessary. And what is in it is not hidden; for what is necessary in the necessary proposition is the necessity of the subject, even if by a cause; for what is considered in it is general necessity, so it is permissible that the subject is originated, so the predicate is necessary as long as its subject exists.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection to the definition of the absolute necessary proposition. The objection argues that the definition would restrict absolute necessary propositions only to those with eternal subjects, such that the absolute necessary proposition would not be more general than the eternal proposition. The reasoning is: if the subject's existence is not necessary in itself, then nothing can be necessary for it at the time of its existence, because if the subject can cease to exist, the affirmation of the predicate can also cease. Therefore, only subjects that are necessary (i.e., eternal) can have necessary predicates. The author responds by pointing out that what is necessary in the necessary proposition is the necessity of the subject, even if that necessity is derived from a cause. The criterion is general necessity (al-ḍarūra al-ʿāmma). Thus, it is permissible for the subject to be originated (ḥādith), and the predicate is necessary as long as the subject exists. The response effectively allows absolute necessary propositions to apply to temporal subjects during their period of existence, thus preserving the generality of the concept.

Key Terms
الأزلية
الضرورة العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The predicate must be essential to the subject.
D) The subject must have perpetual existence.

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary proposition is not restricted to eternal subjects because it relies on general necessity, which applies as long as the subject exists.

The Necessity of Essential Properties Without Condition of Existence
Raises objection about essential properties being necessary without condition of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why essential properties (al-dhātīyāt) are necessarily true of an essence without any condition of existence, and reconstruct the reductio argument that refutes conditional necessity for them.

Core Idea
Essential properties are necessary for an essence continuously, not only when it exists — a condition of existence would make them made properties, which is impossible.

This passage presents a critical objection targeting the idea that the necessity of a predicate could be conditioned on existence. The objector argues that for essential properties (dhātīyāt), necessity is unconditional and continuous. If it were conditioned on existence, an impossible priority would result. This distinction is fundamental for understanding modal predication in Islamic logic and metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Find the claim being refuted: that the necessity of a predicate (e.g., 'animality') depends on the existence of the subject (e.g., 'human').
The doctrine under attack is the conditional necessity view mentioned earlier.
2
Extract the essential property example
Notice the example 'animality of man' (ḥayawāniyyat al-insān). Understand that animality is an essential property of the human essence.
3
Reconstruct the reductio argument
Assume the opposite: if animality were necessary only when the human exists, then animality would be 'made' (majʿūla) for the human essence. This would require the essence to be prior to the essential property.
The argument shows that conditional necessity for essential properties leads to an impossible priority.
4
Evaluate the impossibility
Why is priority of essence over essential property impossible? Because essential properties are concomitant with the essence, not caused by it. They are co-eternal in terms of essence.
This is a metaphysical principle: the essence and its essential properties are simultaneous.
5
Apply the lesson
How does this affect the larger discussion? It shows that for essential predicates, necessity is unconditional. This distinction is crucial in Islamic logic for classifying propositions and definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَنُوقِضَ بِثُبُوتِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ لِلذَّاتِ؛ فَإِنَّهُ ضَرُورِيٌّ لِلذَّاتِ دَائِمًا لَا بِشَرْطِ الْوُجُودِ، وَإِلَّا يَكُنْ ضَرُورِيًّا لَا بِشَرْطِ الْوُجُودِ كَانَ وُجُودُ الذَّاتِ شَرْطًا لَكَانَتْ حَيَوَانِيَّةُ الْإِنْسَانِ مَثَلًا مَجْعُولَةً لِذَاتِ الْإِنْسَانِ، فَيَلْزَمُ تَقَدُّمُ الذَّاتِ عَلَى الذَّاتِيِّ، وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ، فَلَيْسَ هَذَا مَوْقُوفًا عَلَى بُطْلَانِ الْمَجْعُولِيَّةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ.
And it was refuted by the establishment of essential properties for the essence; for it is necessary for the essence continuously without the condition of existence, otherwise if it were not necessary without the condition of existence, and existence of the essence were a condition, then the animality of man, for example, would be made (majʿūla) for the essence of man, and that would entail the priority of the essence over the essential property, which is impossible. So this is not dependent on the invalidity of the essential made-ness.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection, framed as a refutation (naqḍ). The objection targets the idea that the necessity of a predicate could be conditioned on existence. The objector argues that essential properties (dhātīyāt) are necessarily true of their essences continuously, without any condition of existence. For example, the animality of a human is necessary for the human essence regardless of whether the human exists at a particular time. If one supposed that animality were necessary only with the condition of existence (i.e., only when the human exists), then animality would be a property that is 'made' (majʿūla) for the essence, implying that the essence is prior to the essential property. This priority is impossible because essential properties are concomitant with the essence, not caused by it. The objection concludes that the previous discussion about conditional necessity does not depend on rejecting the idea that essential properties are 'made' (al-majʿūliyya al-dhātiyya); rather, the point is simply that essential properties are necessary unconditionally. This passage reinforces the distinction between essential and accidental predication in modal logic.

Key Terms
الذاتيات
المجعولية الذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Essential properties (al-dhātīyāt) are necessarily true of an essence continuously, without dependence on existence, and conditioning their necessity on existence leads to logical impossibility.

Refining Absolute Necessary Propositions: General vs Eternal Necessity
Author presents and refutes an objection that absolute necessary propositions are confined to eternal things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute necessary propositions confined to eternal subjects and those that hold with general necessity over temporal subjects.

Core Idea
Absolute necessity does not require an eternal subject; it only requires the predicate to hold as long as the subject exists.

This passage presents an objection that appears to limit absolute necessary propositions to eternal things, and the author's response that clarifies the true nature of the necessity involved. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating propositions in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with temporal subjects like created beings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that if the subject (موضوع) is not necessary in itself, then the predicate cannot be truly necessary even at the time of the subject's existence.
2
Extract the author's distinction
The author introduces 'general necessity' (الضرورة العامة): the predicate is necessary as long as the subject exists, even if the subject is temporal.
3
Apply the distinction to an example
Consider 'Every human is an animal'. The subject 'human' is not eternal, but as long as humans exist, the predicate 'animal' holds necessarily. This is 'absolute necessary' in the author's sense.
4
Check the refined definition
The author defines the absolute necessary proposition not as eternally true, but as necessarily true for the duration of the subject's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأُورِدَ عليه أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ حَصْرُهَا فِي الْأَزَلِيَّةِ الَّتِي يُحْكُمْ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ أَزَلًا وَأَبَدًا ، فَلَا تَكُونُ الضرورية المطلقة أَعَمَّ منها؛ لأَنَّهُ لَمَا لَمْ يَجِبْ وُجُودُ الْمَوْضُوعِ في نفسه لَمْ يَجِبْ لَهُ شَيْءٌ فِي وَقْتِ وُجُودِهِ ؛ إذ من إمكان ارتفاعه يمكن ارتفاع الثبوت، فتكون الضرورية منحصرة فيما موضوعه الواجب. ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن الواجب في الضرورية وجوب الموضوع ولو علة ؛ فإن المعتبر فيها الضرورة العامة، فيجوز أن يكون الموضوع حادثا ، فيكون المحمول واجبا ما دام موضوعه موجودا.
And it was objected against it that it necessitates confining it to eternal things in which the necessity of the relation is judged eternally and forever, so the absolute necessary would not be more general than it; because since the existence of the subject is not necessary in itself, nothing is necessary for it at the time of its existence, since from the possibility of its removal the removal of the affirmation is possible, so the necessary would be confined to that whose subject is necessary. And it is not hidden what is in it; for the necessary in the necessary proposition is the necessity of the subject even if by a cause; for what is considered in it is general necessity, so it is permissible that the subject be temporal, so the predicate would be necessary as long as its subject exists.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response regarding the scope of absolute necessary propositions. The objector claims that absolute necessary propositions (الضرورية المطلقة) must be limited to eternal subjects, because if the subject's existence is not necessary, then the predicate's necessity could be doubted. The author refutes this by explaining that the necessity required is only 'general necessity' (الضرورة العامة), meaning the predicate is necessary as long as the subject exists, even if the subject itself is temporal. For example, 'Every human is an animal' is necessary as long as humans exist, but not eternally. Thus, absolute necessary propositions are not confined to eternal things.

Key Terms
الضرورية المطلقة (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa): A proposition judged to be necessary without any condition, but the necessity is only as long as the subject exists [Note: In earlier texts, الضرورية المطلقة sometimes meant necessity for all time; here it is defined as general necessity tied to the subject's existence.]
الضرورة العامة (al-darura al-‘amma): The necessity that holds as long as the subject exists, even if the subject is not eternal

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is true only if the subject is eternal.
D) It is false if the subject does not exist eternally.

Key Takeaway
Absolute necessary propositions are not confined to eternal subjects; they require only that the predicate holds necessarily as long as the subject exists.
