Clarifying Negative Propositions with 'As Long As Existence'
Author resolves a doubt about negative conditioned propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why negative propositions with the condition 'as long as existence' are true even when the subject does not exist, using the temporal container interpretation.

Core Idea
Negative 'as long as existence' propositions are true by subject non-existence.

This lesson tackles a subtle doubt in Islamic logic: if a negative proposition is conditioned on the existence of its subject, how can it be true when the subject does not exist? The passage from Bahr al-Ulum presents the doubt and the resolution from the glosses, teaching us how 'as long as' functions as a temporal container for negation rather than a strict condition on the subject's existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
Read the first sentence: 'The negative with as long as existence is not true without it.' The doubt is that a conditioned proposition requires its condition to be realized. If the condition is the existence of the subject, then when the subject does not exist, the proposition cannot be true. This leads to problems like the statement 'No phoenix is a human' being false.
2
Locate the two consequences
The passage lists two consequences: (1) the negative would not be more general than the modified affirmative, and (2) it would necessitate the raising of the two contradictories when the subject is nonexistent. Understand that the first concerns the logical relationship between negative and modified affirmative propositions, and the second is a logical impossibility (both true and false simultaneously).
3
Examine the resolution
The answer from the glosses clarifies that 'as long as' (ما دام) is a container (ظرف) for the negation, not a condition on the subject's existence. This means the negation is judged over that time span. The proposition can be true either by the non-existence of the subject or by the non-existence of the predicate, either in all times or some times.
4
Apply to the examples
The example 'No moon is eclipsed by necessity' is true because the moon is not always eclipsed (predicate non-existence in some times). Similarly, 'The phoenix is not a human' is true because the phoenix does not exist in any time (subject non-existence in all times). Thus, the negative proposition holds without requiring the subject to exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشك الثاني: السَّلْبُ مَا دَامَ الْوُجُودُ لَا يَصْدُقُ بِدُونِهِ؛ إذ لا بد لتحقق المقيد من تحقق القيد ، فَلَا تَكُونُ السَّالِبَةُ أَعَمَّ من الموجبة المعدولة ، وَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ قولنا: ((لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْعَنْقَاءِ بِإِنْسَانِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ»)، ويلزم ارتفاع النقيضين فيما إذا كان الموضوع معدوما. وَأُجِيبَ في الحواشي المتعلقة بأَنَّ مَا دَامَ ظَرْفُ لِلنُّبُوتِ الَّذِي يَتَضَمَّنُهُ السَّلْبُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَجُوزُ صِدْقُهَا بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوْضُوعِ وَبِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَحْمُولِ، إِمَّا في جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ أَوْ بَعْضِهَا، نَحْوُ «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْقَمَرِ بِمُنْخَسِفَ بِالضَّرُورَةِ ، فحينئذ يصدق «العنقاء ليس بإنسان ، فلا إشكال .
The second doubt: The negative [proposition] with 'as long as existence' is not true without it; because for the realization of the conditioned, the realization of the condition is necessary, so the negative would not be more general than the modified affirmative, and it would necessitate that our statement 'No phoenix is a human by necessity' would not be true, and it would necessitate the raising of the two contradictories when the subject is nonexistent. And it was answered in the related glosses that 'as long as' is a container for the negation which the negative entails, and then its truth is permissible by the non-existence of the subject and by the non-existence of the predicate, either in all times or some of them, like 'No moon is eclipsed by necessity', and then 'The phoenix is not a human' is true, so there is no problem.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage presents a second doubt about negative propositions that include the condition 'as long as existence' (السلب ما دام الوجود). The doubt claims that such a negative proposition cannot be true when the subject does not exist, because a conditioned proposition requires its condition. Consequently, statements like 'No phoenix is a human by necessity' would be false, leading to a contradiction. The answer, derived from glosses, clarifies that 'as long as' (ما دام) is a temporal container for the negation, meaning the negation is judged over that time span. The proposition can be true either because the subject does not exist (so negation holds in all times) or because the predicate does not exist. For example, 'No moon is eclipsed by necessity' is true because the moon is not always eclipsed. Thus, 'No phoenix is a human' is true because the phoenix does not exist in any time, so the negation holds.

Key Terms
السلب ما دام الوجود
الموجبة المعدولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the condition 'as long as existence' fails.

Key Takeaway
The condition 'as long as existence' in negative propositions acts as a temporal container for the negation, not a strict existential condition, allowing truth even when the subject does not exist.

Negative Necessary Does Not Negate Essential Possibility
Author argues that a negative necessary proposition does not contradict essential possibility of the opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a negative necessary proposition and a claim of impossibility, using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
A negative necessary proposition only denies necessity, not possibility.

In logic, it is easy to confuse 'not necessary' with 'impossible'. This passage shows that a negative necessary proposition (e.g., 'No moon is eclipsed by necessity') does not contradict the essential possibility of its opposite (e.g., the moon can be eclipsed). The example of lunar eclipses illustrates how something can be possible even if it is not necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negative necessary proposition
In the passage, the author refers to 'No moon is eclipsed by necessity'. This is a negative necessary proposition: it denies that the moon's eclipse is necessary.
2
Understand essential possibility
Essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) means that something is neither necessary nor impossible in itself. For the moon, being eclipsed is possible: it can happen, but it is not always.
3
Test compatibility with an actual statement
The author says 'Every moon is eclipsed' is true with possibility (i.e., it can be true at a given time). This actual eclipse does not contradict the negative necessary claim because the negative necessary only says it is not always necessary.
4
Apply the distinction to other Islamic examples
Consider: 'No prayer is valid without purification by necessity.' This is a negative necessary proposition. It does not mean purification is impossible in prayer; rather, it is necessary for validity, but the negation denies necessity in the sense that without purification validity is impossible. This shows careful handling is needed.
Note: the example is illustrative; in fiqh, such statements are understood as conditional necessities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِيهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يُنَافِيَ الْإِمْكَانَ الذاتي؛ فَإِنَّ كُلَّ قَمَرِ مُنْخَسِفٌ بِالْفِعْلِ صَادِقٌ ، فَيَصْدُقُ كُل قمر منخسف بِالْإِمْكَانِ، وقد اعترف بصدق «لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة ، وَيَبْطُلُ مَا قَالُوا: إِنَّ السَّالِبَةَ
And in it [there is] that it necessitates that it does not contradict essential possibility; for every moon that is actually eclipsed is true, so 'Every moon is eclipsed' is true with possibility, and it has been acknowledged that 'No moon is eclipsed by necessity' is true, and it invalidates what they said: that the negative...
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage raises a further point about negative necessary propositions. It argues that a negative necessary proposition (e.g., 'No moon is eclipsed by necessity') does not contradict essential possibility (الإمكان الذاتي). For example, 'Every moon is eclipsed' is not actually true at all times, but it is possible. The negative necessary proposition is true because the moon is not necessarily eclipsed, yet a particular eclipse is possible. This invalidates the claim that the negative proposition must contradict the possibility of the opposite. The text is incomplete, but the core argument is clear: a negative necessary proposition does not entail the impossibility of the opposite; it only negates necessity.

Key Terms
الإمكان الذاتي
السلبية الضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It implies that the moon cannot be eclipsed.

C) It contradicts the essential possibility of the moon.
D) It is false because the moon is eclipsed.

Key Takeaway
A negative necessary proposition only denies necessity, not possibility; it does not contradict essential possibility.

Doubt about Negative Propositions: Initial Answer
Presents a doubt and its initial answer regarding negative propositions with non-existent subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why negative propositions with non-existent subjects require temporal restrictions and how the initial answer from the margins resolves the apparent paradox.

Core Idea
A negative proposition can be true if its subject does not exist, because 'as long as' refers to the time of the negation's implication.

In modal logic, a negative proposition like 'No S is P' is typically considered true only if the subject exists. But what about subjects that do not exist, like a phoenix? This creates a doubt. The initial answer from marginal commentaries proposes that the temporal phrase 'as long as existence' modifies the negation's time frame, allowing the proposition to be true due to the non-existence of the subject. We will examine this answer and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
Find the core problem: a negative proposition restricted by 'as long as existence' cannot be true if the subject does not exist, because the restriction fails. This is shown with the example of the phoenix (عنقاء).
2
Understand the initial answer
The answer from the margins reinterprets 'as long as' (ما دام) as a time indicator for the implied proposition within the negation. Thus, the negation can be true either because the subject does not exist or because the predicate does not hold at all times. The example of the moon's eclipse illustrates this.
3
Apply to the phoenix case
Following the answer, 'Nothing of the phoenix is a human being by necessity' becomes true because the phoenix does not exist, so the negation holds for all times.
4
Evaluate the answer
Note that this answer resolves the initial issue, but the passage will later raise objections. For now, understand the reasoning behind it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشك (الثَّانِي: السَّلْبُ مَا دَامَ الْوُجُودُ لَا يَصْدُقُ بِدُونِهِ)؛ إذ لا بد لتحقق المقيد من تحقق القيد ، (فَلَا تَكُونُ السَّالِبَةُ أَعَمَّ) من الموجبة المعدولة ، (وَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ) قولنا: ((لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْعَنْقَاءِ بِإِنْسَانِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ»)، ويلزم ارتفاع النقيضين فيما إذا كان الموضوع معدوما. (وَأُجِيبَ) في الحواشي المتعلقة بأَنَّ مَا دَامَ ظَرْفُ لِلنُّبُوتِ الَّذِي يَتَضَمَّنُهُ السَّلْبُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَجُوزُ صِدْقُهَا بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوْضُوعِ (٢) وَبِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَحْمُولِ (٣) ، إِمَّا في جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ(٤) أَوْ بَعْضِهَا، نَحْوُ «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْقَمَرِ بِمُنْخَسِفَ بِالضَّرُورَةِ ، فحينئذ يصدق «العنقاء ليس بإنسان ، فلا إشكال . (وَفِيهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يُنَافِيَ الْإِمْكَانَ الذاتي؛ فَإِنَّ كُلَّ قَمَرِ مُنْخَسِفٌ بِالْفِعْلِ [ صَادِقٌ ](٥) ، فَيَصْدُقُ كل قمر منخسف» (بِالْإِمْكَانِ)، وقد اعترف بصدق «لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة ، (وَيَبْطُلُ مَا قَالُوا: إِنَّ السَّالِبَةَ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ الْأَزَلِيَّةَ وَالْمُطْلَقَةَ مُتَسَاوِيَتَانِ) ؛ لأنه إن جعل الأزلية والأبدية قيدا للسلب فحينئذ لا يصدق سلب الانخساف أزلا وأبدا ، مع صدق السلب ما دام الذات، وإن جعل قيدا للمسلوب فكذا ؛ فإن الثبوت أزلا وأبدا أخص من الثبوت ما دام الذات، فسلب الثبوت أزلا وأبدا أعم منه ما دام الذات؛ (فَإِنَّ سَلْبَ الْأَعَمِّ أَخَصُّ مِنْ سَلْب الْأَخَصَّ). وإذا كان بين السلبين عموما فكذا بين ضروريتهما ؛ فإن ضرورة الخاص مستلزمة لضرورة العام من غير عكس، فإذن السالبة الضرورية المطلقة أخص من السالبة الأزلية. (وَبِالْجُمْلَةِ يَلْزَمُ مَفَاسِدُ غَيْرُ عَدِيدَةٍ)، كلزوم تساوي السالبة الضرورية والمنتشرة المطلقة، وعدم تقييدها بالضرورة الذاتية ، وانعكاسها كنفسها ولا إلى الدائمة، وعدم إنتاجها مع الكبرى الغير المنعكسة في الشكل الثاني، ومع الصغرى غير الدائمتين فيه، وغير ذلك . لَا تَخْفَىَ عَلَى الْمُتَدَرِّبِ، وَغَايَةُ مَا يُجَابُ بِهِ عن أصل الإشكال (أَنَّ الْوُجُودَ) المأخوذ في التعريف (أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُحَقَّقِ وَالْمُقَدَّرِ ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ)؛ إذ إشكال تساوي السالبة والموجبة المعدولة الحقيقية باق. والحق في الجواب أن الضرورة ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة كناية عن الضرورة المغايرة للوصفية والوقتية هذا ، والله أعلم بالصواب.
The second doubt: Negation as long as existence does not hold true without it; because for the restricted to be realized, the restriction must be realized, so the negative is not more general than the modified affirmative. And it follows that our statement 'Nothing of the phoenix is a human being by necessity' is not true, and it follows that both contradictories are raised when the subject is non-existent. And it was answered in the marginalia that 'as long as' is a time for the propostion which the negation entails, and then it is possible for it to be true by the non-existence of the subject and by the non-existence of the predicate, either in all times or some of them, like 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity'; then the statement 'The phoenix is not a human being' is true, so there is no problem. But it is objected that it would follow that it does not contradict essential possibility; for every moon is actually eclipsed, so 'Every moon is eclipsed' is true by possibility, and they have admitted the truth of 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity'. And what they said—that the eternal necessary negative and the absolute necessary negative are equivalent—is invalidated; because if eternity and perpetuity are taken as a restriction on the negation, then the negation of eclipse does not hold true eternally and perpetually, while the negation as long as the essence holds true. If they are taken as a restriction on the negated, likewise; for eternal and perpetual affirmation is more specific than affirmation as long as essence exists, so the negation of affirmation eternally and perpetually is more general than negation as long as essence exists. For the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific. And if there is generality between the two negations, likewise between their necessities; for the necessity of the specific entails the necessity of the general without inverse, so the absolute necessary negative is more specific than the eternal necessary negative. In sum, numerous corruptions follow, such as the equivalence of the necessary negative and the distributed absolute negative, its lack of restriction by essential necessity, its conversion like itself and not to the perpetual, its lack of productivity with the major that is not converted in the second figure, and with the minor that is not perpetual in it, and other things not hidden from the practiced. The utmost that can be answered concerning the original problem is that existence taken in the definition is more general than actual and assumed, but there is something in it; for the problem of the equivalence of the negative and the true modified affirmative remains. And the truth in the answer is that necessity as long as the subject's essence exists is an allusion to the necessity that is different from descriptive and temporal. This is it, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a point about modal negative propositions. It begins by stating a doubt: the definition of 'negation as long as existence' implies that a negative proposition is not true without the existence of the subject. This leads to problems when the subject is non-existent, e.g., 'Nothing of the phoenix is a human being by necessity' would not be true, and both contradictories would be raised. The initial answer from the margins (hashi) explains that 'as long as' refers to the time of the negation's implication, so the proposition can be true due to the non-existence of the subject or predicate, like 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity' being true at all times when there is no eclipse. Thus 'The phoenix is not a human being' is true. The author then objects to this answer: it would contradict essential possibility because 'Every moon is eclipsed' is true by possibility (since it is possible for the moon to be eclipsed), while 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity' is also claimed true, leading to a contradiction. This invalidates the claim that the eternal necessary negative and the absolute necessary negative are equivalent. The author explains that if eternity and perpetuity are taken as restrictions on the negation, then eternal negation of eclipse is false because it is not always eclipsed, whereas 'as long as essence' negation is true. If they are restrictions on the negated proposition, then eternal affirmation is more specific than 'as long as essence' affirmation, so negating the more general makes the result more specific. Thus the absolute necessary negative is more specific than the eternal one. Many corruptions follow, such as the equivalence of necessary and absolute negatives, conversion issues, and unproductive syllogisms. The final answer is that 'necessity as long as the essence exists' refers to a necessity distinct from descriptive and temporal necessity, resolving the issues.

Key Terms
السَّالِبَة
مَا دَامَ
الضَّرُورِيَّة

Checkpoint

A) Because the phoenix is a species that can be human
B) Because 'as long as' refers to the time of the negation's implication, so the negation is true due to non-existence of the subject
C) Because the proposition is not necessary
D) Because the phoenix exists in some worlds

Key Takeaway
The initial answer resolves the doubt by reinterpreting 'as long as' as a temporal modifier for the implied proposition, allowing negative propositions to be true when the subject does not exist.

?

Critique of the Answer: Contradiction with Essential Possibility
Presents an objection to the initial answer and analyzes the relationship between eternal and absolute necessary negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection that the initial answer violates essential possibility and analyze the relationship between eternal and absolute necessary negatives.

Core Idea
The initial answer leads to a contradiction: it affirms both a necessary negative and an affirmative possibility for the same subject.

The initial answer, while resolving the doubt about non-existent subjects, creates a new problem. The objection states that it contradicts essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati). For example, 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity' is claimed true, but at the same time 'Every moon is eclipsed by possibility' is also true, leading to a contradiction. This forces a reexamination of the modal properties of negation and the distinction between eternal and absolute necessity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that the initial answer makes the necessary negative (e.g., 'No moon is eclipsed by necessity') compatible with an affirmative possibility ('Every moon is eclipsed by possibility'). Since possibility entails that the negation is not necessary, this is a contradiction.
Essential possibility means that something is possible in itself, not externally constrained.
2
Examine the moon example
The moon is actually eclipsed sometimes, so 'Every moon is eclipsed by possibility' is true (since it is possible for it to be eclipsed). The answer also claims 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity' is true (since it is not always eclipsed). These two statements appear contradictory.
3
Analyze the claim about eternal and absolute necessity
The passage then criticizes the view that 'eternal necessary negative' and 'absolute necessary negative' are equivalent. It shows that if eternity/perpetuity is a restriction on the negation, then the eternal negation (e.g., the moon is never eclipsed) is false, while the absolute negation (as long as essence) is true. Thus they are not equivalent.
4
Understand the relationship of generality
If the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific, then the absolute necessary negative is more specific than the eternal necessary negative. The necessity of the specific entails the necessity of the general, but not vice versa.
This is a key logical principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشك (الثَّانِي: السَّلْبُ مَا دَامَ الْوُجُودُ لَا يَصْدُقُ بِدُونِهِ)؛ إذ لا بد لتحقق المقيد من تحقق القيد ، (فَلَا تَكُونُ السَّالِبَةُ أَعَمَّ) من الموجبة المعدولة ، (وَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ) قولنا: ((لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْعَنْقَاءِ بِإِنْسَانِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ»)، ويلزم ارتفاع النقيضين فيما إذا كان الموضوع معدوما. (وَأُجِيبَ) في الحواشي المتعلقة بأَنَّ مَا دَامَ ظَرْفُ لِلنُّبُوتِ الَّذِي يَتَضَمَّنُهُ السَّلْبُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَجُوزُ صِدْقُهَا بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوْضُوعِ (٢) وَبِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَحْمُولِ (٣) ، إِمَّا في جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ(٤) أَوْ بَعْضِهَا، نَحْوُ «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْقَمَرِ بِمُنْخَسِفَ بِالضَّرُورَةِ ، فحينئذ يصدق «العنقاء ليس بإنسان ، فلا إشكال . (وَفِيهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يُنَافِيَ الْإِمْكَانَ الذاتي؛ فَإِنَّ كُلَّ قَمَرِ مُنْخَسِفٌ بِالْفِعْلِ [ صَادِقٌ ](٥) ، فَيَصْدُقُ كل قمر منخسف» (بِالْإِمْكَانِ)، وقد اعترف بصدق «لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة ، (وَيَبْطُلُ مَا قَالُوا: إِنَّ السَّالِبَةَ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ الْأَزَلِيَّةَ وَالْمُطْلَقَةَ مُتَسَاوِيَتَانِ) ؛ لأنه إن جعل الأزلية والأبدية قيدا للسلب فحينئذ لا يصدق سلب الانخساف أزلا وأبدا ، مع صدق السلب ما دام الذات، وإن جعل قيدا للمسلوب فكذا ؛ فإن الثبوت أزلا وأبدا أخص من الثبوت ما دام الذات، فسلب الثبوت أزلا وأبدا أعم منه ما دام الذات؛ (فَإِنَّ سَلْبَ الْأَعَمِّ أَخَصُّ مِنْ سَلْب الْأَخَصَّ). وإذا كان بين السلبين عموما فكذا بين ضروريتهما ؛ فإن ضرورة الخاص مستلزمة لضرورة العام من غير عكس، فإذن السالبة الضرورية المطلقة أخص من السالبة الأزلية. (وَبِالْجُمْلَةِ يَلْزَمُ مَفَاسِدُ غَيْرُ عَدِيدَةٍ)، كلزوم تساوي السالبة الضرورية والمنتشرة المطلقة، وعدم تقييدها بالضرورة الذاتية ، وانعكاسها كنفسها ولا إلى الدائمة، وعدم إنتاجها مع الكبرى الغير المنعكسة في الشكل الثاني، ومع الصغرى غير الدائمتين فيه، وغير ذلك . لَا تَخْفَىَ عَلَى الْمُتَدَرِّبِ، وَغَايَةُ مَا يُجَابُ بِهِ عن أصل الإشكال (أَنَّ الْوُجُودَ) المأخوذ في التعريف (أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُحَقَّقِ وَالْمُقَدَّرِ ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ)؛ إذ إشكال تساوي السالبة والموجبة المعدولة الحقيقية باق. والحق في الجواب أن الضرورة ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة كناية عن الضرورة المغايرة للوصفية والوقتية هذا ، والله أعلم بالصواب.
The second doubt: Negation as long as existence does not hold true without it; because for the restricted to be realized, the restriction must be realized, so the negative is not more general than the modified affirmative. And it follows that our statement 'Nothing of the phoenix is a human being by necessity' is not true, and it follows that both contradictories are raised when the subject is non-existent. And it was answered in the marginalia that 'as long as' is a time for the propostion which the negation entails, and then it is possible for it to be true by the non-existence of the subject and by the non-existence of the predicate, either in all times or some of them, like 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity'; then the statement 'The phoenix is not a human being' is true, so there is no problem. But it is objected that it would follow that it does not contradict essential possibility; for every moon is actually eclipsed, so 'Every moon is eclipsed' is true by possibility, and they have admitted the truth of 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity'. And what they said—that the eternal necessary negative and the absolute necessary negative are equivalent—is invalidated; because if eternity and perpetuity are taken as a restriction on the negation, then the negation of eclipse does not hold true eternally and perpetually, while the negation as long as the essence holds true. If they are taken as a restriction on the negated, likewise; for eternal and perpetual affirmation is more specific than affirmation as long as essence exists, so the negation of affirmation eternally and perpetually is more general than negation as long as essence exists. For the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific. And if there is generality between the two negations, likewise between their necessities; for the necessity of the specific entails the necessity of the general without inverse, so the absolute necessary negative is more specific than the eternal necessary negative. In sum, numerous corruptions follow, such as the equivalence of the necessary negative and the distributed absolute negative, its lack of restriction by essential necessity, its conversion like itself and not to the perpetual, its lack of productivity with the major that is not converted in the second figure, and with the minor that is not perpetual in it, and other things not hidden from the practiced. The utmost that can be answered concerning the original problem is that existence taken in the definition is more general than actual and assumed, but there is something in it; for the problem of the equivalence of the negative and the true modified affirmative remains. And the truth in the answer is that necessity as long as the subject's essence exists is an allusion to the necessity that is different from descriptive and temporal. This is it, and God knows best what is correct.

Final Resolution: Distinguishing Necessities
Concludes the discussion with a list of corruptions and the final resolution distinguishing types of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the final answer: that 'necessity as long as essence exists' alludes to a necessity distinct from descriptive and temporal necessity, resolving the logical corruptions.

Core Idea
The final answer distinguishes 'necessity as long as essence' from eternal, descriptive, and temporal necessity.

After enumerating multiple logical corruptions that follow from the confusion, the passage presents the ultimate resolution. The best answer to the original doubt is that 'existence' in the definition includes both actual and assumed existence, but this still leaves some issues. The true solution is that 'necessity as long as the subject's essence exists' is a metaphor for a necessity that is neither descriptive nor temporal. This distinction, while subtle, resolves the paradoxes and preserves the correct logical relations.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the list of corruptions
The author enumerates several logical problems that follow from the previous analysis, such as the equivalence of necessary and absolute negatives, conversion issues, and failures in syllogistic figures.
2
Understand the first proposed answer
One attempted answer is that 'existence' in the definition is more general and includes both actual and assumed existence. However, this still leaves the problem of the equivalence of the negative and the true modified affirmative unresolved.
3
Grasp the final solution
The author states that the necessity in 'as long as the subject's essence exists' is a specific type of necessity that is different from descriptive necessity (which holds as long as the subject has a certain description) and temporal necessity (which holds at specific times).
4
Appreciate the resolution
With this understanding, the earlier corruptions are avoided. The phrase 'necessity as long as essence exists' is a technical term that refers to essential necessity, not accidental or temporal.
This is the author's final verdict.

The Definition of the Absolute Perpetual and a Doubt When Predicate Is Existence
Defines the absolute perpetual proposition and raises a doubt about its distinction from general absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of the absolute perpetual proposition and explain the doubt that collapses it into the general absolute when the predicate is existence.

Core Idea
The perpetual proposition must differ from the absolute, but if the predicate is existence, they become identical.

In this passage, the author presents the standard definition of the absolute perpetual proposition and then raises a critical doubt: when the predicate is 'existence', the definition fails to distinguish it from the general absolute. This doubt forces us to refine our understanding of modal propositions, which is essential for precise logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the phrase that defines the absolute perpetual proposition. It states that the judgment of the relation (affirmation or negation) continues as long as the subject's essence exists. Write this definition in your own words.
The Arabic is: 'ما حكم فيها بدوام النسبة ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة'.
2
Understand the terms
Distinguish between 'da'im mutlaq' (absolute perpetual) and 'itlaq 'amm' (general absolute). The perpetual asserts truth for all times the subject exists; the general absolute asserts truth at least once. List one example of each from Islamic knowledge to solidify the distinction.
For example: 'Every human is mortal' is perpetual; 'Some humans are prophets' is absolute.
3
Locate the doubt
The author says 'وههنا شك' (and here there is a doubt). Identify the doubt: if the predicate is 'existence', the perpetual becomes equivalent to the general absolute. Explain why this happens using the definition.
Because existence is always true of the subject as long as it exists, so the perpetual condition (continuous truth) is automatically satisfied, just as the absolute requires only one instance. Thus they collapse.
4
Trace the implication
Even though the text cuts off, infer what the doubt implies for the definition. What modification might be needed to avoid this collapse?
The definition must be refined to exclude predicates that are always true of the subject, such as existence, or to add a condition that the predicate is not identical to the subject's essence.

Commentary
This passage begins a new topic (al-mabhath al-thani) on the definition of the absolute perpetual proposition (al-da'imatu al-mutlaqa). The standard definition states that a perpetual proposition is one that asserts the continuance of the relation (the predicate being affirmed or negated of the subject) as long as the subject's essence exists. The author then raises a doubt: if the predicate of the proposition is 'existence' itself, then the definition causes the perpetual to be indistinguishable from the general absolute proposition (which is true at least once). The doubt is that the perpetual would not differ from the general absolute when the predicate is existence, because existence is co-extensive with the subject's essence. The text cuts off, but the intended point is that the definition needs refinement to avoid this collapse.

Absolute Perpetual Definition and Its Problem
Defines absolute perpetual and raises an objection about its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of the absolute perpetual proposition and explain why it fails for predicates of existence.

Core Idea
A definition must cover all cases; existence as predicate breaks the distinction between perpetual and absolute.

In this lesson, we examine the standard definition of the absolute perpetual proposition (al-dā'ima al-muṭlaqa) and a subtle problem that arises when the predicate is existence itself. A gloss from a commentary attempts to save the definition, but the author argues that this answer is insufficient. Understanding this issue sharpens your ability to evaluate definitions in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
The well-known definition: a proposition judged to have persistence of relation as long as the subject's substance exists. This is called essential perpetuity (al-dawām al-dhātī).
For affirmative propositions, the relation holds for the entire duration of the subject's existence.
2
Identify the problem predicate
Consider when the predicate is existence itself, e.g., 'Existence exists.' Such a proposition would be necessarily perpetual because existence always exists, so the perpetual proposition collapses into the general absolute (al-muṭlaqa al-ʿāmma), violating the intended distinction.
The problem arises because existence is a necessary attribute of the subject's substance.
3
Examine the gloss's attempt
A gloss on the commentary of al-Shamsiyya argues that the definition's prima facie understanding requires the predicate to be other than existence. Restricting a thing to its own time is conventionally unintelligible.
This attempt limits the definition to predicates that are not existence.
4
Evaluate the author's counterexample
The author objects using predicates that are necessary concomitants of existence (lāzim al-wujūd). Example: 'The Active Intellect is not in actual existence' is a false absolute general, so its contradictory is a true absolute perpetual with existence as predicate, showing the definition fails.
This demonstrates that the gloss's answer is insufficient.
5
Understand the resolution
The definition must be reinterpreted: the absolute perpetual means actuality of the relation at the time of the subject's existence, while the absolute general means actuality absolutely (without temporal restriction). This restores the distinction.
This resolution is not explicitly stated in earlier texts but is implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني: الْمَشْهُورُ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الدَّائِمَةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِدَوَامِ النِّسْبَةِ مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ الْمَوْضُوعِ مَوْجُودَةً، وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ يُفَارِقَ الدَّوَامُ الذَّاتِيُّ الْإِطْلَاقَ الْعَامَّ في قَضِيَّةِ مَحْمُولُهَا الْوُجُودُ؛ فإن الوجود ما دام الوجود ضروري دائم، فَلَا يَكُونُ بَيْنَهُمَا تَنَاقُضِ قِيلَ في الحواشي المتعلقة بـ«شرح الشمسية للفاضل اللاهوري: (الْمُتَبَادَرُ مِنَ التَّعْرِيفِ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَحْمُولُ مُغَايِرًا لِلْوُجُودِ؛ فإن تقييد الشيء بزمان نفسه غير معقول عرفا، فَلَيْسَ هُنَاكَ)، أي فيما المحمول فيه الوجود (دَوَامٌ ذَاتِيُّ) اصطلاحي. وهذا الجواب لا يتأتى إذا نقض بما فيه المحمول من لوازم الوجود. أَقُولُ: «الْعَقْلُ الْفَعَالُ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودِ بِالْفِعْلِ») مطلقة عامة ( كَاذِبٌ، فَيَلْزَمُ صِدْقُ نَقِيضِهِ، وَهُوَ دَائِمَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ مَحْمُولُهَا الْوُجُودُ)، فلا بد من حمل التعريف على غير المتبادر، وإلا لم يكن جامعا فإذن لا جواب عن الإشكال المذكور إلا بالتصرف في معنى المطلقة التي هو نقيضها؛ فإنها ما حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة حال وجود الذات، وأما ما حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة مطلقا فهو أعم ونقيض الأزلية هذا وإن لم يكن منصوصا في كلامهم لكن ينبغي أن يكون مرادهم ذلك، والله أعلم بمقاصد عباده.
The second investigation: The well-known definition of the absolute perpetual is that in which the persistence of the relation is judged as long as the substance of the subject exists. And here there is a doubt, which is: that it necessarily follows that the essential perpetuity differs from the general absolute in a proposition whose predicate is existence; for existence as long as existence is necessarily perpetual, so there is no contradiction between them. It was said in the glosses related to the Commentary on al-Shamsiyya by al-Fāḍil al-Lāhūrī: 'The prima facie understanding of the definition is that the predicate be other than existence; for restricting a thing to its own time is conventionally unintelligible, so there is no (essential perpetuity) there, i.e., in what has existence as predicate, in the technical sense.' And this answer does not hold if it is refuted by what has the predicate from the concomitants of existence. I say: 'The Active Intellect is not in actual existence' is a false absolute general, so the truth of its contradictory follows, which is an absolute perpetual whose predicate is existence. So the definition must be interpreted in a non-prima facie way, otherwise it would not be comprehensive. Thus there is no answer to the mentioned objection except by modifying the meaning of the absolute which is its contradictory; for it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged at the time of the existence of the substance, while that in which the actuality of the relation is judged absolutely is more general and the contradictory of the eternal. This, even if not explicitly stated in their words, should be their intended meaning, and God knows best the purposes of His servants.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is a definitional investigation with a critical objection. The author states the well-known definition of the absolute perpetual (dā'ima muṭlaqa): a proposition judged to have a relation that persists as long as the subject's substance exists. A problem is raised: if the predicate is existence itself, then the proposition 'existence exists as long as existence' is necessarily perpetual, so the perpetual and the general absolute become equivalent, undermining the distinction. A gloss from a commentary on al-Shamsiyya attempts to answer by saying that the definition's prima facie understanding requires the predicate to be other than existence, because restricting a thing to its own time is irrational. The author objects that this answer fails if the predicate is a necessary concomitant of existence (lāzim al-wujūd). An example is given: 'The Active Intellect is not in actual existence' is a false absolute general, so its contradictory is true and would be an absolute perpetual with existence as predicate. The author concludes that the definition must be reinterpreted: the absolute perpetual means actuality of the relation at the time of the subject's existence, while the absolute general means actuality absolutely. This resolves the issue, even if not explicitly stated by earlier scholars. The commentary highlights the logical precision required in defining modal propositions.

Key Terms
الدائمة المطلقة
الدوام الذاتي
المطلقة العامة
النقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because 'existence exists' is necessarily perpetual, making the definition tautological.
C) Because the definition requires the predicate to be other than existence, but existence is not a genuine predicate.
D) Because the subject's existence is not guaranteed in such propositions.

Key Takeaway
The definition of absolute perpetual must exclude predicates of existence or its necessary concomitants to remain distinct from the general absolute.

Two Senses of the General Conditional
Defines the general conditional in two senses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two senses of the general conditional and identify which sense is being used in a given proposition from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The general conditional has two distinct senses: necessity tied to the attribute or to the subject during the attribute's time.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial distinction in modal propositions: the general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma) can be understood in two ways. The first sense involves the necessity of the relation being conditional upon the descriptive attribute itself, meaning the necessity belongs to the combination of the subject and the attribute. The second sense attributes necessity to the subject alone, but only during the time the attribute holds. This distinction is essential for correctly interpreting conditional statements in Islamic logic, particularly in discussions of divine attributes and temporal relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses of the general conditional
Read the Arabic carefully and note that the author presents two interpretations: (1) necessity of the relation with the condition of the descriptive attribute, and (2) necessity of the relation in all times of the attribute. These are distinct.
The first sense is more restrictive; the second is broader.
2
Determine what the necessity attaches to in each sense
In the first sense, the necessity belongs to the combination of the substance (dhāt) and the attribute (waṣf). In the second sense, the necessity belongs to the substance itself, but only during the time of the attribute.
The phrase 'ضَرُورَةَ النِّسْبَةِ' (necessity of the relation) is key.
3
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Consider: 'Every writer is human as long as he is writing.' In the first sense, the necessity of being human is tied to the attribute of being a writer. In the second sense, the necessity is for the writer himself but only while he writes.
This example is from the commentary and helps solidify the distinction.
4
Check the condition of actual occurrence
Both senses require that the relation actually occur during the relevant time. The phrase 'في كليهما لا بد من وقوع النسبة بالفعل' confirms this.
Without actual occurrence, the proposition would not hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث: الْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْعَامَّةُ تَارَةً تُؤْخَذُ بِمَعْنَى ضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ بِشَرْطِ الْوَصْفِ الْعِنْوَانِيِّ، وَأُخْرَى بِمَعْنَى ضَرُورَتِهَا فِي جَمِيعِ أَوْقَاتِ الْوَصْفِ، الْأَوَّلُ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ لِلْوَصْفِ مَدْخَلٌ فِي الضَّرُورَةِ، فالضرورة فيها لمجموع الذات والوصف، بِخِلَافِ الثَّاني؛ فإن الضرورة فيها للذات، لكن في زمان الوصف، لكن في كليهما لا بد من وقوع النسبة بالفعل.
The third investigation: The general conditional (mashrūṭa ʿāmma) is sometimes taken to mean the necessity of the relation with the condition of the descriptive attribute, and another time to mean its necessity in all times of the attribute. In the first, the attribute must have an involvement in the necessity; so the necessity belongs to the combination of the substance and the attribute, unlike the second, for the necessity in it belongs to the substance, but in the time of the attribute. However, in both, the actual occurrence of the relation is necessary.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage defines the general conditional proposition (mashrūṭa ʿāmma) by distinguishing two senses. In the first sense, the necessity of the relation is conditional upon the descriptive attribute (al-waṣf al-ʿunwānī), meaning the attribute itself plays a role in the necessity—so the necessity attaches to the combination of the subject's substance and the attribute. In the second sense, the necessity belongs to the subject itself, but only during the time when the attribute holds. An example: 'Every writer is human as long as he is writing'—in the first sense, the necessity of being human is tied to the attribute of being a writer; in the second sense, the necessity is for the writer himself but only while he is writing. Both senses require that the relation actually occur during the relevant time. The commentary emphasizes the precision needed to distinguish these modal nuances.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
الوصف العنواني
الضرورة

Key Takeaway
The general conditional proposition has two senses: one where the necessity is conditional on the attribute, and another where the necessity is for the subject during the attribute's time.

Rebuttal of an Objection to the Definition of Perpetual Propositions
Author rebuts an objection concerning the definition of perpetual necessity and the meaning of absolute propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in a common objection to the definition of perpetual propositions and reconstruct the author's alternative interpretation of the absolute contradictory.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a general absolute proposition must be reinterpreted to avoid logical collapse.

In this passage, the author tackles a technical difficulty in modal logic: if the contradictory of a false general absolute proposition is a perpetual absolute proposition, then the contradictory relation seems to vanish. By analyzing a gloss and offering his own solution, the author deepens our understanding of the temporal scope of absolute propositions. This lesson will guide you through the logical moves and show how the author's reinterpretation preserves the integrity of the modal system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem Context
The author considers an objection: if we take a false general absolute proposition like 'The active intellect is not existent in actuality', its contradictory should be true. But if that contradictory is a perpetual absolute proposition with 'existence' as predicate, the two propositions seem not to contradict each other because the perpetual proposition's predicate is existence itself, which is always true of any existing thing.
The issue is that the contradictory of a general absolute (exists at some time) is a perpetual (exists at all times the subject exists), but if predicate is 'existence', the perpetual is trivially true, breaking the expected contradiction.
2
Understand the Gloss's Attempted Solution
A gloss tries to solve this by saying that the definition of a perpetual proposition requires the predicate to be something other than existence. The reasoning is that restricting a thing to its own time is meaningless (conventionally unintelligible). So if the predicate is existence, there is no real 'perpetuity' in the technical sense.
The gloss aims to exclude predicates that are identical to existence from the scope of perpetual propositions.
3
See Why the Gloss Fails
The author points out that the gloss's solution fails when the predicate, while not identical to existence, is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of existence. For example, 'existent' is not existence itself but necessarily accompanies existence. In such cases, the predicate is still not 'other than existence' in the required sense, so the gloss's condition is violated.
Thus, the gloss's condition is too narrow; it only works if the predicate is absolutely distinct from existence.
4
Grasp the Author's Own Solution
The author proposes to reinterpret the meaning of the absolute proposition that serves as the contradictory. He says that the absolute proposition should be understood as judging the actuality of the relation only at the time when the subject exists (not absolutely at all times). In other words, the contradictory of a perpetual is not an absolute proposition that holds at least once, but one that holds at the moment of existence. This makes the contradictory more specific and avoids the triviality.
The author redefines the absolute contradictory: it is not a general absolute (at some time), but rather an 'existential absolute' (at the time of existence).
5
Evaluate the Author's Conclusion
The author admits that this reinterpretation is not explicitly stated in the works of earlier logicians, but he asserts that it must be their intended meaning. He concludes with a pious phrase acknowledging that only God knows the true intentions.
This shows that the author is critically engaging with the tradition and proposing a refinement based on logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا قِيلَ: «الْعَقْلُ الْفَعَالُ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودِ بِالْفِعْلِ» مطلقة عامة كَاذِبٌ، فَيَلْزَمُ صِدْقُ نَقِيضِهِ، وَهُوَ دَائِمَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ مَحْمُولُهَا الْوُجُودُ، وَلَا يَكُونُ بَيْنَهُمَا تَنَاقُضٌ. قِيلَ فِي الحَوَاشِي: الْمُتَبَادَرُ مِنَ التَّعْرِيفِ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَحْمُولُ مُغَايِرًا لِلْوُجُودِ؛ فَإِنَّ تَقْيِيدَ الشَّيْءِ بِزَمَانِ نَفْسِهِ غَيْرُ مَعْقُولٍ عُرْفًا. فَلَيْسَ هُنَاكَ دَوَامٌ ذَاتِيٌّ اصْطِلَاحِيٌّ. وَهَذَا الْجَوَابُ لَا يَتَأَتَّى إِذَا نَقَضَ بِمَا فِيهِ الْمَحْمُولُ مِنْ لَوَازِمِ الْوُجُودِ. فَلَا جَوَابَ عَنِ الْإِشْكَالِ الْمَذْكُورِ إِلَّا بِالتَّصَرُّفِ فِي مَعْنَى الْمُطْلَقَةِ الَّتِي هُوَ نَقِيضُهَا؛ فَإِنَّهَا مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِفَعْلِيَّةِ النِّسْبَةِ حَالَ وُجُودِ الذَّاتِ. وَأَمَّا مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِفَعْلِيَّةِ النِّسْبَةِ مُطْلَقًا فَهُوَ أَعَمُّ وَنَقِيضُ الْأَزَلِيَّةِ. هَذَا وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مَنْصُوصًا فِي كَلَامِهِمْ لَكِنْ يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يَكُونَ مُرَادَهُمْ ذَلِكَ. وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ بِمَقَاصِدِ عِبَادِهِ.
And if it is said: 'The active intellect is not existent in actuality' as a general absolute proposition is false, then the truth of its contradictory follows, which is a perpetual absolute proposition whose predicate is existence, and there is no contradiction between them. It was said in the glosses: The obvious meaning of the definition is that the predicate is other than existence, because restricting a thing to its own time is conventionally unintelligible. So there is no terminological essential perpetuity there. And this answer does not hold if one objects with a proposition whose predicate is among the necessary concomitants of existence. So there is no answer to the mentioned difficulty except by reinterpreting the meaning of the absolute proposition which is its contradictory; for it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged at the time of the existence of the subject. As for that in which the actuality of the relation is judged absolutely, it is more general and the contradictory of the eternal. This, even if not explicitly stated in their words, ought to be their intended meaning. And God knows best the intentions of His servants.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a critical rebuttal of an objection concerning the definition of perpetual (daim) modal propositions. The author addresses a problem: if the contradictory of a false general absolute proposition is a perpetual absolute proposition whose predicate is existence, there appears to be no real contradiction. A gloss attempts to solve this by saying the predicate in the definition must be other than existence, but the author argues that this fails when the predicate is a necessary concomitant of existence. The only solution is to reinterpret the meaning of the absolute proposition that serves as the contradictory: it should be understood as judging the actuality of the relation only at the time of the subject's existence, not absolutely. This reinterpretation makes the contradictory more specific and avoids the difficulty. The author concludes that while not explicit, this must be the intended meaning.

Key Terms
دائمة مطلقة (dā'ima muṭlaqa): A perpetual absolute proposition: one that asserts the predicate holds for the subject at all times the subject exists, without restriction to a specific time. [Note: Standard terminology in modal logic; stable across the tradition.]
مطلقة عامة (muṭlaqa ʿāmma): A general absolute proposition: one that asserts the predicate holds for the subject at least once during its existence, without specifying when. [Note: Stable term.]
دوام ذاتي (dawām dhātī): Essential perpetuity: a technical term meaning the predicate is necessary for the subject as long as the subject exists, by virtue of its essence. [Note: Distinguished from accidental perpetuity; used in modal logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the gloss's solution fails when the predicate is a necessary concomitant of existence?
A) Because necessary concomitants are always true regardless of existence.
B) Because the predicate is not other than existence, so the definition's condition is violated.
C) Because the contradictory would then be false.
D) Because the gloss misinterpreted the term 'perpetual'.

Key Takeaway
When dealing with contradictions involving modal propositions, one must carefully specify the temporal scope of the judgment to avoid triviality or loss of contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a proposition in usul al-fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' as a perpetual proposition. If someone argues that nabidh (date-wine) is not forbidden, crafting the correct contradictory requires understanding the temporal scope. Using the author's reinterpretation, formulate the contradictory of 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and explain whether it is a valid contradiction.
?
Identify a real example from kalam where the predicate of a proposition is a necessary concomitant of existence (e.g., 'The world is temporally originated' where 'temporally originated' is a necessary concomitant of being a contingent thing). Apply the author's critique to show why a gloss requiring the predicate to be 'other than existence' would fail.

Two Meanings of the General Conditional Proposition
Author distinguishes two meanings of the general conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two interpretations of the general conditional proposition and identify which interpretation applies to a given example.

Core Idea
The general conditional has two distinct meanings: necessity of the combination versus necessity of the subject at the time of description.

This passage from the Bahr al-'Ulum introduces a key distinction in modal logic: the general conditional proposition can be understood in two ways. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting statements about necessary properties under conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
First interpretation: necessity of the relation under the condition of the attributive description. Second interpretation: necessity of the relation in all times of the description.
The attributive description is the condition (e.g., 'writer', 'mount of Zayd').
2
Understand the difference in necessity attachment
In the first interpretation, necessity attaches to the combination of subject and description (the whole 'subject-with-description'). In the second, necessity attaches to the subject itself, but only during the time the description holds.
The author uses 'الضرورة فيها للذات، لكن في زمان الوصف' for the second.
3
Note the requirement of actual occurrence
Both interpretations require that the relation actually occur (بالفعل).
4
Examine the partial overlap
They agree when the description is essential to the subject (e.g., 'rational' for humans). The first is more specific when the description is separable; the second is more specific when the description is non-essential.
The author gives 'common from one respect' (عموم من وجه) to describe this.
5
Apply to examples
Example 1: 'Every mount of Zayd is a horse' – the necessity (horse) applies to the combination of 'thing ridden by Zayd'; the description is separable. Example 2: 'Every writer is a mover of the fingers' – the necessity applies to the subject during the time of description (writing).
Identify which interpretation each example fits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَبْحَثُ الثَّالِثُ: الْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْعَامَّةُ تَارَةً تُؤْخَذُ بِمَعْنَى ضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ بِشَرْطِ الْوَصْفِ الْعِنْوَانِيِّ، وَأُخْرَى بِمَعْنَى ضَرُورَتِهَا فِي جَمِيعِ أَوْقَاتِ الْوَصْفِ. الْأَوَّلُ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ لِلْوَصْفِ مَدْخَلٌ فِي الضَّرُورَةِ، فَالضَّرُورَةُ فِيهَا لِمَجْمُوعِ الذَّاتِ وَالْوَصْفِ، بِخِلَافِ الثَّانِي؛ فَإِنَّ الضَّرُورَةَ فِيهَا لِلذَّاتِ، لَكِنْ فِي زَمَانِ الْوَصْفِ. لَكِنْ فِي كِلَيْهِمَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْ وُقُوعِ النِّسْبَةِ بِالْفِعْلِ. وَبَيْنَهُمَا عُمُومٌ مِنْ وَجْهٍ؛ لِتَصَادُقِهِمَا فِي ضَرُورَةٍ يَكُونُ الْوَصْفُ ذَاتِيًّا لِلذَّاتِ وَلَازِمًا وَاتِّفَاقِ الْأُولَى مِنَ الثَّانِيَةِ فِيمَا كَانَ الضَّرُورَةُ لِلذَّاتِ وَالْوَصْفِ مُفَارِقًا، نَحْوَ «كُلُّ مَرْكُوبِ زَيْدٍ فَرَسُ»، وَالثَّانِيَةِ مِنَ الْأُولَى فِيمَا لَمْ يَكُنِ الْوَصْفُ ضَرُورِيًّا، فَمَا بِالْمَشْرُوطِ بِهِ نَحْوَ «كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الْأَصَابِعِ».
The third investigation: The general conditional is sometimes taken in the sense of the necessity of the relation under the condition of the attributive description, and at other times in the sense of its necessity in all times of the description. The first requires that the description have an entry into the necessity, so the necessity in it is for the combination of the subject and the description, unlike the second; for the necessity in it is for the subject, but in the time of the description. However, in both, the occurrence of the relation in actuality is necessary. And between them is generality from one respect; because they agree in a necessity where the description is essential to the subject and necessary, and the first is more specific than the second when the necessity is for the subject and the description is separable, as in 'Every mount of Zayd is a horse', and the second is more specific than the first when the description is not necessary, as in 'Every writer is a mover of the fingers'.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two interpretations of the general conditional proposition (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma). The author states that this type of conditional can be understood in two ways: first, as necessity of the relation (i.e., predication) under the condition of the attributive description (al-waṣf al-ʿunwānī); second, as necessity of the relation in all times when the description holds. In the first interpretation, the necessity attaches to the combination of the subject and the description, meaning the description affects the necessity. In the second, the necessity attaches to the subject itself, but only during the time the description applies. Both require actual occurrence of the relation. The two interpretations have a partial overlap (ʿumūm min wajh): they agree when the description is essential and necessary to the subject; the first is more specific than the second when the description is separable (as in 'Every mount of Zayd is a horse': the description 'mount of Zayd' is separable, but the necessity of being a horse applies to the combination); the second is more specific than the first when the description is not necessary (as in 'Every writer is a mover of the fingers': the description 'writer' is not necessary for the subject, but the necessity of moving fingers applies to the subject at times of writing). The author provides these examples to illustrate the distinction.

Key Terms
مشروطة عامة
وصف عنواني
ضرورة النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To the description alone.
B) To the combination of subject and description.
C) To the subject, but only during the time of the description.
D) To the predicate absolutely.

Key Takeaway
The general conditional has two distinct meanings: the necessity may attach to the combination of subject and description or to the subject during the time of the description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'Every moving thing is contingent', determine which interpretation of the general conditional is being used and explain why based on the text.

Construct an original example of a general conditional that fits the first interpretation (necessity of the combination) and one that fits the second interpretation (necessity during the time of the description).

Is the General Possible a Real Proposition? A Dialectical Analysis
Author defends the general possible as a genuine proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the debate over the general possible proposition and evaluate the author's refutation of the claim that it is not an actual proposition.

Core Idea
A proposition does not require actual existence; possibility itself is a mode of judgment.

Some logicians, including the commentator of al-Mutāliʿ, argued that the general possible proposition (e.g., 'The phoenix exists in possibility') is not a real proposition because it lacks actual judgment or assent. The author of this passage critically examines that view, distinguishing its possible interpretations and refuting them. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to follow his dialectical reasoning and see how he defends the propositional status of the possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Find the opponents' position: the general possible is not an actual proposition. What is their core objection?
They say it lacks actual judgment (ḥukm) which they equate with affirmation (thubūt) or assent (taṣdīq).
2
Extract the opponents' reasoning
Why do they think judgment is missing? Because in a possible proposition, the predicate is affirmed only potentially, not actually. Therefore, it cannot be a qaḍiyya bi-l-fiʿl.
Some say judgment means taṣdīq (assent), so the possible does not involve assent.
3
Analyze the author's initial response
The author grants that if 'no assent' means no assent to actualization, that is true but irrelevant. But if it means no assent at all, that is clearly false.
He shows the argument is either trivial or false.
4
Examine the analogical argument
The author compares the possible proposition to the necessary proposition: the necessary also does not involve assent to the eternal relation, yet it remains a proposition. So the absence of a certain kind of assent does not disqualify a proposition.
This shows the opponents' reasoning would apply too broadly.
5
Evaluate the author's positive defense
The author asserts that possibility is a quality of the reporting relation (nisba ḥākiya), and the basis of all relations is affirmation (thubūt), even if only potential. He proves this by noting that such propositions are usable in demonstrations and have truth value.
Thus the general possible does contain a judgment, albeit a modal one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَبْحَثُ الرَّابِعُ: ذَهَبَ قَوْمٌ، وَمِنْهُمْ شَارِحُ الْمُطَالِعِ، إِلَى أَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَةَ الْعَامَّةَ لَيْسَتْ قَضِيَّةً بِالْفِعْلِ؛ لِعَدَمِ اشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى الْحُكْمِ الَّذِي هُوَ الثُّبُوتُ؛ فَإِنَّ الثُّبُوتَ إِنَّمَا يَكُونُ فِيهِ بِالْقُوَّةِ، فَلَيْسَتْ مُوَجَّهَةً؛ لِأَنَّهَا أَخَصُّ مِنَ الْقَضِيَّةِ. وَقِيلَ: الْمُرَادُ بِالْحُكْمِ التَّصْدِيقُ. فَالْحَاصِلُ أَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَةَ لَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهَا التَّصْدِيقُ فَلَا تَكُونُ قَضِيَّةً. وَفِيهِ نَظَرٌ؛ فَإِنَّهُ بَعْدَ تَسْلِيمِ عَدَمِ الْمُذْعِنِ قَضِيَّةً لَا يَتِمُّ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِنْ أَرَادَ أَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَةَ لَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهَا الْإِذْعَانُ أَصْلًا فَذَلِكَ ظَاهِرُ الْبُطْلَانِ، وَإِنْ أَرَادَ أَنَّهُ لَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِخُرُوجِهَا إِلَى الْفِعْلِ فَمُسَلَّمٌ، لَكِنْ لَا يَنْفَعُ. وَهَلْ هَذَا إِلَّا كَمَا يُقَالُ: لَيْسَ فِي الضَّرُورِيَّةِ إِذْعَانُ النِّسْبَةِ الْأَزَلِيَّةِ؟ فَهِيَ وَسَائِرُ الْقَضَايَا سَوَاءٌ فِي عَدَمِ تَعَلُّقِ الْإِذْعَانِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ الْمُتَكَيِّفَةِ بِجِهَةٍ أُخْرَى. وَذَلِكَ خَطَأٌ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ الْإِمْكَانَ كَيْفِيَّةُ النِّسْبَةِ الْحَاكِيَةِ، وَأَصْلُ النِّسْبَةِ الثُّبُوتُ مُطْلَقًا، وَلَوْ بِالْقُوَّةِ، كَيْفَ لَا، وَقَدْ يُقْصَدُ تَصْدِيقُهُ بِالْبُرْهَانِ، وَالْعَنْقَاءُ مَوْجُودٌ بِالْإِمْكَانِ مُفِيدٌ لِلسُّكُوتِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، وَمُحْتَمِلٌ لِلصِّدْقِ وَالْكَذِبِ. نَعَمْ، الْمُتَبَادَرُ مِنَ الثُّبُوتِ الثُّبُوتُ عَلَى نَهْجِ الْفِعْلِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ لَا يَضُرُّ.
The fourth investigation: Some people, among them the commentator of al-Mutāliʿ, held that the general possible is not an actual proposition because it does not contain the judgment which is affirmation; for affirmation in it is only potential, so it is not a modalized proposition because it is more specific than a proposition. And it was said: By judgment is meant assent. So the conclusion is that the possible does not involve assent, so it is not a proposition. But in this there is consideration; for after conceding that the non-assenting is a proposition, it is not complete; because if he means that the possible does not involve any assent at all, that is clearly false; and if he means that it does not involve assent to its actualization, that is granted, but it does not help. And is this not like saying: 'There is no assent in the necessary proposition to the eternal relation'? So it is like other propositions in lacking assent to the relation qualified by another mode. And that is wrong. Do you not see that possibility is a quality of the reporting relation, and the basis of the relation is affirmation absolutely, even if by potentiality? How could it not be, when one may aim to assent to it by demonstration? And 'The phoenix exists in possibility' is useful for silence necessarily, and it is liable to truth and falsehood. Yes, the obvious meaning of affirmation is affirmation in the manner of actuality, but that does not harm.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about whether the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-ʿāmma) counts as an actual proposition (qaḍiyya bi-l-fiʿl). The author reports the view of some scholars, including the commentator of al-Mutāliʿ, who argue that the possible proposition is not an actual proposition because it lacks actual judgment (ḥukm), which they equate with affirmation (thubūt). They say affirmation in a possible proposition is only potential, so the proposition is not a modalized proposition (muwajjaha) because it is too specific. Others say judgment means assent (taṣdīq), so the possible does not involve assent. The author then critically examines this: first, if they mean no assent at all, that is false; if they mean no assent to actualization, that is true but irrelevant. He analogizes to the necessary proposition, which also does not involve assent to the eternal relation, but is still a proposition. The author refutes the objection by asserting that possibility is a quality of the relation that reports, and the basis of all relations is affirmation, even if only potential. He gives an example: 'The phoenix exists in possibility' is useful for silence (i.e., it conveys meaning) and can be true or false. He concedes that the common understanding of affirmation is actuality, but that does not undermine the point.

حكم
تصديق
ثبوت
قضية بالفعل
موجهة
نسبة حاكية

Key Takeaway
The general possible proposition is a genuine proposition because it contains a modal judgment (the predicate is affirmed in possibility) and satisfies the conditions of meaning and truth-aptness.

Debate on Whether General Possibility is an Actual Proposition
Author critiques the view that mumkina 'amma is not an actual proposition due to lack of actual judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the position that general possibility propositions are not actual propositions and identify the logical flaws in that position.

Core Idea
A proposition's truth-value does not require actuality; possibility propositions are informative and can be true or false.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle but important debate: whether a proposition that asserts mere possibility (like 'Man is a writer by possibility') qualifies as an actual proposition (qadiyya bi'l-fi'l). Some scholars argued that because such propositions lack actual judgment (hukm) or assent (tasdiq), they are not genuine propositions. The author dissects their reasoning, revealing a crucial confusion about the nature of judgment and truth. By following his critique, you will sharpen your understanding of what makes a proposition a proposition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested claim
Find the statement that 'the general possibility is not an actual proposition' and note the reasons given: lack of actual judgment and lack of assent.
2
Understand the objectors' reasoning
The objectors equate judgment with affirmation (thubut) and argue that in a possibility proposition, affirmation is only potential, not actual. Therefore, they conclude it is not a proposition.
3
Examine the author's critique
The author interjects with 'Fihi nazar' (there is consideration). He points out that if the objectors mean no assent at all, that is false; if they mean no assent to actualization, that is conceded but irrelevant.
4
Analyze the analogy with necessary propositions
The author draws a parallel: necessary propositions also require a relation (nisba) that is eternal – we do not assent to the eternal relation per se, yet they are propositions. So possibility propositions are similar; the modality (imkan) is a quality of the relation, not a denial of the relation itself.
5
Recognize the conclusion
The author concludes that possibility propositions are indeed informative (mufid), can be true or false, and can be assented to via demonstration. The 'affirmation' that is understood as actuality is not the only kind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث (الرَّابِعُ : ذَهَبَ قَوْمٌ ، ومنهم شارح المطالع» (إِلَى أَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَةَ الْعَامَّةَ لَيْسَتْ قَضِيَّةً بِالْفِعْلِ ؛ لِعَدَمِ اشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى الْحُكم) الذي هو الثبوت؛ فإن الثبوت إنما يكون فيه بالقوة، (فَلَيْسَتْ مُوَجَهَةً ؛ لأنها أخص من القضية. وقيل : المراد بالحكم التصديق . فالحاصل أن الممكنة لا يتعلق بها التصديق فلا تكون قضية. وفيه : نظر ؛ فإنه بعد تسليم عدم غير المذعن قضية لا يتم ؛ لأنه إن أراد أن الممكنة لا يتعلق بها الإذعان أصلا فذلك ظاهر البطلان، وإن أراد أنه لا يتعلق بخروجها إلى الفعل فمسلم، لكن لا ينفع وهل هذا إلا كما يقال: ليس في الضرورية إذعان النسبة الأزلية، فهي وسائر القضايا سواء في عدم تعلق الإذعان بالنسبة المتكيفة بجهة أخرى. (وَذَلِكَ خَطَةٌ ، أَلاَ تَرَى أَنَّ الْإِمْكَانَ كَيْفِيَّةُ النِّسْبَةِ) الحاكية، (وَأَصْلُ النِّسْبَةِ التُّبُوتُ) مطلقا، ولو بالقوة، كيف لا ، وقد يقصد تصديقه بالبرهان، «والعنقاء موجود بالإمكان مفيد للسكوت بالضرورة، ومحتمل للصدق والكذب. نعم، المتبادر من الثبوت الثبوت على نهج الفعلية، وهو لا يضر.
The fourth investigation: A group, among them the commentator of al-Mutala', went to the opinion that the general possibility is not an actual proposition, because it does not include judgment, which is affirmation; for affirmation in it is only potential. Thus it is not modal, because it is more specific than the proposition. And it is said: what is meant by judgment is assent. So the conclusion is that the possible does not involve assent, so it is not a proposition. There is consideration in this: because after conceding that an indefinable [non-assertive] is not a proposition, it is not complete; because if he means that the possible does not involve assent at all, that is evidently false; and if he means that it does not involve assent to its actualization, that is conceded but does not help. Is this anything but like saying: in the necessary there is no assent to the eternal relation? So it is like all propositions in the absence of assent to the relation qualified by another modality. That is an error; do you not see that possibility is a quality of the relative predicate, and the basis of the relative predicate is affirmation absolutely, even if potentiality. How not? For it may be intended to be confirmed by demonstration. 'The phoenix exists by possibility' is necessarily informative and can be true or false. Yes, the immediate understanding of affirmation is affirmation in the manner of actuality, but that does not harm.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage addresses a debate about whether general possibility propositions are actual propositions. The author first presents the view of some, including the commentator of al-Mutala', who argue that the general possibility is not an actual proposition because it lacks actual judgment; instead, it only contains potential affirmation, and thus does not involve assent (tasdiq). The author then critiques this view. He argues that if by 'no assent' they mean no assent at all, that is false; if they mean no assent to its actualization, that is conceded but irrelevant. He draws an analogy: in necessary propositions, there is no assent to the eternal relation of the predicate to the subject in a timeless sense. The author asserts that possibility is a quality of the relative predicate, and the basis of any relative predicate is affirmation in an absolute sense, even if only potential. For instance, the statement 'the phoenix exists by possibility' is informative and can be true or false. The common understanding of 'affirmation' as actual existence is accepted but does not refute the point. The passage uses the example of the phoenix, a mythical creature, to illustrate a possibility proposition.

General possibility propositions are genuine propositions because they involve a relation (nisba) with potential affirmation, are informative, and can be true or false.
